This report is based on the findings of a pre-appraisal and an appraisal mission which visited Croatia in
November, 1994 and March 1995, respectively. The project was prepared by the FAO/CP. The Bank's
missions comprised: Severin Kodderitzsch, Task Manager (EC2AU); Garry Smith, Agriculture Specialist
(FAO/CP); John Pesek, Agriculture Research  Management; Geoffrey  Adams, Extension; Garry
Christensen, Agriculture Policy Analyst; John Wrigley, Farm Management Specialist; (consultants). The
Peer Reviewers are: Tjaart Schillhorn-van Veen (AGRTN) and John Cole (EC3AI). Naushad Khan
(EC2DR) contributed to the procurement and implementation sections of this report. Richard Huber
(EC2AU) was responsible for document processing. Margret Thalwitz is the acting Division Chief and
Christine Wallich is the acting Department Director.



REPUBLIC OF CROATIA
FARMER SUPPORT SERVICES PROJECT
Loan and Project Summary
Borrower:            Republic of Croatia
Executing Agencg:    Ministry of Agriculture and Forestry
Beneficiary:        Private farmers
Amount:              US$17.0 miilion equivalent
Terms:               17 years, including five years of grace, at the level repayment of principals
(LRP), at standard IBRD variable interest rate.
Objectives:         The project would support the transition of the agriculture sector by providing
technology-related support services that are relevant to the private farmers. The
aim is to improve the income of the private farming community and increase its
productivity and hence the competitiveness of the sector. The project would (i)
change the delivery of technical services to the private farming community and
(ii) restructure and strengthen institutions to meet the needs of a market
economy.
Description:        The proposed project consists of six components: (i) Extension, (ii) Research,
(iii) Animal Health, (iv) Pasture and Fodder Development, (v) Seed Industry
Development, and (vi) Policy Analysis Support.
The project would:
(i) modernize agriculture practices and farm management through private and
public agriculture extension involving farmers in the services;
(ii) improve the applied agriculture research management system ensuring
farmers' participation in the formulation of the research agenda;
(iii) strengthen the private and public veterinary services and, in particular, the
epidemiological services;
(iv) assist in the development of the country's grassland and pasture potential;
(v) provide the regulatory and institutional framework for the seed industry; and
(vi) improve the Ministry of Agriculture and Forestry policy analysis and
formulation capabilities; and strengthen the agriculture data base available to
policy decision-makers.



- ii -
Benefits:            Private farmers will be the principal beneficiaries, gaining advantage from
improved production technology and animal health services, a strengthened
regulatory framework for the seeds industry, and from government policies and
services more conducive to the development of a vigorous private agricultural
sector. The project would contribute to improving living standards in rural areas
in which small-scale agriculture is a major employer and source of income. The
project will also generate substantial public benefits: disease control and livestock
product sanitary inspection will reduce losses; and improved agricultural
production and policy would foster better resource utilization.
Risks:               The potential resistance at the operational level by the various Government
institutions and services involved in the project to give up traditional influence
and responsibilities following their reorganization is a risk associated with the
project. This would be addressed through a substantial training program for the
staff of these institutions with a focus on farm community participation and on
the rationale for the changes introduced under the project. Associated to this is
the risk of a slow establishment of an Agriculture Research Council and a
National Seed Authority. This is being addressed through considerable technical
assistance provided both during pre-project start-up using Japanese grant funds,
and during early project implementation to work out procedures and to assist
these nascent institutions. A second risk lies in the number and size of
procurement contracts to be implemented during the first project years; to
mitigate a procurement specialist has already been hired to compile the major
packages to be procured during the first project year. A third risk during
implementation stems from limited farmer capacity to form and support private
associations, and the potential limitations of extension and research staff to evolve
with the market economy. This risk will be counteracted by project activities
which will focus technology development on efficiency increases, improved farm
management practices and product diversification; the assurance of unequivocal
Government support to farmer associations and farmer guided extension and
research services; and a focus of project design on farm community participation.

REPUBLIC OF CROATIA
FARMER SUPPORT SERVICES PROJECT
CHAPTER I. BACKGROUND
A. INTRODUCTION
1.1    Croatia's agriculture economy was stagnant during the 1980's and declined sharply at
independence due to war and the associated loss of land and livestock resources. The origin of the
stagnation in the 1980's lies in the economic policies of the former Yugoslavia: an inequitable division
of resources between the private and the social sectors, inadequate fiscal and monetary policies,
over-capacities in agro-industry, and domestic market protection. Agrarian policy was not, however, as
rigidly collectivist in Croatia as in countries under central planning. For instance, Yugoslavia's
Constitution of 1974 allowed unlimited leasing of land, and by the end of the 1980s the limit on land
ownership had been raised to 30 ha; the Constitution of the Republic of Croatia places no limit on the
size of private landholding.
1.2   Private farming enjoyed government support in the former Yugoslavia only to the extent that it
benefited the social and cooperative agro-industrial subsector. Agricultural technology development was
focused on large scale farming and aimed at production maximization, paying insufficient attention to
economic sustainability. While some private farmers adapted this technology to meet their needs, most
learned and applied technology which is now widely recognized as being inappropriate in a market
economy. Although a body of technology exists and more can be gleaned from the practices of successful
private farmers, there remains the need to restructure and reorient the agriculture knowledge system. This
includes rebuilding its structure and management, giving it a market focus, making it responsive and
accountable to the private farming community and ensuring its future financial sustainability. Other
support services, such as veterinary services, and the regulatory framework for the seed industry require
considerable institutional reforms in order to overcome the legacy of the past and to increase participation
of the private farming community. Policy formulation capabilities need to be developed in order to ensure
more rational agriculture policies.
1.3   The Croatian Government is committed to the development of the private, family farming sector
within a market economy. To this end, Government has: removed limits on agriculture land ownership;
enacted legislation for the sale or lease of state-owned land and supported the development of institutions
to implement this policy; legislated the sale of former socially-owned enterprises; ceased the use of
subsidized credit through the National Bank of Croatia - which previously represented 80% of direct State
transfers to agriculture; reduced budget expenditures to the sector to some 3 % of government
expenditures; and reduced the use of quotas to control foreign trade. The commitment to the private,
family farm sector is reflected in Government's "Agricultural Policy Strategy" which was approved by
Parliament in March 1995. The Farmer Support Services Project (FSSP) would be fully compatible with
and support the implementation of Government's "Agriculture Policy Strategy".
B. BANK'S COUNTRY ASSISTANCE STRATEGY
1.4   Private sector development is the focal point of the Bank's assistance to Croatia, and is a key
element in the development of the agriculture sector. The proposed project would support the reform
agenda outlined in the Bank's Country Assistance Strategy, discussed on April 20, 1995, by fostering



private sector led growth in agriculture. The project would help Government establish essential public
and private services to support the development of a private, mostly family-based agriculture sector.
Other relevant issues, such privatization of former social sector farms and state-owned land, and trade
and price policies are being handled under a proposed Agriculture Sector Adjustment Loan operation,
currently under preparation. A proposed Rural Finance Pilot Project would support rural investments by
facilitating farmers' links with the national financial network. The project is, therefore, one important
element of a broader assistance strategy to the agriculture sector in Croatia, the combined effect of which
would lead the way to a market-based, largely privately owned and operated agriculture sector in
Croatia's reformed economy.
C. RATiONALE FOR WORLD BANK INVOLVEMENT
1.5    This relatively modest project offers the opportunity to assist government in redefining its role
in the agricultural sector, in developing policy analysis capacity, and in laying the foundation, through
a range of limited programs, for the development of a mix of private and public agricultural support
services which are accountable and responsive to the needs of the private fanner. With the break-up and
privatization of large social sector farms and agro-processing units, traditional sources of support services
for private farmers, such as provision of farm advice, extension services, and applied agriculture research
are disappearing. Other services such as veterinary services, seed regulations, agriculture policies and
statistics are in need of restructuring. The project would help fill this gap by supporting, through
contracting out to the private sector wherever feasible, more efficient extension services, agricultural
research and other services essential to the development of a modem, private agriculture sector.
1.6   Investment in support of private agriculture production is also shown to be indicated on economic,
social and technical grounds. Growth in agriculture is crucial to support economic growth, and well
within the realm of technical feasibility. Much of the known technology could be applied or easily adapted
to meet private farmer needs. The project would also contribute to improving living standards in rural
areas in which small-scale agriculture is a major employer and source of income.



-3 -
CHAPTER II. THE AGRICULTURE SECTOR
A. AGRICULTURE IN THE ECONOMY
2.1    Agriculture is an important sector of the Croatian economy. The share of primary agriculture
(including fisheries) in GDP increased from 10.0 percent in 1990 to 14.6 percent in 1992, mainly as a
result of the fall in industrial production. With food processing included, the sectors share amounts to
some 24 percent of GDP. The agriculture sector (including fisheries and food processing) employs some
15 percent of the active population. Some 41 percent of Croatia's population lives in rural areas.
2.2    Exports of agricultural and fisheries products have increased, both in absolute and relative terms.
Between 1991 and 1994 agriculture exports increased by some 44 percent from roughly US$340 million
to roughly US$490 million. As a share of the country's exports, it increased from some 9 percent to 12
percent during the same period. Imports during the same period fell from around US$560 million in 1991
to US$440 million in 1994, while the share of agriculture commodities and processed food in the
country's total imports remained stable around 11 percent. Hence, in 1994 the agricultural sector had a
modest trade surplus.
2.3    Levels of employment in agriculture have been more resilient than other sectors in the economy.
From 1990 to 1993, employment in agriculture fell by 25 percent, compared with a fall of 50 percent
for the rest of the economy. This is associated with the fall in GDP and the absence of employment in
other branches of the economy. Agriculture has, therefore, been able to provide a buffer during a difficult
economic and social adjustment period.
2.4    Agriculture is likely to play an increasingly supportive role in the development of tourism, in
terms of supplying the increased demand for agricultural products (especially for fruits and vegetables
in the vicinity of the Adriatic coast), and services in terms of specialized forms of tourism such as
farmhouse tourism or hunting and fishing.
B. NATURAL RESOURCE BASE
2.5    Croatia covers an area of about 56,750 km2 or 5,675,000 ha, of which 3,230,000 ha are
agriculture land and 2,030,000 ha is arable land. Croatia has 2,028 km of land boundaries with other
states. It is bounded to the west by the Adriatic Sea, to the northwest by Slovenia, to the northeast by
Hungary, to the east by the Federal Republic of Yugoslavia and to the south by Bosnia-Herzegovina.
There are three main physiographic regions in the country: the Adriatic region to the west (32 percent
of Croatian territory); the mountainous region in the center (12 percent); and the Pannonian region in the
east (56 percent).
2.6   The coastal area, its hinterland and islands have a Mediterranean climate with mild wet winters
and warm dry summers although some rain does fall in every month of the year. In the central mountain
region the climate is affected to some extent by the altitude but otherwise this region is ameliorated by
the Mediterranean climate of the coast and the continental type climate farther east. Eastern Croatia, or
the Pannonian region has a continental climate with cold winters and hot summers although here again
there is some rain in the sunmmer period, often falling in the form of thunderstorms.



-4 -
2.7    Croatia has some 1.2 million hectares of grassland of which 0.4 million ha are meadows' and
800,000 million ha are pastures. Grasslands thus occupy about 40 percent of all agricultural land but the
proportion of grass to arable varies greatly among regions and agroecological zones. Pasture yields are
very low and average less than 1.0 mt/ha. Low production can be attributed in part to the neglect of these
areas as a resource in favor of intensive systems of grain-based animal production by the former socialist
regime. Meadows and pasture land usually occupy poorer quality land.
C. THE SOCIAL SETTING
2.8    Croatia's population is estimated at 4.78 million. The 1990 household consumption survey
estimated a total of 1.35 million households, of which 96,724 were classified as purely farming
households and 404,355 were mixed households deriving part of their income from agriculture. Some 58
percent of the economically active farmers are 50 years of age or older.
2.9    Ex-social sector farms employ about 40,000 people, cultivates some 37 percent of all agriculture
land (1.2 million ha) of which 22 percent is arable land (430,000 ha), 20 percent of the total cattle and
30 percent of the national pig population. In most cases 50 percent of the assets of these agro-kombinats,
excluding land, has been transferred to employees and local civil servants, the remaining assets having
been transferred to the State. It is rare for privatization of agricultural firms to have proceeded further.
The State currently owns the lands of these farms and the land de-nationalization process, though
legislated, is stalled. The lands are used under temporary, informal rental arrangements, mostly by the
ex-social sector farms, often without payments being made to the State.
D. FARM STRucTuRE
2.10   Croatia's agriculture is marked by a dual farm structure, consisting of numerous small private
family farms on the one hand and some 500 large, vertically integrated agrokombinats (AKs) which have
dominated the sector during the socialist period. The average farm size of Croatia's roughly 500,000
private farms is 2.9 ha of arable land consisting of about eight usually non-contiguous plots. Moreover,
33% of these farms are under one hectare in area and only 2.6 percent are more than 10 ha, though,
more farmers operate enterprises in excess of 10 ha, usually through annual lease agreements.
2.11   Private farmers own about 63 percent of all agriculture land (2 million ha) and 78 percent (1.6
million ha) of arable land. The private fanrning structure essentially falls into two classes: some 400,000
part-time farmers and around 100,000 full-time farmers. First, the part-time farmers often operate on a
sub-subsistence unit of less than 3 ha and with a growing labor constraint due to age. These aged farmers
rent out land, but under the current land policy would not sell, as their land provides security. These
farms will gradually be absorbed by the progressive farmers who already form the production core of the
future. The rate of demise of these units will depend on what security and incentives Government policies
will provide to encourage land transfers.
2.12   Second, the professional full-time private farmers (some 100,000 households) who own from 3
to 10 ha and rent extra land on an annual basis. These farms are generally profitable and often have three
generations working. Despite the lack of technical and financial support, there is a confidence in the
future of private farming as a profitable venture and as an active export prospect. Mixed livestock/arable
1/  Meadows are natural or improved perennial pasture lands on cultivable soils, some of which have a history of fertilization.



-5-
farming predominates with farm sales mostly from grain, horticulture, and livestock products. Farms are
often over-mechanized but, with a rapid growth in contracting services, utilization of equipment is
improving. In the absence of land purchase possibilities and a mistrust of the banking system, tractors
are considered a safe placement of possible surplus funds. A recent survey based on progressive full-time
farmers showed that about 60 percent of full-time farmers own between 5 and 20 ha and 34% had
holdings of less than 5 ha, being mostly in the Mediterranean intensive cropping area.
2.13   The 500 agro-kombinats (AK), often vertically integrated enterprises that not only farm but also
own processing and marketing industries (as well as in addition some 86 agriculture cooperatives)
dominated the agriculture sector during the socialist regime. The AKs were the main source of food
production and received virtually all financial assistance to the agriculture sector to support maximization
of production with little consideration of costs involved. The AKs held the most productive land but,
through inappropriate methods of production, particularly cultivation practices, degradation of the soils
has taken place and yields have been declining. In a market economy, and with progressive withdrawal
of Government finance, the AK system has come under severe financial and operational pressures and
now presents a formidable problem as to what procedures should be instituted to return the land and other
assets to profitable farming systems. The AKs were also set up as a product procurement, storage and
processing bases.
E. AGRICULTURAL PRODUCTION
2.14   Land use is marked by the following features: the predominance of both agricultural land and,
particularly, arable land in the private sector; the extensive areas of meadows and pasture with ownership
of the latter dominated by the ex-social sector; private sector predominance in the viticulture and
horticulture industries; and, the relative importance of cereal and industrial crop (oilseed, sugar beet) as
opposed to fodder production for a predominantly continental climate.
2.15   Maize is by far the dominant crop in terms of area, followed by wheat which is the second most
important crop. Barley ranks a poor third among the cereals and there are smaller areas of rye and other
cereals. Potatoes predominate amongst the non-cereal crops, averaging 60,000-80,000 ha per year and
are followed in importance in terms of area by sugar beet, soybeans and sunflower. Orchards occupy
about 70,000 ha while vineyards cover almost 75,000 ha. Fodder crops are grown on about 172,000 ha
and there are just over 400,000 ha of meadows. Crop yields in the ex-social sector often surpassed those
of the private sector. Better access to credit and hence inputs favored social production, but the social
sector orientation of maximizing production rather than profit was an important contributory factor. This
is best demonstrated by comparing wheat yields, a crop sold mostly to the State, where ex-social sector
farms yielded as much as 70 percent higher than private farms. The latter's maize, however, typically
used for feeding livestock on-farm, out yields the former by more than 50 percent.
2.16   Livestock numbers decreased between 1961 and 1990. The population decrease has generally been
accompanied by a progressive improvement in productivity per head in most sectors. Predominantly
privately owned, 1989 data on herd and flock size in the private sector show about half of private farmers
owned cattle but only about 4 percent had more than five cows, 52 percent kept pigs with only roughly
5 percent having five or more sows, and eight percent kept sheep with about 3 percent owning more than
20 head. By far the largest numbers of cattle, pigs and sheep are kept on average size farms of about 3
ha, but an increasing number of more commercially viable livestock farming units are emerging in the
private sector.



-6-
F. SECTORAL DEVELOPMENT ISSUES
2.17   Due to the legacy of the past regime, the development of the agriculture sector is presently
hampered by major constraints which Government is currently striving to address. These include: (1)
changes in agriculture structures in terms of privatization of agro-kombinats and agro-processing
companies, and in terms of land policy and privatization of agriculture land; (2) rural finance; (3) price
and trade policy reform; and (4) farm support services.
2.18   Structural changes to be introduced in the sector include (i) the further privatization of the now
semi-public assets owned by the agro-kombinats, (ii) the privatization of land (some 37 percent of the
total agriculture land) of these ex-social sector farms which is presently Government owned and is
currently farmed under short-term lease arrangements, either by the agro-kombinats or by private
farmers; (iii) the privatization of the remaining publicly owned agro-processing entities; and (iv) the
development of a land policy which would include the modernization of the cadastre system. The majority
of land (some 63 percent) is, however, under private ownership allowing private farmers to produce and
to provide a supply response to the services introduced under the project; the issue of land privatization
relates to the 37 percent of the agriculture land under state ownership and agrokombinat management that
would be addressed through a possible agriculture adjustment operation.
2.19   Further changes in the sector would also lead to increased competition in commodity and factor
markets. Government is considering an Agriculture Sector Adjustment Loan (ASAL) with assistance of
the Bank in order to address the privatization question of agro-kombinats and agro-processors as well as
land related issues. Marketing, however, is not a major constraint to the success of the proposed project,
because (i) most farmers at present manage to dispose of their production, even though marketing
channels are not very sophisticated in Croatia, and (ii) agriculture inputs are generally available to
farmers by a fairly broad range of input suppliers. Nevertheless, the project would assist in introducing
market transparency and competition through a limited market information system monitoring sales at
selected central points. Given the sometimes disappointing track record in the public support to
marketing development it is not suggested to add such further activities to the agenda of the FSSP. In
order to increase marketing infrastructure in the country MAF is currently discussing with the European
Bank for Reconstruction and Development (EBRD) the possibility of financing wholesale markets in the
vicinity of Zagreb and other major towns in Croatia.
2.20 Rural Finance. Government perceives the lack of access to rural finance services as a major
obstacle to significant renewed investment in the sector, and is concerned that this problem could
ultimately constrain private farm productivity. MAF has requested the Bank to support a Line of Credit
(LoC) to be channeled through selected commercial banks on a pilot basis in order to test effective
demand and to overcome market failures associated with formal bank lending to the emerging private
farm sector. The Bank is considering such a LoC under a separate Rural Finance Pilot Project. In
general, however, farmers have reasonable scope to improve their operations, and introduce efficiency
gains (e.g. fertilizer and animal feeding regime, crop and farm management) without the need for
incremental external finance through farmers own internal cash generations and retained earnings as
observed during numerous farm visits and consistently shown by farm models. Further development,
however, should be supported through the provision of long-term financing of on-farm investments as
envisaged under a possible Rural Finance Pilot Project.
2.21   Trade and Price Regime. The overall incentive structure has improved markedly over the past
years and is likely to continue to evolve in the direction of border pricing indicating the true opportunity
costs of resources to producers. Nevertheless, some issues persist in the field of pricing policies (e.g.,



-7 -
some import quotas, and some floor prices). During the last years of the Yugoslav Federation the policy
environment was characterized by price policies with far-reaching intervention on both the production and
the consumption side, a trade regime based almost exclusively on import and export quotas, and a subsidy
policy pursuing cheap finance through broad interest rate subsidies. The highly distorted incentive
framework of the past was almost exclusively geared towards the support of social enterprises and agro-
kombinats.
2.22   In recent years Croatia's agriculture incentive environment has undergone important change
towards a more market-based incentive system. Consumer prices have been completely liberalized. Import
quotas on many products have been removed and replaced with tariffs. The application for membership
in the GATT / WTO, which has been recently submitted, will spur these changes. On the macro side,
rampant inflation has been brought to a halt through tight monetary and fiscal policies. Independence
and the war have led to a dramatic cut in the agricultural budget, from roughly DM360 million in 1990
to about DM140 million in 1994 which corresponds to a share of 2.6 percent of total government
expenditures compared to 15.0 percent in 1990. Substantial changes have also occurred in the allocation
of this budget. A huge interest rate subsidy was the dominant form of direct farm support during 1991 -
1993 (on average DM150 million annually or 80 percent of the budget). These interest rate subsidies
were discontinued at the end of 1993.
2.23   Croatia's agriculture policy has moved steadily towards the model of more liberalized economies
since the 1980s. Price support to most farm commodities is modest, and much lower than equivalent
levels of support in the EU, with the exception of sugar. Part of this support comes directly from modest
allocations of public funds, while most is provided implicitly through the trade regime. The range of
commodities supported has been considerably reduced over the years, and now includes only those
considered by Government to be strategically important, such as sugar, wheat, maize and milk. Prices
for commodities such as fruit, vegetables, meat and other dairy products are determined by market forces.
Some quotas, though significantly reduced over the past years, remain on sugar, wheat, seed and fertilizer
imports, in particular. Changes of trade and price policies would involve a revision of the operations of
the State Directorate for Commodity Reserves.
2.24   Table 1 below shows nominal protection coefficients for selected agricultural commodities.
Current price and trade policies result in low protection for oilseeds and pork, moderate protection for
cereals and milk and very high trade protection for sugarbeets. Beef, on the contrary, is not protected.
Moderate levels of support for farmers have been retained; most of the support directed to agriculture
accrues, however, to agricultural industries rather than farmers. Private farming historically has been
and continues to be associated with the commodities for which the least protection was granted and the
least price distortions introduced (i.e., horticulture, and animal production, in particular, meat, and to
a lesser extent, milk for which protection is moderate). The social farm sector has typically specialized
in wheat and maize grain production, and in sugar. While the level of protection associated with maize
grain has been moderately high, most maize produced by private farmers is not marketed but fed on-farm
to cattle and pigs (i.e., products without important price distortions). Price policies for wheat, which is
also grown by private farmers, is presently driven by strategic considerations, and likely to change to a
more rational policy, once hostilities cease in the region.

2.25   With the exception of sugar, the incentive framework is, therefore, sufficiently undistorted to
allow research and extension services to provide farming advice without leading to resource
misallocations. Financial and economic models of indicative farms, representing major agriculture
production areas and farm systems, further support this view (Annex E and M). It is anticipated that
under the planned ASAL further alignment of domestic input and commodity prices with border price
levels would be supported.
2.26   Support services to agriculture have, in the past, been geared to the agro-kombinats and farm
cooperatives. No support services have been directly available to the private farming community in terms
of extension, applied research, or animal breeding services; veterinary services have, in the past, been
oriented towards social sector agriculture. The seed industry has been left without a regulatory framework
which would enhance private sector participation in seed multiplication and export; agriculture statistics
and the policy formulating capacities of MAF have not been developed under the former Yugoslavia. The
thrust of the FSSP is on improving these services in the short term.
2.27   Agriculture technology is generally inefficient when judged, not only by European standards, but
also by those of elite farmers within Croatia. Efficiency gains in production are available in fertilizer use,
land preparation, crop sowing, crop rotations, and animal feeding, provided the research/extension system
is enabled to respond to farmers' perceived needs.
2.28   Combined, these partly historic constraints continue to curtail the development of commercially
viable family farms in Croatia. In the short term, private agriculture could be substantially supported and
the framework for major structural reforms established through a process of institutional restructuring
leading to increased client accountability and ownership.



-9 -
G. GOVERNMENT'S AGRIcuLTURE DEVELOPMENT STRATEGY
2.29   Through it's "Agriculture Policy Strategy" approved by Parliament in March 1995, Government
has made a clear commitment to promote the development of private farming within the framework of
a market economy. The objective of Government's agriculture policy is to promote efficiency in
production and marketing, and ensure the competitiveness of the agriculture sector on world markets.
The aim is also to overcome the legacy of large scale kombinats by giving emphasis to the development
of private family farms. This would be achieved by further restructuring of the current system of direct
support to agriculture production from the budget; replacement of import quotas with tariffs; and
reduction of tariffs under GATT's developing country status. Policy actions would also include the
introduction of structural changes in land and asset ownership by the full privatization of former social
sector farms and land, and of publicly owned agroprocessing enterprises; improving rural financial
services on a sustainable basis using both commercial banks and public institutions; tariffication of
remaining import quotas and strictly limiting the commodities for which limited support will be provided;
development of farm support services; and social policies including the reconstruction of war damaged
areas and restructuring of the agriculture pension fund.
2.30   The provision of relevant support services to the private farming community is a key instrument
of MAF's strategy. The need to establish a relevant agriculture knowledge system which would explicitly
allow for participation of the local farming communities, and combine private and public sector elements
in the provision of extension services is clearly recognized. Improving the regulatory framework of the
seed industry, as well as the veterinary services in terms of epidemiological analysis and monitoring, has
also been stressed by MAF as being an important public service. MAF also recognizes the potential of
the country's grassland resources which so far have been under utilized. Policy decision-makers are,
furthermore, aware of the need to improve the empirical basis and analytical methods in formulating
policies. These support services are recognized to have many characteristics of public goods, hence
justifying the public provision of such services.



- 10-
CHAPTER III. ISSUES IN AGRICULTURE SUPPORT SERVICES
A. AGRICULTURE EXTENSION AND APPLIED RESEARCH
3.1    Private farming enjoyed government support in the former Yugoslavia only to the extent that it
benefitted the social or cooperative agro-industrial subsectors. Agricultural technology development was
focused on large scale farming and production maximization, paying insufficient heed to profitability and
economic sustainability. While some private farmers adapted this technology to meet their needs, most
learned and applied technology which is now widely recognized as being inappropriate to a market
economy. Although a body of appropriate technology exists and more can be gleaned from the practices
of successful private farmers, there remains the need to restructure and reorient the agriculture knowledge
system, comprising agriculture extension and applied research. This includes redefining its present
mandate and structure, giving it a market focus, making it responsive and accountable to its clients and
financiers and ensuring its future financial sustainability. While public institutions and investment will
be required to initiate the process, the medium term objective will be the progressive transfer of
management and financial responsibility for agricultural knowledge services to the private agricultural
sector.
3.2   Improved technology would enable farmers to increase production efficiency, reduce costs,
increase yield, improve quality and diversify production. Both public and private benefit would ensue,
the latter already in evidence amongst a core group of elite farmers. Instruments to support technology
promotion are presently limited. The main sources of information available to farmers are a small but
competent rural press, ill-trained Zupanija-based2 advisors, some elements of the scientific community,
a recently privatized veterinary service and a small, under-resourced national extension service. These
poorly wedded extension elements generally lack market or farm management focus, are short on
appropriate technology and, in the public sector, are largely unaccountable to their farmer clients. A
stimulus is required which would engage the farmer in constraint analysis and technology development,
better employ the public and private extension resources to address identified needs, increase client
accountability, and ultimately, transfer responsibility for the delivery of technical advice to the private
sector. In a similar way, agriculture research lacks relevance and accountability to the farming
community.  All agriculture research is under the responsibility of the Ministry of Science and
Technology (MOST), and only modestly funded at a level equivalent to roughly 0.2 percent of agriculture
GDP. A detailed description of issues are provided in Annex H (Agriculture Extension) and Annex J
(Agriculture Research) of the Supplemental Volume. The project would strengthen the agriculture
knowledge system by developing public and private extension services and by restructuring applied
research management through a competitive grant based research system. Given the broadly public goods
nature of these services, no cost recovery is envisaged during project implementation.
B. ANIMAL PRODUCTION SUPPORT SERVICES
3.3    Livestock production in Croatia is mainly a private sector enterprise. Animal output provides 40
percent of agricultural GDP. Support services have historically been provided through the government
veterinary service, and public sector breeding enterprises which require reform in order to be relevant
in a private farming context. Clinical veterinary services have recently been privatized and some of the
public functions of disease surveillance and vaccination are now carried out under contract from MAF
by private veterinary stations; however, competition in the provision of veterinary services is still limited
through regional monopolies granted to the newly private veterinary stations. Insufficient epidemiological
2/ Zupanija is the Croatian word for district.



- 11 -
disease control and monitoring has resulted in import bans to key markets in the EU. A new Law on
Veterinary Services is currently being drafted. The draft version of the new law on veterinary services
has been selectively reviewed and assurances were received that (i) the new veterinary law, when
promulgated, will remove constraints to competition in the provision of animal breeding and veterinary
services, in particular, that regional monopolies for veterinary services would be abolished, and that the
prices for clinical veterinary services were subject to a negotiation process between veterinarians and
farmers or their respective representatives; and (ii) animal semen importation is unconstrained outside of
normal quarantine requirements.
3.4    Livestock production has been largely based on housed animals fed mainly concentrates, in
production systems focused on maximum per animal production rather than on optimum profitability
within a sustainable system context. This grain-based feeding largely ignores the comparative advantage
Croatia has through its largely under-utilized grassland and pasture resources (some 1.2 million ha). The
project would improve animal health services, assist in the development of the grassland potential, and
introduce private sector breeding-related activities on a pilot scale. (See also Annex J, Animal Health
Support Services, and Annex K, Pasture and Forage Development, Supplemental Volume).
C. SEED INDUSTRY
3.5    The Croatian seed industry has considerable potential but is constrained by an inadequate
regulatory framework, particularly the lack of Plant Variety Rights (PVR) legislation, and deficiencies
in the present seed legislation. With no statutory authority to regulate the industry and a lack of
transparency in the testing process (including failure to enforce regulations) international suppliers are
deterred from entering the market. Importation quotas are a further constraint. This results in absence
of competition in the provision of plant genetic materials to the farming community, and foregone seed
export possibilities. The project would support the development of the regulatory and institutional
framework within which a private sector seed industry could operate efficiently. (See also Annex L, Seed
Industry Development, Supplemental Volume).
D. MARKET INFORMATION SYSTEM AND AGRICULTURE STATISTICS
3.6    Prior to independence, the collection of agricultural statistics was oriented towards production on
ex-social sector farms. The consequent lack of information on private farms has become a serious
constraint to policy analysis, especially following the adoption of a new Agricultural Policy Strategy
which emphasizes the role of family farms. It is difficult to respond effectively to the needs of Croatia's
small-scale family farms when so little is known about the constraints they face and the implications of
policy changes for their well-being. Collection and access to timely and relevant agriculture market and
price information is also deficient. The project would support the establishment of a modest market
information system, and of regular on-farm monitoring surveys.
E. POLICY FORMULATION CAPABILITIES
3.7    Adoption of the Agricultural Policy Strategy has paved the way for widespread policy reform over
the next 2-3 years. The MAF's capacity for policy analysis and policy formation is limited however, both
in available staff and skills. Given the upcoming GATT / WTO membership negotiations and the possible
discussions on EU Association Agreements, price and trade policy formulation capabilities are recognized
as being of the highest priority. The project would strengthen MAF's policy formulation capabilities.
(See also Annex M, The Policy Analysis Unit: Background and Scope of Work, Supplemental Volume).



- 12 -
CHAPTER IV. THE PROJECT
A. PROJECT OBJECTIVES AND CONCEPT
4.1    The project would support the GOC's agricultural policy by efficiently providing technology-
related support services that are relevant to the private farmers. The aim is to improve the income of the
private farming community and increase its productivity and hence the competitiveness of the sector. The
project would address the need: (i) to change delivery mechanisms for the provision of technical services
to the private farming community and (ii) for institutional restructuring to meet the needs of a market
economy. It would allow public authorities to respond to the service needs of the private farming
community and to ensure that private agriculture and agribusiness irrevocably have a voice in the
formulation of agricultural services and support policies.
4.2    The activities pursued under the project aim to have a major impact on private farming in the
short term and to introduce significant institutional improvements in the medium term. Increased farmer
ownership and accountability of the agricultural knowledge system (i.e., extension and applied research)
are an essential element of project design. The provision of relevant and efficient veterinary services and
support for the shift from a grain-based animal production system to a lower cost pasture-based livestock
industry are other central objectives. Rationalizing the regulatory framework for the seed industry would
also be supported. The project would furthermore seek to strengthen MAF's policy formulation
capabilities and the agriculture data base in order to ensure more rational policy decisions in the future.
No support would be provided to the ex-social sector Agro-Kombinats under the project (with the
exception of the research divisions of the AKs who would be eligible to participate in the competitive
research grant system (see para 4.15)).
B. SUMMARY PROJECT DESCRIPTION
4.3    The proposed project consists of six components: (i) Extension, (ii) Research, (iii) Animal Health,
(iv) Pasture and Fodder Development, (v) Seed Industry Development, and (vi) Policy Analysis Support.
4.4    The project would:
(i)  modernize agriculture practices and farm management through extension and applied
research ensuring farmers' participation in these services. Extension would be supported
though the provision of public and private sector extension. The component would include
(a) a comprehensive training program and equipment to the existing county level extension
offices (which would be guided by farmer advisory councils) of MAF; (b) the formation
on a pilot scale of some 45 farmers associations who would hire private advisors on a cost
sharing basis with MAF; (c) the development of 25 extension training packages which
would be used for mass media based extension; (d) an on-farm trial and demonstration
program to be linked to the applied research supported under the project; and (e) the
introduction of farmer based milk recording on a pilot scale;
(ii)  improve applied agriculture research management through the establishment of an
Agriculture Research Council (ARC), with majority representation of the private farming
community. The ARC would define the applied agriculture research program and allocate
resources for research contracts through an Agriculture Research Fund (ARF) to be
established by the project (some US$5.2 million) on a competitive grant basis. The project
would establish four small zonal research-extension coordination units (ZRECU); and a
training program for researchers working on applied agriculture issues;



- 13 -
(iii) strengthen public and private veterinary services. The National Veterinary Institute (NVI)
and its four regional branches would receive vehicles and a comprehensive range of
analytical equipment to intensify the national disease surveillance, complemented by
improved border quarantine posts. NVI national and regional animal health data bases
would be linked to MAF's epidemiological and disease reporting division which would
receive computer equipment and technical assistance. The managerial skills of private and
public veterinarians would be improved through training;
(iv) assist in the development of the country's underutilized grassland and pasture potential
(which represents some 40 percent of agriculture land). Measures would include: a
systematic evaluation of the national grassland resources; improvement of the laboratory
and training facilities of a pasture research station; local and overseas training; laboratory
and field experimental equipment for pasture trials and demonstrations; and  the
establishment of a modest seed bank;
(v) provide the regulatory framework for the seed industry. MAF would establish a National
Seed Authority (NSA), with legislated responsibility to regulate the seed and agro-chemical
markets, in which several institutions would be joined under one coordinated framework.
The NSA would be supplied with laboratory and field equipment for seed variety trials and
certification, plant pathology, and chemical registration; and NSA staff would also receive
overseas training; and
(vi)  improve MAF's policy analysis and formulation capabilities. Overseas training, long term
TA, selected policy studies, and computer equipment would be financed; and the
agriculture data base available to policy decision-makers would be strengthened through a
farm baseline survey, a farm monitoring program and a simple market information system.
C. DETAILED FEATURES
I. Agriculture Extension (US$7.2 million 3; see Annex A and H)
4.5    Public Extension. Under the project the existing public Zupanija (district) extension offices (21
offices with a total staff of 110) would develop into community supervised Zupaniia Extension Service
Centers (ZESC). These would provide the impetus to farmer group formation (see below) and act as a
source of information and specialized guidance to private advisers directly serving the farmers.
Relevance of the public extension services would be ensured through voluntary local Advisory
Committees representing the local farming community and agro-business which would allow for a flexible
reorientation of extension services in line with changing local priorities. All farmers, whether full-time
or part-time operators, would have the possibility to join and influence these advisory councils. Local
Advisory Committees are likely to provide substantial guidance to the public extension services given (i)
the active development and involvement of the recently established Croatian Farmers Association at the
district level; and (ii) the positive response this approach was given by individuals during farm visits, and
during an Extension Workshop bringing together farmers, extension workers and the research community
during project preparation. The establishment of local Advisory Committees may initially be on an ad-hoc
basis requiring some extension support until formal procedures have been established by the individual
Committees.
31 Cost estimates including physical and price contingencies and duties and taxes (see also Annex B).



- 14 -
4.6   The project would develop the required skills, provide means of mass media communication, and
establish the necessary infrastructure for the 21 ZESCs and MAF's Extension Department to ensure
effective dissemination of relevant information to the private farming community. Through the project
the public extension services would be provided with 44 vehicles (two in each ZESC, two to MAF's
Extension Department), 21 sets of basic office infrastructure (including furniture, photocopier, and fax;
one set per ZESC), 67 computers (three in each ZESC and four at MAF's Extension Department), 23
sets of audio-visual equipment (one set per ZESC, two for MAF's Extension Department), and 21 kits
of extension field equipment (including a microscope, tape measures and scales, one kit per ZESC). Each
ZESC would also be provided with a basic reference library and annual subscriptions to farm production
journals.
4.7    Each ZESC would carry out a one-time, farm community based diagnostic survey in order to
develop a thorough understanding of the constraints and potential of the areas they are servicing, and to
give the extension services the opportunity to inform farmers in their area of the new extension approach.
The diagnostic survey exercise would help define the annual working program of the Zupanija extension
service.
4.8    The project would implement a substantial extension training program amounting to a total of
some 462 months, phased over project implementation, including: (i) a trainers of trainer program; (ii)
a one-time general orientation course for each extension staff; (iii) regular and (iv) specialized in-service
training. In addition, study tours and post-graduate fellowships would be carried out by selected extension
experts to get a better understanding of extension issues and operations in other countries. This training
program would enable staff of the extension service to acquire the skills necessary to operate under a
changed mandate. Subjects would include farm business management, financial and marketing skills,
group formation skills, environmental awareness, preparation of mass media and training packages, and
information management. The training of trainer program would be carried out prior to project start-up
financed through a Japanese Grant.
4.9   The project would provide for the development of some 25 thematic trainin,e packa,es for the
ZESCs and other advisers for incorporation within an extension program for farmers. The training
packages would also form the basis of mass media activities which would be developed on a contractual
basis involving the extension, research and broadcasting (radio and television) services. Topics would
include farm business management, improving marketing arrangements of primary producers, and
technical items such as crop and integrated pest management. The project would also develop a Farm
Management Handbook for farmers which would include, inter alia, agriculture price schedules, and farm
enterprise budgets across agriculture zones.
4.10   The project would also support an on-farm trial and demonstration program consisting of some
2,200 field demonstrations over the life of the project which would be carried out through the ZESCs in
coordination with agriculture researchers pursuing competitive grant-based applied research also supported
through the project (see agriculture research component, below).
4.11   Private Extension. The project would develop private advisorv services which currently are
practically non-existent in Croatia outside of the agrokombinats. Under the project, the formation of a
limited number of farmer associations (some 45 associations, roughly 2 associations in each Zupanija)
on a pilot basis would be supported in view of farmer associations employing private advisors in
agricultural production, farm business management; and marketing associations management on a
matching grant basis. Each association would directly employ its own adviser who would be funded by



- 15 -
MAF during the first four years on a cost sharing-basis 4 and receive some basic training equipment, also
on a grant basis. The public extension service would develop standard contracts and provide the newly
established fanner associations with legal advice during the process of hiring private advisors. Private
advisors working under the farmer association pilot scheme would also participate in the above extension
training program. In order to ensure (i) accountability and (ii) the interests of farmers, MAF has provided
the Bank with draft regulations, satisfactory to the Bank, for allocating grants and for supervising and
auditing the public funds under the farmer association pilot scheme. Some ten associations eligible for
support have so far been established in different zupanija, and it is anticipated that most of the
associations would be established during the first two years of project implementation. This pilot scheme
(as well as the pilot scheme described in para. 4.12) would be phased over the four years of project
implementation. Existing farm associations have expressed strong interest in a phased cost sharing
arrangements in view of hiring private advisors.
4.12   Also on a pilot basis, fanner herd recording groups (some 45 in three Zupanijas) would be
supported through the Croatian Livestock Selection Center (CLSC) in close collaboration with the ZESC
to promote farmer-based milk recording. Milk recording data, an important management tool in milk and
animal production would be used by the extension service and the farmer herd recording group. Farmer-
based milk recording would also be a first step in supporting private animal breeding efforts and in
reducing the high costs of recording animal performance presently carried out fully by the public sector.
Project support would include the establishment of small recording groups, the provision of milk
recording and testing equipment, limited number of computers for data processing at the Zupanija level,
and industry staff training including a study tour to selected European animal breeding programs.
4.13   Over the course of project implementation, the extension component would be supported through
international and national technical assistance amounting to 23 person months of assistance (extension
specialists, training specialists, herd recording advisors). The project would also support incremental
recurrent costs including operation and maintenance of ZESCs and MAF's Extension Department offices
and vehicles. No incremental public extension staff would be hired under the project.
4.14   In order to ensure sustainability of agriculture production and the safe use and handling of agro-
chemicals, MAF would develop environmental ('best practice') guidelines for the extension services in
line with applicable environmental legislation as agreed under a dated covenant to the Loan Agreement
no later than April 30, 1997 (see para. 7.1).
II. Agriculture Research (US$8.0 million; see Annex A and I)
4.15   The project would support an applied agriculture research management system to produce relevant
and timely information for the private family farmers through research contracts on the basis of
competitive research grants. Under the project an independent Agriculture Research Council (ARC) with
a majority of farmer representation, and a small secretariat would be established for defining policy, and
allocating resources under an Agriculture Research Fund for applied research contracts on a competitive
grant basis. In order to ensure that a clear understanding on the concept and principles for the applied
research component under the project has been reached, MAF has provided the Bank with a
Memorandum of Understanding, satisfactory to the Bank, signed by the respective Ministers of
Agriculture and Forestry, and Science and Technology, which outlines the responsibilities of the
respective Ministries and of the future ARC for the implementation of the Agriculture Research
component. The research agenda would be (i) open-ended and determined by the ARC, and (ii) would
4/Based on the following formula for private advisor's salary support: 80 percent in year 1, 50 percent in year 2, 35 percent in year
3, and 25 percent in year 4. No support would be provided thereafter. The balance would be provided by the farmer group.



- 16 -
allow for a broad scope of topics to be researched ranging from production technology to market research
and development. Any bona fide Croatian research institution, public or private, would be eligible to
participate under the competitive research grant system and make research proposals in the framework
of publicly announced Terms of Reference by the ARC in view of obtaining research contracts supported
under the project. The ARC secretariat, consisting of one high caliber professional staff and an
administrative assistant, would be provided with a set of office and computer equipment, and a vehicle.
The ARC secretariat would also be equipped with a computer network allowing it to link with MAF and
MOST and the academic networks in the country.
4.16   The ARC would administer an Agriculture Research Fund (ARF) established under the project
providing roughly US$1.3 million during each year of project implementation (i.e., a total of US$5.2
million over four years) to finance applied research contracts on a competitive grant basis. During
project implementation this fund would be financed to a large extent through the World Bank loan
(roughly estimated at around 80 percent) with the balance from budget allocations (roughly 20 percent).
Project funding would increase overall allocations to agriculture research to the equivalent of roughly 0.35
percent of agriculture GDP. In order to ensure that both regional and national research priorities are being
addressed a funding formula would be agreed upon by the ARC prior to project implementation, and
acceptable to the Bank, for a period not shorter than three years, which would allocate funds to the main
agriculture regions (based, for instance, on the relative contribution to agriculture GDP) and to overall
national research. In order to ensure financial sustainability of the Agriculture Research Fund, MAF is
committed to introducing in the course of project implementation modest commodity based levies to be
paid by the producer, once the overall concept has gained credibility within the farming community. This
credibility is largely a function of the relevance and quality of the applied research and the associated
independence of the ARC. The project would provide for technical assistance to review the funding of
the Agriculture Research Fund and make recommendations for the possible introduction of commodity
based levies after project mid-term review.
4.17   Under the project a substantial research management training program amounting to some 168
months of training for applied research staff and the ARC (and its secretariat) would be carried out. This
training program would include a visiting scientist program, research staff orientation courses, research
management training, research management study tours for ARC members, and research reporting
seminars. Training would be provided in particular for researchers who have obtained research contracts
from the ARC. Computer networking of the Agriculture Faculty, University of Zagreb, with international
agriculture data bases would be provided, as would be subscriptions to journals and books.
4.18   Four Zonal Research Extension Coordination Units (ZRECU) would be phased in during project
implementation as the ARC becomes operational and each ZRECU would be provided with office and
computer equipment; each ZRECU would have incremental staff consisting of one economist and one
research/extension specialist. The ZRECUs would (i) serve as backstopping service to the district
extension services by providing specialized advice, (ii) would create a formal link between research,
extension and the farming community and (iii) would be current on research results and provide guidance
to the ARC.
4.19   Prior to project start-up, technical assistance would be provided under a Japanese Grant fund to
develop operational procedures for the ARC and the ARF, as would the procedures for the scientific
screening and review of research proposals, contract administration, and supervision to be carried out by
MOST. Short-term technical assistance (12 months) would be provided over the life of the project
including research management consultancy and training, and expertise on specialized research topics.
4.20   The project would also support the incremental recurrent costs associated with the research
component including: honoraria and travel allowance of the ARC chairperson and members, salaries and



- 17 -
travel allowance of the secretariat and ZRECU staff; operation and maintenance costs for the secretariat's
and the ZRECU's office (including office rents) and vehicles. The institutional requirements to operate
this component are relatively modest, i.e., the ARC which meets some 4 - 5 times a year, a two person
secretariat, the support of the MOST (without incremental staff), and four small ZRECU. The latter
would be phased in during project implementation comprising two professional staff each.
4.21   In order to ensure financial sustainability of the Agriculture Research Fund, it has been agreed
under a dated covenant, that a review of the funding arrangements for the Agriculture Research Fund
(including the possibility to introduce modest commodity based levies) would take place not later than
April 30, 1999 (see para 7.1). Also under a dated covenant it has been agreed that the first of the four
ZRECU would be established by MAF not later than October 31, 1997; and that the three remaining
ZRECUs would be established at the latest by October 31, 1998 (see para 7.1). As a Condition of
Board Presentation, and in view of ensuring efficient implementation of this component through a
phased approach to establishing the new research management system, MAF has established the ARC,
including (a) the nomination of a chairperson and a core council (consisting of at least three farmer
representatives; a representative each of MAF and MOST; and one representative from the research
community); in addition, MAF has identified the secretary of the ARC to be appointed at the time of
declaration of effectiveness of the Loan Agreement (see para. 7.2). As a condition of disbursement for
the research component it has been agreed that MAF would (i) have worked-out jointly with MOST
and the ARC operational guidelines (including a funding formula) satisfactory to the Bank (this would
be supported through technical assistance fielded in September 1995 under a Japanese Grant Fund); and
(ii) MAF would have established the full ARC council (see para. 7.3).
III. Animal Health Support Services (US$6.1 million, see Annex A and J)
4.22   Animal health would be improved through the strengthening of epidemiological services leading
to better disease identification and eradication programs. The National Veterinary Institute (NVI) and its
four regional branches would be provided with eight vehicles, and a comprehensive range of laboratory
equipment to intensify the national disease surveillance program (US$2.9 million), a process which would
be complemented by strengthened border quarantine posts. NVI national and regional animal health data
bases would be linked to MAF's epidemiological service, and MAF's national disease reporting service
strengthened through the provision of computing equipment to support national disease eradication, and
international disease reporting requirements. The technical and managerial skills of private and public
veterinarian would be improved through local and overseas training (178 months). The National
Veterinary Chamber would be strengthened. International specialists would support the epidemiological
services, data management and veterinarian training programs (10 months). The project would support
the incremental recurrent cost of disease monitoring and control in terms of salary (two incremental staff
to MAF's epidemiological service), vehicle, and laboratory operations.
4.23   The new regulatory framework (see para. 3.3) will have a major impact on the effectiveness of
the investments for animal health described above and on veterinary services and disease control in
general. Therefore, as Condition of Board Presentation, MAF has carried out a review of the draft
veterinary legislation ('Veterinary Law') through the International Organization of Epidemiology (Paris),
and has presented the draft law to Parliament (see para. 7.2). MAF has assured the Bank that the revised
draft to Parliament includes the abolishment of regional monopolies for veterinary services, and that the
law foresees prices for clinical services to be subject to a free negotiations process between farmers and
veterinarians or their respective professional representatives.



- 18 -
IV. Pasture and Fodder Development (US$2.3 million, see Annex A and K)
4.24   The Grassland and Fodder Development component would initiate an evaluation of the national
grassland resources using recently completed national vegetation maps supported by field observations.
An international pasture/livestock economist would provide guidance to the pasture program during early
project implementation. Concurrently, laboratory and training facilities at the Slejme Pasture Research
Station of the University of Zagreb would be improved and expanded (detailed designs have already been
completed) and a comprehensive program of post-graduate and in-service training in pasture management
initiated (in particular for farmers and extension service staff). Laboratory and field experimental
equipment would be provided to support an adaptive natural and improved pasture trial and demonstration
program, supported by limited specialist technical assistance. Pasture trials and demonstrations would be
carried out in close collaboration with research institutions and extension services across the country.
Linkages to European pasture research networks would be improved. The program would also support
the University-based forage breeding program and establish a modest seed bank at the Agricultural
Faculty, Zagreb. These activities would support the shift from a grain-based to a pasture and fodder-based
animal feeding system. Once this component has been implemented under the project, grassland research
would compete for research funds from the ARC under the competitive grant research system.
V. Seed Industry Development (US$3.8 million; see Annex A and L)
4.25   Government is committed to bringing the seed industry into line with European norms. To this
effect, new seed legislation is drafted, and an application made to join the International Union for the
Protection of New Plant Varieties (UPOV). Investment in the completion of the regulatory framework
and establishment of a statutory seed industry regulatory body would accelerate this process, ensuring
better seeds for Croatian farmers and improved export opportunities for seeds. The project would provide
the intermational expertise and staff training required to establish and implement a seed industry regulatory
framework in line with European Union requirements. A National Seed Authority (NSA) would be
established with legislated responsibility to regulate the seed and agro-chemical markets and which would
amalgamate several institutions under one coordinated framework. The NSA would be responsible for
seed testing and certification, agro-chemical registration, and development of Integrated Pest
Management. Inspection of commercial seed multiplication would be carried out by private sector
inspectors under license from NSA. The NSA would incorporate the existing seed testing and border
quarantine services of MAF; the Plant Protection Institute, the Viticulture Institute and the services to be
established for seed testing of plant material under the project.
4.26   Project support to the NSA would include: laboratory and field equipment for variety trials, plant
pathology, seed and chemical testing; eight vehicles for field work and equipment transportation; in-
service training (50 months) for private seed certification inspectors; specialized overseas training in seed
testing and variety trials; 18 months of technical assistance; and incremental staff (about 25 professional
staff phased over the course of project implementation of which 11 would be at NSA headquarters and
7 at each of the two field trials sub-centers), office rental, and vehicle and equipment operating expenses.
4.27   The new seed legislation I will have a major impact on the future of the seed sector and the
impact of the NSA supported under the project. In order to ensure a meaningful use of the investments
supported under the project, it has been agreed that Croatia would (i) have enacted the new seed
legislation (the draft of which would have been reviewed by the Intermational Union for the Protection
of New Varieties of Plants (Geneva), and the Intermational Seed Testing Association (Zurich),
respectively, and comments included in the revised draft law presented to Parliament); (ii) MAF would
5/Consisting of the Law on Plant Variety Protection, and the Law on Plant Variety Approval.



- 19 -
establish the National Seed Authority (NSA), (iii) appoint a director satisfactory to the Bank; and (iv)
obtain suitable locations for setting up core NSA infrastructure as a condition of disbursement for the
seed industry component (see para. 7.3). In order to ensure farmers' access to good plant genetic
material and competition in the subsector, it has been agreed that at the latest under a dated covenant,
at the latest by October 31, 1997 Government would convert all existing import quotas to tariffs (see para
7.1). In view of ensuring the financial viability of the NSA, preparation of a schedule on cost recovery
for the NSA by no later than six months after establishment of the NSA has been agreed upon under a
dated covenant (see para. 7.1).
VI. Policy Analysis Support (US$2.3 million, see Annex A and M)
4.28   The project would strengthen the policy formulation capacity of MAF's restructured Agriculture
Policy Analysis Unit (APAU) through: (i) staff overseas training (15 months); (ii) a long-term resident
and short-term international TA (24 months); (iii) funds to contract studies on pressing policy issues (a
total of 5 major studies; topics include: rural land markets; marketing development, sugar, oilseeds,
dairy, and fertilizer subsector studies) and (iv) computing and some office equipment and a vehicle. In
addition, the project would strengthen the empirical basis required for decision makers by establishing
a simple market information system and by carrying out under contract with the Institute of Agriculture
Economics, University of Zagreb, a baseline survey, and a farm monitoring survey program. The project
would also support recurrent costs associated with this component. These costs include: salaries for four
incremental professionals and support staff at the APAU, and ten part time market enumerators; travel
allowance and car mileage; and incremental operating costs of APAU including publishing of policy
reports, and market news communication.
4.29   The market information system would record prices and marketed quantities for key commodities
and inputs at some ten central locations throughout the country on a daily basis. The data would be
transmitted to MAF's APAU by fax/modem, analyzed and forwarded to a national radio station for
broadcasting on the same day.  A baseline survey would be carried out early during project
implementation by the Institute of Agriculture Economics, University of Zagreb (which would be
provided with software and computing equipment) to provide up-to-date information on factors such as
demographic characteristics, the farm resource base, cropping patterns and livestock holdings, levels of
production and marketed surplus, the level and composition of farm income and expenditure, and the
contribution of non-farm income to total household income. It would also include behavioral and
attitudinal variables. A second, smaller and on-going survey would then be initiated as the basis for an
annual monitoring of the status and the changes on family farms. This second survey will be of a size
(in terms of both numbers and data collected) which allows rapid data collection, processing and analysis.
4.30   Staff development is key to the success of this component. Therefore, under a dated covenant,
MAF would staff the Agriculture Policy Analysis Unit with four additional policy analysts at the latest
by October 31, 1996 (see para. 7.1). In view to assist the Agriculture Policy Analysis Unit during
implementation, the APAU would exchange views annually on its work program with the Bank (see
para. 7.1).
Project Implementation Unit (US$0.3 million)
4.31   A Project Implementation Unit (PIU) has been established within MAF under the Department
of International Relations. The PIU would be responsible for overall project implementation and
coordination. This Department has already acquired substantial experience in procurement under the
agriculture component of the Bank-financed Emergency Reconstruction Project. MAF has assigned the
Deputy Ministers, International Relations, and Agriculture as the project manager and the deputy project
manager, respectively. MAF has also hired an implementation officer for the PIU who would be



- 20 -
responsible for the day-to-day management of the PIU on a full-time basis. The latter has received basic
training in World Bank procurement procedures. In addition, MAF would assign a full time procurement
staff, a financial officer, and an administrative assistant to the PIU. The PIU would receive two vehicles,
and office and computing equipment, and be supported by two months of technical assistance to carry
out the project mid-term review.
4.32  In order to ensure expedient project start-up, as a condition of Board Presentation, MAF has
appointed the procurement staff, and has identified a financial officer and an administrative assistant who
would be appointed on a full-time basis to the PIU upon Loan effectiveness (see para. 7.2). PIU staff
members would be provided training in procurement and disbursements through Bank-organized seminars
and workshops prior to project start-up using a Japanese Grant fund. In light of MAF's current
procurement experience and the appointment of procurement staff, MAF is judged to be capable of
undertaking procurement under this project.
D. PROJECT COST AND FINANCING
4.33   Project Costs. The total project costs including contingencies are estimated at US$30.0 million
of which the foreign exchange component is estimated at US$17.0 million (56.6 percent). Project cost
summary is presented in Table 2 below. Detailed project costs are given in Annex B. Taxes and duties
amount to about 16 percent of total project costs (US$4.7 million). Incremental recurrent costs represent
some 25 percent of total project costs.
4.34   Project Financing. The World Bank would provide a loan amounting to US$17.0 million (i.e.,
the equivalent of the foreign exchange component). The World Bank loan would finance all direct and
indirect foreign exchange associated with project costs net of taxes and duties. The balance (including
taxes and duties) would be financed by GOC. Table 3 shows the sources of finance by component.
4.35  Incremental recurrent cost. All incremental recurrent costs associated with the project would be
funded from Government resources. Annual incremental recurrent costs after project implementation are
estimated to amount to approximately US$2.3 million equivalent.
4.36   Fiscal Impact. The fiscal impact on Government budget is estimated to amount to approximately
US$13.0 million equivalent over the project implementation period. The Ministry of Finance has
repeatedly stated that this project is considered to be of high priority and, as implementation would
commence in full only after the beginning of 1996, the budget would assure the necessary counterpart
funding in CY1996 and onwards. The Bank has been informed that MAF has submitted a budget proposal
for CY96 which requests an adequate level of counterpart funding. Table 4 below gives a breakdown of
the estimated annual fiscal contribution to the project over the calendar years of the implementation
period.
4.37  Japanese Grant. A Japanese Grant Agreement to fund project start-up activities in the amount of
US$535,000 has been signed by the Ministry of Finance on March 20, 1995.
