4.43   Disbursements against contracts for goods and services exceeding US$300,000 equivalent would
be made against normal documentation. For contracts below that level, disbursements would be made on
the basis of Statements of Expenditure (SOEs) and would be kept available for examination by Bank
missions, with appropriate documentation retained by the PIU.
4.44   Special Account: To facilitate timely project implementation, the borrower would establish,
maintain and operate a Special Account, under condition acceptable to the Bank. During the early stage
of the project, the initial allocation of the Special Account would be limited to US$1.0 million. However,
when the aggregate disbursements under the Loan have reached the level of US$5.0 million, the initial
allocation may be increased up to the authorized allocation of US$1.5 million by submitting the relevant
Application for Withdrawal. Replenishment applications should be submitted at least every three months,
and must include reconciled bank statements as well as other appropriate supporting documents. These
authorized allocations have been finalized with the Borrower during Negotiations (see para. 7.1).
4.45   A Government Budget Account (GBA) for the project would be established as an identifiable
project line item under the regular budget of MAF which would be used (a) in full payment of all
recurrent costs associated with the project; (b) in payment of all taxes and duties associated with the
project; and (c) to replenish the Project Bank Account (see below). In addition, a Project Bank Account
(PBA) denominated in Kuna would be used in payment of local contracts (i.e. civil works, agriculture
research contracts, local consulting services and studies, training) financed jointly under the Bank's Loan
and from the Government budget in order to avoid separate payment of suppliers from two distinct
accounts. The PBA would be replenished on a quarterly basis (a) from the SA upon request of MAF
(PIU) and authorized by MOF; and (b) from the Government budget account for the project.
4.46  In order to ensure efficient project implementation, two Conditions of Disbursement for individual
components have been agreed upon for the following components: (1) Agriculture Research: (i) MAF
would have worked-out jointly with MOST and ARC operational guidelines (including a funding formula)
satisfactory to the Bank; and (ii) MAF would have established the full ARC Council (see para. 7.3); and
(2) Seed Industry Development: Croatia would have enacted the new seed legislation, and MAF would
have (a) established the National Seed Authority (NSA), (b) appointed a director satisfactory to the Bank
and (c) obtained suitable locations for setting-up core NSA infrastructure (see para. 7.3).

CHAPTER V. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
5.1    The project would be implemented over a period of four years (mid-CY1996 to mid-CY2000)
after a period of intensive pre-project activities in CY95 and CY96 financed under a Japanese Grant.
Prior to project effectiveness major packages for procurement during the first year of implementation
would be compiled. In order to ensure that the PIU staff and others involved in project implementation
understand the implementation arrangements including procurement and disbursement requirements, the
Bank has organized a one-week project start-up seminar during January 1996. Given the legal reforms
introduced and the support of the envisaged TA under the projects, the institutions involved are judged
to capable of implementing the project components.
A. PROJECT COORDINATION ANI) MANAGEMENT
5.2    Overall Project Implementation. MAF  would take overall responsibility  for project
implementation. Detailed implementation arrangements prepared jointly with the Borrower are given in
Annex A of the PIP.
5.3    Extension. The Deputy Minister, Extension, would take responsibility for implementing the
agriculture extension program.
5.4    Research. The Deputy Minister, Agriculture, together with the project manager would coordinate
the establishment of the Agricultural Research Council (ARC) and provide guidance to the ARC during
project implementation. The ARC, responsible for the allocation of the national applied agriculture
research budget under the ARF through research contracts under a competitive selection system, would
be independent of the MAF in its operational and budgetary decisions but answerable to the Minister of
Agriculture and Forestry. MOST will be responsible for the general administration of applied research
contracts using procedures acceptable to the Bank; this would include inter alia screening research
proposals, and off-site and on-site supervision of research work.
5.5    Animal Health. The animal health program would be managed by the MAF's Veterinary
Department while diagnostic wok would be under the responsibility of the NVI.
5.6   Pasture and Fodder Development. The grassland program would be managed and implemented
by the Department of Grassland and Fodder Development, Agricultural Faculty, Zagreb University.
5.7   Seed Industry Development. MAF's Agriculture Department would establish the National Seed
Authority (NSA) which would subsequently administer the national seed industry quality control and
regulatory policy, as well as agro-chemical registration.
5.8   Policy Analysis Support. MAF's Policy Department would be responsible for restructuring its
Agriculture Policy Analysis Unit, hiring the required additional staff, contracting selected policy studies,
and for establishing the market information system. The one-time farm baseline survey and the annual
farm monitoring exercises would be carried out by the Institute of Agriculture Economics, University of
Zagreb.
5.9   Technical Assistance. The TA program envisaged for the project (some 75 months amounting
to some US$1.8 million) is essential for project success. In order to ensure timely recruitment of key
TA, it has been agreed that the extension specialists, the research management specialist, the veterinary
epidemiologist, the pasture/livestock economist, the seed certification specialist, and the long-term policy
specialist would be hired at the latest by October 31, 1996 under a dated covenant (see para. 7.1).



- 26 -
B. AccouNTs, AuDrrING AND REPORTING
5.10   Accounts. Separate and auditable project accounts would be established with the PIU in MAF and
project expenditures (irrespective of sources of funding) would be kept separate and identifiable. These
would comprise inter alia (i) a record of withdrawals on the Bank Loan with copies of all disbursement
requests and underlying documentation; (ii) a record of transactions on the Special Account and copies
of the bank statements on this Account; and (iii) project related expenditures incurred directly by MAF.
These records would be monitored on an up-to-date basis, and quarterly statements of transactions and
balances, together with updated procurement contract information, would be forwarded to the Bank.
5.11   Audits. Project accounts would be audited annually by independent auditors and under terns of
reference acceptable to the Bank. Sperate audited financial statements would be prepared for the SA, and
for the project accounts (including Statement of Expenditures). Separate financial records would be kept
and audited annually for the Agriculture Research Council, and the National Seed Authority, respectively.
All audit reports would be made available to the Bank at the latest four months after the end of the fiscal
year.
5.12   Reporting. MAF will prepare and furnish to the Bank, by December 1 of each year during the
disbursement period, forward looking technical and financial projections in a format acceptable to the
Bank, and review with the Bank the conclusions and recommendations of this projection, including the
proposed forthcoming annual budget for the Project and any measures that need to be taken during the
following year to ensure proper execution of the project. Assurances on these auditing and reporting
requirements have been obtained during negotiations (see para. 7.1 ).
5.13   A semestrial report on project progress and linked to the quarterly unaudited statements would
be submitted to the World Bank by the PIU and a comprehensive annual report would be compiled which
would include the results of monitoring activities based upon inter alia on the Project Performance
Indicators (see Annex D). A project completion report would be prepared by the PIU and MAF upon the
completion of the project.
C. MONITORING
5.14   Monitoring of the project's impact on the private farming community would be carried out
through a baseline survey in the first year of the project and through annual farm monitoring exercises
under taken by the Institute of Agriculture Economics (see para. 4.29). In addition, performance
indicators would be monitored by the PIU for each component. Project Performance Indicators jointly
developed with MAF are presented in Annex D of the PIP, and have been agreed upon during
negotiations (see para. 7.1).
D. BANK SUPERVISION
5.15   The project would be supervised by the Bank on average twice a year, however, one additional
start-up mission would be necessary, including a start-up workshop; the cost in that year is expected to
be 24 staff weeks (involving expertise in the field of extension, research management, seed testing and
certification) falling to 18 staff weeks in each of the subsequent years. A Mid-term Review would be
carried out at the latest by April 30, 1998 (see para. 7.1).



- 27 -
CHAPTER VI. PROJECT IMPACT
A. BENEFITS
6.1    Private farmers will be the principal beneficiaries, deriving advantage from inproved production
technology and animal health services, and a strengthened regulatory framework for the seeds industry
and from government policies and services more conducive to the development of a vigorous private
agricultural sector. The project would contribute to improving living standards in rural areas in which
small-scale agriculture is a major employer and source of income. The project would also contribute to
increased productivity and production, as well as to an improved domestic food supply. Adaptive and
applied research would initially be focused on areas yielding quick returns, as part of a confidence
building process for the farming community and the extension and research services. Improved production
efficiency through better soil and crop management and crop rotations, and improved agro-chemical use
in the cropping sector; and improved returns from dairy and sheep production through better nutrition
and health care are likely early outcomes. Private farm advisors and public extension staff, trained in
financial and farm management principals, would assist farmers in making more rational investment
decisions. The investment will also generate substantial public benefits: disease control and livestock
product sanitary inspection will improve, and improved agricultural production and policy is likely to lead
to better resource utilization.
6.2   Improved Support Services. Farmers would have access to advisory services in which they have
ownership - the cost partly shared with Government - that are better able to respond, both quantitatively
and qualitatively, to needs and opportunities. Public extension and research staff would be more
accountable to farmers and adequately resourced, both materially and conceptually, to meet the demands
of the emerging commercial family farms. Staff motivation is also expected to improve.
6.3   Institutional Reforms. The process of resource transfer in the agricultural knowledge system from
wholly public to a partnership of farmers and agri-business will be initiated leading to a longer-term
possibility for public and private sector cooperation. The capacity to deliver timely, accurate market and
sectoral information will be improved.
Increased Farm Incomes (see Annex E)
6.4    Quantifying the incremental impact of the broad range of project activities which to a large extent
are of an institutional development nature is notoriously difficult, given the numerous non-service factors
influencing decision-making at the farm level. General research indicates that economic returns to
investments in agriculture research and extesnion as well as in other services can be very high. As an
approximation, indicative financial and economic farm models have been prepared to assess the likely
impact of project activities at the farm level. Farm models across the main agro-ecological zones of the
country and representing major production systems are presented in Annex E (and N of the Supplemental
Volume). The models are indicative and it is believed that they reflect changes which are likely to occur
on farms which are exposed to more focused extension services and have access to improved management
advice, seed, livestock and other inputs.
6.5    The crop and livestock budgets which form the basis of each farm/household model were
prepared in consultation with local agronomists and economists and checked by field discussion with
farmers, where possible. Generally they postulate crop yield increases of about 15 percent with project
and include higher fertilizer, better seed inputs and improved livestock rations. Farm size has been held
constant between without and with project situations, although there are changes in cropping patterns with
project. The investments to expand farm size, buy additional machinery or livestock or to install



- 28 -
irrigation, are excluded from these models as currently medium to short term credit is not available to
farmers. Model farm size is typically in the range of 5 to 7 ha (except for the intensive Dalmatia
Vegetable model). This is a little larger than average private farm size in these regions but there are many
such farms and it is considered that they are likely to respond first to improved support services. Labor
budgets show substantial annual surpluses of family labor in all models, without and with project.
Seasonal peaks in labor demand require use of hired labor in the intensive vegetable models and most
models do require incremental family labor with project. Household consumption of farm produce has
been included in each model and meets staple grain, milk, egg and meat needs in most cases (with the
exception of the high value vegetable model). Farm families will have to draw upon the cash surpluses
shown in each model to cover other food and living expenses. Overhead costs are also included in each
model to cover business expenses not readily allocated to individual crop or livestock enterprises. Such
costs are mostly in the range US$1,450 to US$1,800 per farm and cover electricity, other fuel, land tax
and insurance, tractor and equipment services and labor, not related to individual crop activities.
6.6    As very few private farms in Croatia have access to short or long term credit, the financing of
production inputs in these models follows reality as in both without and with project situations 100
percent of operating costs are financed by retained farm earnings from the previous year. Incremental
production costs typically lie in the range of US$715 to US$2,200 per farm, or a little under half of the
expected gross value of incremental revenue. This is within the capacity of most model farms to finance,
either from without project retained earnings, from off-farm income, or by moving to the with project
situation in steps phased over two or three years. Each model attempts to meet it's livestock feed needs
from on-farm forage and crop production, in line with usual practice in Croatia. The nature of much of
the new technology proposed is such (better seeds, additional fertilizer) that it is likely to be accepted
promptly when the benefits are demonstrated clearly.
6.7    Financial analysis of the five models (see Table 7) indicates that: (i) project activities are likely
to yield sufficient incremental financial benefit to farmers to encourage them to participate in or actively
use better extension, animal health services, seeds and market information (on average returns of 45
percent, ranging from 8 to 92 percent over the without project situation; (ii) in most of the models
farmers clearly have the ability to fund incremental short term inputs from internal cash generation, e.g.:
monthly milk payments or pig sales. In the Istrian model it may be necessary for farmers to fund the
added inputs from off-farm work in the tourist industry or by phasing changes suggested in the model
over several years; (iii) switching values (see Annex E and N) indicate that the models are robust,
requiring an increase of more than 100 percent in operating costs or a fall of more than 50 per cent in
incremental inflows to reduce NPV to zero at 10 percent discount rate; (iv) financial benefits may be
earned through changes in cropping pattern or livestock processing/marketing as well as increased
technical efficiency; (v) there is a substantial surplus of farm labor available on the modelled farms, and
labor returns per hour are maintained or increased under the with-project scenario for each model; (vi)
farmers have an ability to pay for useful services, especially where the scale of operation is large enough
to cover overheads and basic living costs or where high value vegetable, cheese or similar products are
involved.

a/ These models attempt to quantify the possible impact of improved extensions services and access to quality research and other technical services
supported under the project. Farm size has been held constant between without and with project situations, although there are changes in cropping
pattems with project.
b/ The with project cropping pattem expands higher return potato and luceme production and deletes wheat and maize production so as to avoid large
increments in labor or land use. Improved technology is applied to barley, potato and luceme production in the with project situation. Istria
traditionally provides hay and forage seeds to adjacent mountain areas and has adequate markets for increased potato production. Although livestock
activities comprise almost half of the gross value of production no incremental livestock income is assumed, as it focuses on higher value crops.
Family labor requirements increase from 1220 hours per year to 1410 hours with project, an increment of 190 hours. This still leaves a large surplus
which can be used in part to gain employment in the tourist industry.
c/ Dalmatia has a successful and expanding vegetable industry in part due to a warmer climate which enables early harvest and access to high price,
out-of-season markets in other parts of Croatia and nearby countries. No irrigation investment is included in this model. This model has a small farm
area of 1.2 ha but has by far the highest net value of production of any model due to the focus on early tomato and potato crops and other vegetables.
There are no changes of cropping pattem assumed for the with project situation but crop yields are increased by 10 per cent mainly in response to
higher fertilizer and manure inputs. Livestock are produced for home consumption and there are no increments with project. Incremental family labor
with project (to handle larger harvests) is 191 hour per year. Although there is substantial unused family labor, hired labor is needed to meet seasonal
peaks of demand and this also increases with project, by 33 hours per year.
d/ Lika is a mountainous area with much pasture land and some emphasis on livestock production. This model looks at changes in a mixed farm with
crop, cattle and pig production. The model increases potato and reduces wheat production with project in response to crop budget financial
profitability; additionally dairy cow numbers are increased from 3 to 4 and cheese making (600 kg per year) is introduced with project. There is a
strong demand for local cheeses and the necessary skills and interest in increasing production are available. Potato and barley yields are increased
by about 15 per cent with project in response to use of better seed and more fertilizer. The cropped area is constant at 5.8 ha in both project situations
and is below total farm area of 7.9 ha to allow for rotation and non-arable land. With project labor is only about half estimated available labor and
most of the increment is in high value cheese production yielding incremental hourly retums nearly double average without project retums.
e/ Slavonija is the major crop production region in Croatia. This model is representative of private farms which are replacing the Agro-Kombinats as
major producers in the region. The cropping pattem changes with project to reduce sugar beet and maize area and to expand soybean, so as to
improve farm self-sufficiency in pig feed. Wheat, meadow and barley are constant, as is the total cropped area of 5.3 ha. Almost half of the crops
produced (by value) are used on-farm for livestock production. The model includes I cow, 6 sows and a small poultry activity (no increment with
project). Of these, the pig activity is the main commercial livestock activity; with project weaner pig production increases by about 13 per cent and
ration costs are reduced. Crop yield increases of just under 15 per cent are achieved with better fertilizer inputs and seeds. The yield increases do
not demand additional labor as harvest is mechanized and the changes in cropping pattem do not create new labor demands..
f/ This model has four Simnental cows in both project situations. Milk production increases from 2800 1 to 3360 1 per cow per year with project.
Improved animal health, changed rations and better genetic material are expected to be accessible through improved extension and veterinary services.
The cropped area remains constant at 4.3 ha but the pattem changes with project. Maize silage and mixed grass pasture are introduced to boost feed
supply with project and maize for grain and the less productive meadow are reduced slightly. Mixed grass and other improved pastures would be
stimulated by project activities and gradually displace meadow and other unimproved pastures. Further increases in technical and financial performance
are possible, e.g. use of milk replacer for calf rearing or breed changes, and practical but have not been considered as they are still uncommon in
Croatia. Family labor requirements are virtually the same with and without project and are around 40 per cent of estimated available labor. There
is scope to expand the size of farms of this type but this is not considered within this project.



- 30 -
6.8    Farm economic analysis indicate positive incremental returns earned in each model, given the
NPCs estimated in Annex N of the Supplemental Volume. Even in Slavonija where several common crops
have higher NPCs, the usual practice on private farms is to feed these crops to livestock with strong
positive economic and financial results (see Table 8).

Project Econonic Rate of Return
6.9    A conservative estimate of incremental net economic benefits yields an economic rate of return
(ERR) of 28 percent. Project incremental benefits are based only on the economic farm returns as
identified under the indicative models described above; other potential benefits (such as export losses
avoided through improved animal disease control or increased seed export revenues through an improved
seed regulatory framework) have not been quantified. It is assumed that incremental benefits would occur
with only 5 percent of the farming community (i.e., 25,000 out of 500,00 farmers) and that benefits
would set-in only in the sixth year of a ten year time horizon (albeit benefits are expected to accrue well
beyond the tenth year and a considerably larger share of the farming community can be expected to
benefit from the project). Incremental project investment (including replacement) as well as recurrent
costs have been included net of taxes and duties (i.e., net of transfer payments). A fuller discussion of
the analysis is presented in Annex E.
6.10   Sensitivity analysis carried out on the base case ERR indicates that returns on this project are
robust. For instance, a simultaneous increase of project costs and decrease of benefits by 20 percent,
respectively results in an ERR of 15 percent (22 percent and 20 percent if costs and benefits, respectively,
are varied in isolation).
B. RISKS
6.11   The potential resistance at the operational level by the various Government institutions and
services involved in the project to give up traditional influence and responsibilities following their
reorganization is a risk associated with the project. This would be addressed through a substantial training
program for the staff of these institutions with a focus on farm community participation and on the
rationale for the changes introduced under the project. Associated to this is the risk of a slow
establishment of an Agriculture Research Council and a National Seed Authority. This is being addressed
through considerable technical assistance provided both during pre-project start-up using Japanese grant



- 31 -
funds, and during early project implementation to work out procedures and to assist these nascent
institutions. A second risk lies in the number and size of procurement contracts to be implemented during
the first project years; to mitigate a procurement specialist has been hired prior to Loan Effectivness to
compile the major packages to be procured during the first project year. A third risk during
implementation stems from limited farmer capacity to form and support private associations, and the
potential limitations of extension and research staff to evolve with the market economy. This risk will be
counteracted by project activities which will focus technology development on efficiency increases,
improved farm management practices and product diversification; the assurance of unequivocal
Government support to farmer associations and farmer guided extension and research services; and a
focus of project design on farm community participation.
C. ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT
6.12   The project has been placed in the environmental Category B. An Environmental Analysis (EA)
under terms of reference acceptable to the Bank has been carried out by an independent commission
appointed by MAF. The EA (Annex F) concludes: (i) no significant negative environmental impacts
associated with project implementation are to be expected; (ii) while private agriculture production is
likely to intensify, albeit from a low base, the intensity of the social sector production is likely to
decrease; and (iii) by the nature of the various project activities, in particular, extension and applied
research, agricultural practices would become environmentally safer. The EA also presents an inventory
of major environmental legislation relevant for the agriculture sector and concludes that while a whole
body of relevant environmental legislation has developed, enforcement has scope for improvement. The
EA underlines that project implementation would act as a catalyst to the further development of
environmental legislation and improved enforcement. Registration of agrochemicals and development of
Integrated Pest Management would be strengthened under the NSA (see para. 4.25).
6.13   No special mitigation plan has been required by the EA as no negative environmental impact is
directly caused by the project.; emphasis has been put on the extension services to provide guidance to
private farmers on the appropriate use of agro-chemicals. To support the concept of sustainable
agriculture and appropriate handling of agrochemicals, agreement will be sought during negotiations with
the Borrower, that MAF would develop environmental ('best practice') guidelines for its extension
service. These guidelines would give orientation to farmers on the safe and proper use of agro-chemicals
at the latest by April 30, 1997 as a dated covenant to the Loan agreement (see para. 7.1).

CHAPTER VII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
7.1    The following agreements have been reached during negotiations:
(a)    Recruitment of key consultants (i.e., the extension specialists, the research management
specialist, the veterinary epidemiologist, the pasture/livestock economist, the seed
certification specialist, and the long term policy specialist) would have taken place at the
latest by October 31, 1996 (see para. 5.9);
(b)    Review of financial arrangements of the Agriculture Research Fund (including the
possibility to introduce modest commodity-based levies) would be carried not later then
April 30, 1999 (see para 4.21);
(c)    The first of the four ZRECU would be established by MAF not later then October 31,
1997, and the three remaining ZRECUs would be established at the latest by October 31,
1998 (see para. 4.21);
(d)    Monitoring of project impact would be carried out on the basis of Project Performance
Indicators outlined in Annex D of the PIP (see para. 5.14);
(e)    Agriculture Policy Analysis Unit: MAF would staff the APAU with four additional policy
analysts at the latest by October 31, 1996; and the Agriculture Policy Analysis Unit
would exchange views with the Bank annually on its work program (see para. 4.30);
(f)    Conversion of all remaining import quotas to tariffs for seed trade would take place at
the latest by October 31, 1997 (see para. 4.27);
(g)    Preparation of a schedule on cost recovery for the NSA would take place at the latest six
months after establishment of the NSA (see para. 4.27);
(h)    MAF would develop environmental guidelines for its extension service on the use of
agro-chemicals in order to orient private farmers on the safe and sustainable use of such
agents, at the latest by April 30, 1997 (see paras. 4.14 and 6.13);
(i)    Project Mid-term Review: a project mid-term review would be carried out jointly by
MAF and the Bank at the by April 30, 1998 (see para. 5.15)
(j)    Authorized allocations under the Special Account (see para. 4.44); project accounts (see
paras. 4.45 and 5.10); and reporting requirements (see paras. 5.12) have been agreed.
7.2    Government has carried out the following activities in fulfillment of agreed Conditions of Board
Presentation:
(a)    Agriculture Research Component: MAF has established the ARC, including the
nomination of the chairperson and a core council (consisting of at least three farmer
representative; a representative of MAF and MOST, respectively; and one representative
from the research community); in addition, MAF has identified the secretary of the ARC
who will be appointed upon Loan effectiveness (see para. 4.21);



- 33 -
(b)    Animal Health Legislation: MAF has carried out a review of draft veterinary legislation
through an international institution acceptable to the Bank and has presented the draft law
to Parliament (see para. 4.23);
(c)   Implementation Preparation: MAF has appointed a procurement staff to the PIU, and has
identified a suitable financial officer, and an administrative assistant which will be
appointed to the PIU on a full time basis upon Loan effectiveness (see para. 4.32).
7.3   The following Conditions of Disbursement for two specific subcomponents have been agreed
upon:
(a)    Agriculture Research: (i) MAF would have worked-out jointly with MOST and ARC
operational guidelines (including a funding formula) satisfactory to the Bank; and (ii)
MAF would have established the full ARC Council (see para. 4.21 and 4.46);
(b)    Seed Industry Development: Government would have enacted the new seed legislation
and MAF would have (i) established the National Seed Authority (NSA), (ii) appointed
a director satisfactory to the Bank and (iii) obtained suitable locations for setting-up core
NSA infrastructure (see para 4.27 and 4.46).
7.4    No Conditions of Effectiveness other than the standard ones apply.
7.5    With the above agreements and conditions, the proposed Project would be suitable for a Loan of
US$17.0 million at standard IBRD interest rate.

REPUBLIC OF CROATIA
FARMERS SUPPORT SERVICES PROJECT
ANNEX A
PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION ARRANGEMENTS
I. IMPLEMENTATION SUMMARY
1.    The project would be implemented over a period of four years (CY96-99) after a period of
intensive pre-project activities in CY95 financed under a Japanese Grant. Prior to project effectiveness
major packages for procurement during the first year of implementation would be compiled.
2.    Overall Project Implementation. The Ministry of Agriculture and Forestry MAF would take
overall responsibility for project implementation, and direct responsibility for the implementation of the
agricultural extension and applied research components, for the policy analysis support component, and
for the veterinary epidemiological activities.
3.    Extension. The Deputy Minister Extension would take responsibility for implementing the
agriculture extension program. The extension service, which has its own budget and permanent staff,
would operate 21 Zupanija Extension Service Centers under the supervision of MAF. The broad functions
of the extension staff at headquarters would consist of: developing the diagnostic survey methodology;
supporting the national research prioritization process; facilitating research extension linkages; organizing
skill-gap based extension staff training; ensuring technical and market information flows; and developing
the mass media communication programs.
4.    The ZESCs would be staffed with 4 to 5 extension specialists in each of the 21 Zupanijas (no
incremental staff required). They would work through and support the creation of farmer groups, private
consultants and agro-industry and conduct on-farm demonstrations and mass media communications. The
ZESCs would be guided by local Advisory Committees with a broad representation of private farmers
in the Zupanija. Initially such farmer advisory committees would be formed with the assistance of the
ZESCs on an ad-hoc basis (in cooperation with the local chapters of the Croatian Farmers Association).
More formal procedures would be developed through the Advisory Committees once established.
5.    Some 45 local farmers associations would be supported through the project. Local farmers
associations interested in employing private advisors would be eligible for project grant funds through
MAF on a matching basis to pay for the salary of the advisor. Public support to fund a private farm
advisor salary would be based on the following formula: 80 percent of gross salary in year 1, 50 percent in year 2,
35 percent in year 3, and 25 percent in year 4. No support would be provided thereafter. The balance would be provided by the
farmer group. Associations would recruit suitably qualified advisors under a standard 4 year contract with
a 6 month probation period. Association and consultant compliance with the contract conditions would
be closely monitored by the ZESCs. Individual private consultants would be eligible to only one support
package over the project life. The roughly 45 groups under the farmer-based milk recording scheme
would be established in three selected Zupanija by the herd recording staff of the Croatian Livestock
Selection Center (CLSC).



ANNEX A                                         - 38 -                       Project Implementation Plan
6.    Training for extension staff would commence with a trainers program for staff selected from
within the Zupanija extension service. The trainers would subsequently implement a retraining program
for extension staff at all levels including both headquarters and Zupanija, organized by the training
coordinator appointed to the headquarters of the Extension Department. Each three-four week training
program would be for about ten staff. This training would include practical field exercise. About 110 staff
would be trained in this manner during the first two years of project implementation. Additional courses
would be provided for scientific staff involved in adaptive research and for private extension advisors
hired under the farmer group scheme. Regular and special in-service training programs for extension staff
at all levels would also be introduced, organized by MAF's training coordinator. Overseas training would
be provided for four extension managers. They would participate in short-term training programs in
extension methodology with practical exposure to field operations in selected European countries. On
return, the trainees would be required to hold a series of seminars for extension staff and be required to
contribute to in-service staff training courses.
7.    Research. The Deputy Minister Agriculture together with the project manager would coordinate
the establishment of the Agricultural Research Council (ARC) and provide guidance to the ARC during
project implementation. The ARC, responsible for the allocation of the national applied agriculture
research budget, would be independent of MAF but answerable to the Minister of Agriculture and
Forestry. MOST would be responsible for the general administration of applied research contracts; this
would include screening research proposals, procurement of research equipment associated with the
research contracts, and off-site and on-site supervision of research work. The establishment of the ARC
as well as the formulation of the operating procedures would take place prior to project effectiveness with
technical assistance funded under the Japanese Grant.
8.    The project would support an applied agriculture research management system to produce relevant
and timely information for the private family farmers. The establishment of an independent Agriculture
Research Council (ARC) which would establish research policy and priorities for funding applied
agriculture research would be key to ensuring relevance and timeliness of research to farmers. The ARC
would consist of a majority of farmer representatives (9 members nominated from each of the nine
agriculture regions); in addition, ARC would consist of one representative from MAF and MOST,
respectively, three representatives from the research community, and one from agrobusiness. The
Minister of Agriculture and Forestry would nominate the chairperson of the ARC.
9.    Demand-driven research. The ARC would invite the scientific community (both public and private
sector) on the basis of publicly announced Terms of Reference, to submit research proposals to the
council, i.e. contracts for applied research would be demand driven. The research agenda would be (i)
open-ended and determined by the ARC, and (ii) would allow for a broad scope of topics to be researched
ranging from production technology to market research and development. Any bona fide Croatian
research institution, public or private, would be eligible to participate under the competitive research
grant system. The ARC shall make such rules and establish procedures as required openly to carry out
its responsibility. The Council shall also promulgate procedures and select participants for external and
internal training of scientific and faculty personnel supported under the project.. The Ministry of Science
and Technology (MOST) would carry out the technical and scientific review (involving national and
foreign reviewers) of the submitted proposals and would submit to the ARC short lists of proposals in
the respective areas of applied research for decision making. MOST would administer, supervise
(including on-site supervision) and audit the research contracts. The ARC would be assisted by a small
(one or two) person secretariat which would call the ARC meetings on the initiative of the Chairperson.
It would also be responsible to liaise with MOST and to also carry-out on-site supervision of applied
research contracts.



Project Implementation Plan                    - 39 -                                       ANNEX A
10.   Agriculture Research Fund. Applied agriculture research would be funded on a competitive grant
basis through an Agriculture Research Fund which would finance incremental equipment and research
costs. In order to ensure that both regional and national research priorities are being addressed, a funding
formula would be agreed upon by the ARC, for a period not shorter than three years, which would
allocated funds to the main agriculture regions (based on the relative contribution to agriculture GDP)
and to overall national research. In the context of applied research, MOST would continue, as in the past,
to finance the salaries and basic operating costs of the public research institutions; MOST would also
continue to support basic agriculture research (including salaries, buildings, utilities and equipment) and
continue to support and pay for post graduate students.
11.   Research - Extension - Farmer linkages would be strengthened through the following steps:
(i) relevance of future applied research would be improved by the above approach, which focuses
on farmer representation both at the district and the national level.
(ii) invitations for applied research proposals would request a concept by the bidder on how the
research results would reach the extension services and farmers; this concept would be part of
the overall proposal evaluation;
(iii) field trials and demonstrations to be carried out by the extension services and by contracted
researchers under the competitive grant system would be linked to the research proposals in
order to ensure on-farm experimentation. The successful research bidder would engage in
contracts with individual farmers to carry out on-farm trials under the supervision of the
researcher.
12.   Animal Heath. The animal health program would be managed by MAF's Veterinary Department
while diagnostic work would be under the responsibility of the NVI.
13.   Pasture and Fodder Development. The grassland program would be managed and implemented
by the Department of Grassland and Fodder Development, Agricultural Faculty, Zagreb University.
14.   Seed Industry Development. MAF's Agriculture Department would establish the National Seed
Authority (NSA) which would subsequently administer the national seed industry quality control and
regulatory policy, as well as agro-chemical registration.
15.   Policy Analysis Support. MAF's Policy Department would be responsible for restructuring its
Agriculture Policy Analysis Unit, hiring the required additional staff, contracting selected policy studies,
and for establishing the market information system. The one-time farm baseline survey and the annual
farm monitoring exercises would be carried out by the Institute of Agriculture Economics, University of
Zagreb.
16.   Technical Assistance. The TA program envisaged for the project (some 75 months amounting to
some US$1.8 million) is essential for project success. In order to ensure timely recruitment of key TA,
hiring the extension specialists, the research management specialist, and the long term policy specialist
would take place at the latest by June 30, 1996 (i.e. six months after effectiveness).
17.   The following Implementation Arrangement by Components gives a breakdown of major
activities, responsibilities and summary description of tasks on a component basis.

ANNEX A                                        - 46 -                        Project Implementation Plan
m. COMPONENT DESCRIPTION FOR AGRICULTURE EXTENSION
A. Rationale and Design of the Project
18.   The four-year project must lay the foundation for the effective delivery of farner services and
develop a momentum for the continuing development of new technology and farm management practices
responsive to the needs of the private farmer.
19.   With the development of land holding legislation and economic liberalization in general, farming
in Croatia will rapidly change. These developments will press farmers to increase farm size, through
acquisition or lease, and to change their production mix, as well as their production systems in response
to price changes and new market opportunities. Among the factors that will stimulate change are:
(i) the return of tourism to the Adriatic coast, which will expand the market for high quality,
ecologically clean products;
(ii) the growing awareness to environmental issues and to healthy food, and the realization
that Croatia may have a comparative advantage in producing healthy food for the
European market; and
(iii) the low labor costs offers prospects for the development of labor-intensive production.
20.   Low input techniques are more knowledge intensive, if they are to be reliable and profitable.
Practicing a more rational use of inputs to maximize income, rather than yields, also requires fine tuning
at farm level. The adaptation of Croatia's farmers to the new circumstances requires new skills, as well
as closer links with scientists and technicians to develop these techniques. This link is provided through
the extension services.
21.   Given the uncertainties regarding the future of the social farms and cooperatives, and the
continuing processes of economic restructuring, the short-term option is to improve the operational
efficiency and effectiveness of the small state-financed extension service. However, a progression to a
semi-privatized advisory service must be an objective and parallel development of private consultants
would be supported through the project. Future private sector advisory services could emerge through
farmer-owned cooperatives, commodity associations and agribusiness. Such initiatives would also be
encouraged during project implementation. Progressively, farmers are expected to absorb an increasing
share of the costs of their advisory services.
22.   The current structure is being reorganized at all levels. At the Ministry level, a clear definition
of responsibilities has been agreed, providing a direct line of communication between the extension
service and the Ministry and the new Agriculture Research Council. The nucleus Zupanija Extension
Support Centers (ZESCs) is being established and officers will have a career structure and salary scales,
awarding for performance.
23.   The farmers of Croatia are a heterogeneous group, and so are their advisory requirements.
However, with the limited staffing resources available, it will not be possible to give cover to all farmers
on a one-to-one basis. Knowledge on the present situation and performance of these farmers will be
crucial to the design and extension strategies and activities. The current constraints on transport,
equipment and extension aids will have to be addressed and the knowledge and skills mix of the extension
service developed through a broad training program.



Project Implementation Plan                      - 47 -                                        ANNEX A
24.   The future public extension service will have to operate within the limits of a severe budget
constraint and care must be taken to control recurrent costs. These cost constraints will prevent any major
increases in staff numbers, yet farmer contact must be increased, the operational efficiency must also be
improved and the effective delivery of relevant packages given priority. The Zupanija Extension Support
Center (ZESC) will therefore operate as the core resource center for the rural community. It should be
guided by a Zupanija extension advisory committee with strong representation from the local farmer
community, and operate on the basis of "client ownership". With limited staff resources, the ZESCs
would have to operate through farmer groups and use production/ marketing/management associations
as their primary contacts to the extent possible.
25.   Extension support services to individual farmers would have to be built through the private sector,
working in co-operation with the ZESCs and utilizing their technical resources.
26.   The focus of the future program would be on increasing farmer incomes. In the short term this
requires a thorough, critical review of past relevant research and where it can be applied, and also the
identification of the improved practices of progressive farmers through farming systems analysis. The
research program, conversely, would need to be devised jointly between scientists and producers to
develop short-term action programs to develop appropriate technologies. The effective establishment of
this linkage through a farmer guided extension service forms the main rationale of this component.
B. Objectives and Strategies
27.   The objective is to provide an extension system, within the existing fiscal, economic and social
constraints, that helps the development of sustainable increases in private farm family income and well
being. The extension service would aim do this by the provision of sound business management,
marketing and technical advice and information to farmers and their families using the most effective and
efficient communication procedures.
28.   The component, as proposed, would link agricultural extension and research workers with farmers
in the identification of production constraints and opportunities, and the development of appropriate
strategies for their solution or exploitation.
29.    More specifically, the objectives would be:
(i) to generate information on the current systems used by farmers and to identify areas
where improvements can be made;
(ii) to improve the efficiency of technology transfer and improve the relevance of information
to farmers' specific problems;
(iii)  to train and equip the extension service to adjust to the new economic environment; and
(iv) to develop an operating mode conducive to building increased co-operation and
participation between both public and private extension and research, and the farming
community.



ANNEX A                                        - 48 -                       Project Implementation Plan
C. Project Components
30.   Support to Public Extension Services. Under the project the existing public Zupanija (district)
extension offices (21 with a total staff of some 110) would develop into community supervised Zupanija
Extension Service Centers (ZESC). These would provide the impetus to farmer group formation (see
below) and act as a source of infonnation and specialized guidance to private advisers directly serving
the farmers. The ZESC would be assisted through voluntary local Advisory Committees representing the
local farming community and agro-business. Office, computer and teaching equipment, and vehicles
would be made available to the ZESCs.
31.   Detailed Features:
Zupanija Extension Service Centers (ZESCs). The existing county extension centers (one in each
of the 21 Zupanija) would be developed in to Zupanija Extension Service Centers (ZESCs) and
consist of a professional team of 4-5 extension officers and one support staff, led by an extension
coordinator.
Each ZESC would be provided with two cars and mileage allowance for staff-owned cars.
Provision is also made for office accommodation at the Zupanija headquarters including rentals,
communication and office equipment supplies. Each would be provided with a set of office
furniture,  three  personal  computers,  appropriate  software,  and  a  photocopier.
Extension/communication equipment, including slide and overhead projectors, VDO and camera
would be included. To support the development of ZESC activities, the project would provide
funds for on-farm technology testing, publication of extension material, the production of a farm
management handbook, and for subscriptions to magazines.
32.   Diagnostic Survey. During early project implementation, each ZESC would carry out a one-time,
farm community based diagnostic survey in order to develop a thorough understanding of the constraints
and potential of the areas they are servicing, and to give the extension services the opportunity to inform
farmers in their area of the new extension approach. The diagnostic survey exercise would help define
the annual working program of the Zupanija extension service (a systematic working program, including
monitorable performance indicators for the public extension services was developed in 1995 for the first
time) MAF's extension department with TA provided under a Japanese Grant funding would develop a
simple methodology to carry out these diagnostic surveys.
33.   Private Extension Support. The formation of a limited number of farmer groups (some 45
groups, roughly 2 groups in each Zupanija) on a pilot basis would be supported in view of farmer groups
employing private advisors in agricultural production, farm business management, and marketing
associations management on a matching grant basis. Each group would directly employ its own adviser
who would be funded by MAF during the first four years on a matching grant basis and receive some
basic training equipment on a grant basis. The public extension service would develop standard contracts
and provide the newly established farmer groups with legal advice during the process of hiring private
advisors.
34.   Detailed Features:
Support to Farmers Associations. The project would encourage farmers to form self-help groups
with a common interest in technology development, bulk supply of inputs, product marketing,
or in sharing equipment, essentially by: (i) limiting time spent by the public extension officers



Project Implementation Plan                      - 49 -                                        ANNEX A
with individual farmers and extending public extension efforts with farmer groups; (ii) offering
legal and administrative information/advice on formal group formation; and (iii) providing
technical and managerial training to group leaders. Such training would include both informal
training, extended during visits or short seminars by zupanija staff, and formal training, which
would be organized at national level and involve guest speakers from agribusiness.
Private Sector Extension Services.  The provision of private advisory services would be
developed in conjunction with the establishment of farmer associations. Such associations would
be eligible under the project to receive grants to directly hire on a full time basis qualified
professional agricultural staff (many of which are presently with the public sector). It is
anticipated that some 45 groups employing advisers be forned within the duration of the project.
Grants would cover: (1) training in business, marketing and extension management.; (2) partial
salary support for farmer association employing a private consultants over a four year period on
a matching grant basis (based on the following matching grant formula: 80% in year 1, 50% in
year 2, 35% in year 3, and 25 in year 4; no support would be provided thereafter; the balance
would be provided by the farmer group) ; (3) provision of basic equipment (computer & printer
etc.) to the farmer group.
35.   Farmer-based Milk Recording Pilot Scheme. Also on a pilot basis, farmer herd recording
groups (some 45 in three Zupanijas) would be supported through the Croatian Livestock Selection Center
(CLSC) in close collaboration with the ZESC to promote farmer based milk recording. Milk recording
data, an important management tool in milk and animal production would be used by the extension
service and the farmer herd recording group. Farmer-based milk recording would also be a first step in
supporting private animal breeding efforts and in reducing the high costs of animal performance recording
presently carried out by the public sector. Project support would include milk production recording
equipment, limited computer equipment, and industry staff training including a study tour to selected
European animal breeding programs.
36.   Training Program. A substantial training program, including: a trainers of trainer program; a
one-time general staff orientation course; and regular and specialized in-service training, would be carried
out to enable the staff of the extension service to acquire the skills necessary to operate under a changed
mandate. Subjects would include farm business management, financial and marketing skills, group
formation skills, preparation of mass media and training packages, and information management. The
training of trainer program would be carried out prior to project start-up financed through a Japanese
Grant. Limited study tours would also be carried out by selected extension experts to get a better
understanding of extension issues and operations in other countries. Private advisors working under the
farmer group pilot scheme would also participate in this training program.
37.   Detailed Features:
A Reorientation Training program would be supported by the project for all extension officers.
The staff would undertake a short course designed to familiarize them with the role and activities
of the extension service. The course would be conducted by senior extension staff who would
themselves be trained through a eight week training course, designed and conducted by an
internationally recruited consultant. This training of trainers would take place prior to project
start-up. All other extension staff would receive their training within two years of the trainers
being trained.



ANNEX A                                        - 50 -                        Project Implementation Plan
The project would also support a Regular annual In-service Training program for extension
service staff. Extension staff would participate in a one-week training course at the zonal level,
designed to update their knowledge and skills, based on the extension themes for that particular
year. A full-time training and staff development coordinator would be appointed at headquarters
with the task of organizing these annual training programs and conducting skill-gap analysis for
all staff.
Additional Specialized Technical Training would be provided to extension staff in making the
best use of mass media including radio, the press and TV and in technologies of production
where skill gaps are identified. Training would be provided by specialists from Croatia and by
internationally recruited consultants.
Short-term Overseas Training would be supported for eight staff (4 agro-economists and
4 extension officers) for three months each at appropriate training institutions to gain experience
in training organization, skill job analysis and extension methods and administration.
38.   Mass Media Programs. Approximately 25 thematic training packages would be developed for
use by Zupanija extension service staff and other advisers for incorporation within an extension program
for farmers. The training packages would also form the basis of mass media activities which would be
developed on a contractual basis involving the extension and research services. Topics would include farm
business management, improving marketing arrangements of primary producers, and technical items such
as integrated crop production and pest management.
39.   Detailed Features:
The extension service would introduce the use of mass media techniques in widening its farmer
contact capacity. Professional producers (TV and radio) would be commissioned to prepare
programs on specific themes being extended in collaboration with the extension and research
services.. Content would be both technical or of general interest to the rural communities. An
allowance of roughly US$430,000 has been included to cover the costs of production of about
25 programs over four years.
40.   On-farm Trials and Demonstration: Farmer - Extension Research Link. A on-farm trial and
demonstration program would be carried out through the ZESC in coordination with agriculture
researchers perusing competitive grant-based applied research supported through the project (see para
below).
41.   Technical Assistance. Four areas of TA would be required during the life of the project:
*  three months consultancy for the training of trainers (which would be financed under the
Japanese Grant Facility);
*  eleven months consultancy on extension methodology, adaptive research priorities, design and
evaluation of trials; management and organization of an extension program, program
monitoring and evaluation;
*  three months consultancy for special skills training amongst extension staff: audio-visual
communication, farmer association organization, farm business management, financial and
marketing skills, information management, trials and demonstration management.



Project Implementation Plan                    - 51 -                                      ANNEX A
* nine months of local consultant time in media production, support to farmers marketing
associations, guest speakers at local training sessions, and computing skills.
D. Environmental Guidelines
42.   In order to ensure sustainability of agriculture production and the safe use and handling of
agrochemicals, MAF would develop environmental ("best practice") guidelines for the extension services
in line with applicable environmental legislation no later than 12 months after effectiveness (see para. 7.2
in the SAR).
E. Organization, Management and Implementation
43.   The extension program would be managed and implemented through the Extension Division of
the MAF headed by the Deputy Minister (Extension). There will be three tiers of management
(i) MAF Headquarters
(ii) Zupanija Extension Service Centers
(iii) Farmer advisers
44.   The extension program would be managed and implemented through the Extension Division
established within MAF under the Deputy Minister (Extension). The extension service is a separate
operations service, has its own budget and permanent staff. The extension division would be responsible
for its own staff recruitment. A recent staff ranking system has been established which provides for all
professional staff to improve their position based on merit.
45.   The HQ staff comprise an operations manager, information/media officer, a general agriculturalist
with comprehensive experience in dealing with farmers, and a training officer.
HEADQUARTERS STAFFNG
46.   The HQ Division, apart from the general management of the service, would have the following
broad functions:
(i) Designing and coordinating the training programs: reorientation, in-service and technical;
(ii) selecting candidates for overseas training;
(iii) collaboration with the Agriculture Research Council in defining national research
priorities;
(iv) reviewing existing research requests and identifying technology gaps between
"progressive" and "average" farmers in the formulation of short-term extension advice -
packages;
(v)  maintain linkages to the research community



ANNEX A                                          - 52 -                        Project Implementation Plan
(vi) providing technical, economic and market information to field extension staff, and
generally sustaining a farmer profit-oriented approach to be promoted by extension staff;
and
(vii) participation in and developing mass media programs.
TRANING
47.    Training of trainers. All field staff recruited to the new extension service would require extensive
re-training. In view of the comprehensive training programs required, it is proposed to first implement
a trainers' program to produce qualified staff to cater for the various training needs prior to project start-
up of field operations. The training of 12 trainers (eight general agriculturalists and four with farm
management/economics experience) and who are receptive to new ideas would be supported. It is
expected that these trainers would also evolve into leadership functions within the extension service. This
trainers' program would be funded under a Japanese Grant Fund.
48.   The trainers would complete an intensive two-month course which would include the following
general subjects: a market economy and how it operates; the meaning of economic competitiveness from
the production perspective - focusing on profit increases, rather than production; the changing economy
and its implications for farmers for their production mix, and for extension; the role and potential of
small family farms, advantages and disadvantages; investment analysis; gross margin and whole farm
system analysis; participatory constraint analysis of the family farm; how to learn from farmers - listening
as an effective tool in gaining farmers' confidence; public speaking skills, the use of extension aids to
teaching and learning; extension through farmer groups - group dynamics and management, how to formn
extension groups; the need for and advantages of continuing interaction between extension agents
researchers and farmers. To the extent necessary, familiarization with computers and the necessary
analytical tools would be included in the training program. The training would be practically orientated,
with regular on-farm exercises interacting with farm families operating differing production systems with
various resource capacities. The training specialist would prepare guidelines and course material for
extension staff training to be carried out by the trainers.
49.   Reorientation training. The reorientation training would cover all extension staff. Over the first
two years of the project, the total staff complement would receive training. The course would focus on
the operational changes needed to effectively transfer relevant information which would be adopted by
farmers. A two-week course would be held based on the curricula outlined above. Each training course
for about ten officers would be carried out by the trainers over a two-week period. Provision is made for
other specialists, including the media to participate. The program would be organized by the training
specialist of MAF's Extension Department.
50.   In-service technical training. In-service training would focus on up-to-date technology
development from research, the latest development in market opportunities and on the extension themes
developed for various farming systems. This training schedule would provide for a forum to discuss
current farm constraints encountered by field staff. Regular in-service training would be central to
maintaining a responsive and motivated extension operation and establish a base for research extension
dialogue. The training officer from HQ would prepare and organize the program. The four Zonal
Research Extension Coordination Units (ZERCU) to be supported under the project would play a vital
role, both in delivery of technical packages and as listeners to problems identified by extension staff. The
media would also be involved and would benefit from the subject matter discussions for the formulation



Project Implementation Plan                      - 53 -                                       ANNEX A
of their programs. The training sessions would be for one week and would be supported by other
specialists as found relevant. Inputs would be required from agribusiness, banks and progressive farmers.
51.   Overseas training. Eight officers (four agro-economists and four extension officers) would be
selected for overseas visits of three months during the first three years of the project. The visits program
would be set up to cater both for short formal courses in extension methodology and practical exposure
to operations in the field. The candidates would have shown the necessary aptitude to absorb and
communicate and would necessarily have fluency in a West European language. On return, the trainees
would be required to hold a series of seminars for extension staff and be required to participate in in-
service staff training courses.
MONIORING AND CONTROL OF THE EXTENSION SERVICE
52.   Baseline survey. Monitoring and control functions are essential for effective management. The
effectiveness of extension programs are very difficult to assess because of the wide range of factors that
affect farmer decision making and profitability most of which are outside the control of the extension
staff. (Price movements and weather patterns are obvious examples). Nevertheless the changes in the
farming community need to be identified. To identify whether progress towards the objectives has been
made, a baseline survey would be carried out by the Institute of Agriculture Economic in Year one of
the project and that it is repeated in five years time to assess changes. The survey would aim to measure
current production parameters and farmer attitudes. (See Chapter VIII, on Policy Analysis Unit.)
53.   Component Performance. In addition, MAF's Department of Extension and Agriculture would
be responsible for monitoring project performance on the basis of the indicators outlined in Annex D.)
SHE ZUPANIA EXTENSION SERVICE CENTERS (ZESCS)
54.   The 21 centers would be strengthened during the first two years of the project. Although based
at the Zupanija administrative centers, the ZESC staff would report directly on technical and operational
matters to their headquarters in Zagreb. The administrative head of the Zupanija would be fully informed
of the content and planning of field extension programs and his advice and assistance sought in
mobilization of the farmer community and to provide support and endorse media campaigns. The ZESCs
would have no administrative responsibilities within the Zupanija structure.
55.   ZESCAdvisory Committees would be established in each zupanija to assist the ZESC staff identify
and meet the needs of the farmers. Membership would comprise of a majority of farmers with
representatives of produce buyers and the supply industry. Initially members would be appointed by
appropriate organizations including the Croatian Farmers Association. In future elections could be held.
Total membership would be 9. The secretariat would be provided by the ZESC. Committee Members
would represent the Zupanija on the advisory board of the Zonal Research Extension Coordination Units
(see Chapter IV, on Research).
56.   The ZESC team would maintain a close liaison and give operational support to the Advisory
Committees who would be their link to the formulation of research programs. They would provide
information on technical and market constraints.
57.   Each Zupanija has a different density of farmers and mix of farm holding sizes and systems.
Given the limited number of staff available and the large number of farmers the Zupanija extension
service would be structured as a resource center with four main objectives:



ANNEX A                                          - 54 -                        Project Implementation Plan
(i)  To provide information to private sector and other 'front line' advisers.
(ii) To encourage the establishment of self help farmer groups.
(iii) To provide specialist support and advice to advisers and farmers.
(iv) To provide feedback to MAF headquarters.
58.    The center would be staffed by a senior extension specialist (The Center Manager), supported
by 3-4 specialists, depending on the agricultural importance of the area, its cropping systems and the mix
of enterprises. Additional extension staff may be provided by the Zupanija (As is the case at Poric.)
59.   Some individual farmer advice is inevitable and will continue to be necessary. Farmers will
continue to visit the extension officer and field visits by staff will be required to ensure that problems
have been correctly diagnosed and to establish the context in which the request for advice has been made.
Other visits will be necessary in order that the ZESC staff keep in touch with the needs of the farming
industry. This could occupy about 15 per cent of an adviser's time.
60.   The ZESCs would operate through farmer groups and rely heavily on videos and listening tapes
and the use of mass media (radio, television and the press). Farmers groups would be set up and group
responsibilities allocated to individual officers and routine visit programs organized. To provide adequate
coverage of groups (20 groups with about 20 farmers) and assuming 4-5 visits/year, the extension officer
would be required to spend about three days/week in the field. These visits would be supported by field
demonstrations prepared by the farmers themselves with guidance from the extension officer. These
demonstrations would support the thematic approach which is the core of the methodology and would
concentrate on lower production costs, improved profitability, market demand, comparative advantage,
chemical application rates, increasing farmer bargaining power, improvements in tillage, and soil moisture
management, irrigation investment, analysis, etc.
MAss MEDIA EXTENSION
61.   The mass media already make a substantial contribution to the dissemination of farmer
information. Farm programs have a wide audience, and considerable scope exists for expansion of the
service. The project would therefore seek to generate a substantial information base for program content.
The training officer in the Ministry would be responsible for the co-ordination of mass media extension
and for developing information channels. He would have funds at his disposal to employ local media
consultants for specific tasks.
ROLE OF THE PRIVATE SECTOR
62.   The principle of a limited public extension system operating at Zupanija level is fully supported
by MAF. MAF also wishes to control recurrent costs and to retain highly qualified staff and to invest in
their training and equipment. It is fully realized that initially they will be resource persons, and individual
farmer contact will be limited;  considerable emphasis is therefore laid on the use of multiple
communication channels, including mass media.
63.    MAF supports the provision of 'front line' advice by private extension and consultancy services.
There is a pool of staff available from the former municipalities and from the AK support services that
could be gainfully employed in a private sector role, complementary to the public services proposed.
64.   The project would encourage the use of paid-for services, which meet a demand, from farmers
organizations/associations (individual well-off farmers have generally access to good information).



Project Implementation Plan                     - 55 -                                        ANNEX A
65.   There are no legal obstacles to the formation of private consulting companies.
F. Envrionmental Impact
66.   The modification to tillage practices would have a positive effect on improving infiltration rates
and soil moisture relationships. There will be a positive effect on controlling run-off and soil and wind
erosion.
67.   The overall strengthening of the extension service would assist farmers in the safe and rational
use of plant protection chemicals. The increased use of integrated pest management techniques, awarded
priority under the adaptive research program, should provide for a general reduction in chemical use.
DRAFT TERMS OF REFERENCE FCOR
T1ECHNICAL ASSISTANCE CONSULTANTS
SPECIAL SKILLS TRAINERS
Qualifications, The trainers will be specialists in their field able to identify the skils and
techniques appropriate to Croatia. They will have excellent communications abilities .and be
experienced in training advisers and scientists. One of the trainers will be expert in the preparation
of audio visual programs for the mass media and as-training packages fir fanner groups..
Duration. Course duration will vary according to need but is expected to be one or two weeks.:
Four person months.have been allocated.
Duties and Responsibilities. There is a need for the introduction of modern production techniques
on small family farms and for training extension workers in the use of the massnmedia and the.
preparation of training packages. The consultants will deliver participatory courses to extension
workers, researchers, private advisers and leading fanners. Subject areas, apart from use of the
media, are yet to be determined but could include integrated crop production, cattle nutrition,
efficient utilization of forage, producing fruit and vegetables for the European market.
