Project Implementation Plan                    - 59 -                                      ANNEX A
IV. COMPONENT DESCRIPTION FOR AGRICULTURE RESEARCH
A. Components Objectives and Activities
SUMMARY
68.   The main objective of the component would be to develop a research service responsive to serving
the requirements of private farm families now operating in a market economy. More specific objectives
would be:
(i) to initiate and sustain a process where past academic and production orientated research is
replaced by a more analytical, multidisciplinary, economic and producer constraint
orientated approach;
(ii) to establish effective participation of producers in identifying priorities for research and
deciding on allocation of funds to research projects;
(iii) to introduce rational planning of research projects to resolve readily identifiable constraints
at farm level, with due consideration of the total socio-economic farm system;
(iv) to provide for a system which can generate research projects, award priorities and
effectively implement them;
(v) to develop strong linkages between research and extension through which technology transfer
and information flow maintains relevance to farmers' needs;
(vi) to better equip the research services to adjust to the requirements of private farming needs
and to enable them to implement contract research at field level;
69.   Component Activities would consist of the following:
70.   The Agriculture Research Council. The project would support an applied agriculture research
management system to produce relevant and timely information for the private family farmers. The
establishment of an independent Agriculture Research Council (ARC) which would establish research
policy and priorities for funding applied agriculture research would be key to ensuring relevance and
timeliness of research to farmers. The ARC would consist of a majority of farmer representatives (9
members nominated from each of the nine agriculture regions). The chairperson would be appointed by
the Minister of Agriculture and Forestry upon nomination of the ARC. Other council members would
consist of a representative of MAF, a representative from the Ministry of Science and Technology
(MOST), three representatives of the scientific community, and one from the agrobusiness sector. The
research agenda would be (i) open-ended and determined by the ARC, and (ii) would allow for a broad
scope of topics to be researched ranging from production technology to market research and development.
Any bona fide Croatian research institution, public or private, would be eligible to participate under the
competitive research grant system.
71.          Demand-driven research. The ARC would invite the scientific community (both public
and private sector) on the basis of publicly announced Terms of Reference, to submit research proposals
to the council, i.e. contracts for applied research would be demand driven. The ARC shall make such



ANNEX A                                         - 60 -                        Project Implementation Plan
rules and establish procedures as required openly to carry out its responsibility. The Council shall also
promulgate procedures and select participants for external and internal training of scientific and faculty
personnel supported under the project. MOST would carry out the technical and scientific review
(involving national and foreign reviewers) of the submitted proposals and would submit to the ARC
shortlists of proposals in the respective areas of applied research for decision making. MOST would
administer, supervise (including on-site supervision) and audit the research contracts. The ARC would
be assisted by a small (one or two) person secretariat which would call the ARC meetings on the initiative
of the Chairperson. It would also be responsible to liaise with MOST and to also carry-out on-site
supervision of applied research contracts.
72.   Agriculture Research Fund. Applied agriculture research would be funded on a competitive grant
basis through an Agriculture Research Fund which would finance incremental equipment and research
costs. In the context of applied research, MOST would continue, as in the past, to finance the salaries
and basic operating costs of the public research institutions; MOST would also continue to support basic
agriculture research (including salaries, buildings, utilities and equipment) and continue to support and
pay for post graduate students.
73.   The Agriculture Research Fund would be established under the authority of the ARC. During
project implementation this fund would be financed to a large extent through the World Bank loan
(roughly estimated at around 80%) with the balance from budget allocations (roughly 20%). In order
to ensure that both regional and national research priorities are being addressed a funding formula would
be agreed upon by the ARC, for a period not shorter than three years, which would allocated funds to
the main agriculture regions (based on the relative contribution to agriculture GDP) and to overall
national research. In order to ensure financial sustainability of the Agriculture Research Fund, MAF is
committed to introduce in the course of project implementation modest commodity based levies paid by
the producer, once the overall concept has gained credibility within the farming community. This
credibility is largely a function of the relevance and quality of the applied research and the associated
independence of the ARC.
74.   Research - Extension - Farmer Linkages would be strengthened through the following steps:
(i) relevance of future applied research would be improved by the above approach, which
focuses on farmer representation both at the district and the national level. At the district
level, extension and research would be guided by farmer advisory committees, the ZESC
Advisory Committees, and Zonal Advisory Committees of four small Zonal Research
Extension Coordination Units. These district and zonal advisory committees would meet
regularly, and would guide the initiatives of the Zupanija extension staff. The Zonal
Research Extension Coordination Units, each consisting of two professional staff, would
establish a formal link between the research community (in particular with researcher
under contract from the ARC), and the public and private extension services, and would
assist in the coordination of on-field trials and demonstrations. A first Zonal Research
Extension Coordination Unit would be established in the second year of the project, while
the other three be set up subsequently after evaluation of the performance of the first
unit. At the national level, farmer representatives, nominated by their peers from the
district and zonal Advisory Committee, would ensure that farmers' problems become the
focus of the future research agenda;
(ii) invitations for applied research proposals would request a concept by the bidder on how
the research results would reach the extension services and farmers; this concept would
be part of the overall proposal evaluation;



Project Implementation Plan                    - 61 -                                      ANNEX A
(iii) the project has budgeted for field trials and demonstrations to be carried out by the
extension services; the field trials would be linked to the research proposals in order to
ensure on-farm experimentation. The successful research bidder would engage in
contracts with individual farmers to carry out on-farm trials under the supervision of the
researcher.
75.   Project support. The project would support the establishment of the ARC by providing: (i)
technical assistance (partly through a Japanese Grant) to initially work out and during implementation
improve the operational procedures of the competitive grant based research management system; (ii)
office and computer equipment, and a vehicles; and (iii) covering initial operating costs of the Council
and the secretariat.
76.   The project would provide the ARC with an Agriculture Research Fund of roughly US$5.2
million over four years which would finance all the public supported applied and adoptive agricultural
research (including equipment requirements) carried out in the country during the course of project
implementation. The amount to be provided under the Agriculture Research Fund has been established
in function of the amount of resources that could be levied from the farming community in the future;
and by estimating costs of four indicative research programs identified by the research community as
being of potentially high priority. Basic agricultural research would remain under the auspices of MOST.
77.   Under the project a training program for applied research staff would be carried out; including
a visiting scientist program, a research staff orientation training courses, applied research management
training, research management study tours for ARC members, and research reporting seminars. Computer
networking of the Agriculture Faculty, University of Zagreb, to international agriculture databases would
be provided, as would be subscriptions to journals and books. The four Zonal Research Extension
Coordination Units would be phased in during project implementation as the ARC becomes operational
and would be provided with office, computer, and training equipment.
AGRiCuLTuRAL RESEARCH CouNcEL (ARC)
78.   The project would establish the Agriculture Research Council (ARC) to organize, plan and
manage agricultural research on behalf of the MAF. The Council would be the focal point around which
the research investment revolves. This newly created body, chaired by a member appointed by the
Minister of Agriculture but having a majority of members selected by private farmers from among their
ranks, would be the decision making body identifying applied agricultural research that is needed and
selecting those research contracts to be funded.
79.   This Council would consist of fifteen members. Nine would be farmers with one from each of
the nine agriculture areas of Croatia. Farmer organizations would make these selections of members who
would serve fixed terms and who would be eligible to succeed themselves only once before moving off
the Council but with an opportunity of being re-elected again after a hiatus of at least one term. Initial
terms for a half or third of the farmers would be only half or one-third as long as a normal term so that
there would exist a relatively stable body of farmer members with only a half or a third susceptible to
change in any one year.
80.   Other elected members of the Council would be three representing the scientific-academic
community and selected from among active applied research scientists by MOST. These three members
would have staggered terms so that one would continue through each election. The final elected member
of the Council would be elected from the agricultural business community. The principal roles of these



ANNEX A                                          - 62 -                         Project Implementation Plan
four members would be to provide advice and guidance to the Council as pertains to scientific, technical
and business-industry feasibility of possible research and development thrusts to be considered, to help
select domestic and foreign technical reviewers for project proposals and as trainers in Croatia, and to
help identify possible international institutions where Croatians could be trained.
81.   Appointed members in addition to the chair appointed by MAF would be a representative from
the Ministry of Science and Technology and a representative from MAF appointed by its respective
minister. The latter would serve a critical role of helping the Council coordinate research and extension
activities at the national level while the representative from the Ministry of Science nd Technology would
help the Council coordinate academic and basic research funding by this Ministry with applied research
funding by the Council.
82.   The ARC will have a permanent full-time secretariat composed of an Executive Secretary who
would be a senior science administrator with demonstrated executive capabilities working full-time for
the Council. MAF would provide accommodation for the Secretariat. This secretariat would be
responsible for executing the day to day activities and special assignments by the Council. The functions
of the Secretariat would be to: (1) convene the Council; (2) produce the agendas; (3) receive project
proposals; (4) attend project evaluation sessions; (5) prepare the annual report.
83.   The Council will establish its own rules and procedures, set its own agenda, decide what research
thrusts would be advertised for proposals and select those proposals it deems most deserving and qualified
for funding. It would prescribe the format for research proposals. Finally, the Council would approve
persons to receive training domestically and abroad. MOST would: (1) screen and prepare shortlist of
proposals based on peer review for submission to the ARC; (2) process award of contracts; (3) provide
for the timely disbursement of funds; and (4) monitor expenditures against implementation schedule.
ZONAL RESEARCH EXTENSION COORDINATING UNrrs (ZRECU)
84.   One such unit would be created in each of the country's four agro-ecological zones and each
would have an advisory committee of one or three farmers from each Zupanija, depending on the number
of Zupanijas in the zone, and one person from the agri-business community. The farmers would serve
staggered terms and be selected by the respective Zupanija advisory committees in the zone and the agri-
business representative would be selected by the appropriate agri-business organization(s) in the zone.
The advisory committee would serve to advise the zonal units about research and extension needs and
may nominate the representatives from their respective zones to serve on the Agricultural Research
Council.
85.   Staffing of the units is seen as consisting of the coordinator and an agricultural economist-farm
manager. The two science professional persons would have training essentially equivalent to members
of faculties and research institutes. The responsibilities and duties of the zonal units would include:
(i) Coordinating research and demonstration activities in the zone.
(ii) Assuring that extension and research were closely associated and that research reflects
extension needs.
(iii) Coordinating extension and research thrusts.
(iv) Following-up on the performance of research projects in the zone.



Project Implementation Plan                      - 63 -                                        ANNEX A
(v) Sending suggestions for needed research to the Agricultural Research Council.
(vi) Holding field days at experimental and demonstration sites in the zone.
(vii) Assuring that all research and demonstration results were interpreted in simple language.
(viii) Providing for training and updating of Zupanija extension personnel.
(ix) Assisting Zupanija staffs with special problems needing study.
(x) Serving as technical back-up for Zupanija staffs.
(xi) Publishing or preparing for publication such materials that are of significant relevance in the
zone.
(xii) Maintaining close contact with several local media resources.
86.   In the absence of such zonal units, parts of the functions would have to be executed at the
Zupanija Extension Centers and part at the central level in Zagreb. Of necessity, some functions would
not be performed.
RESEARCH FUND
87.   Based on four-year costs estimates (including equipment needs) of four indicative broad areas of
research that had been identified as being potentially of highest priority (the actual research program
would be established by the ARC) a research fund in the amount of US$5.2 million would be made
available to the ARC (see table below).
88.   This amount is also estimated to be roughly in line with what could be mobilized through a future
possible levy collected from farmers applied to commodity sales of products passing through centrally
located collection / processing markets. Such a levy would be considered towards the end of project
implementation, once the new research system is established and has proven its value to the farming
community.
SUSTAINABLE FUNDING
89.   By the time this project ends, there will be, in place, a new system of effective research
management and an extension service made up of public specialists and private advisors. Croatia must
be ready to capitalize on the gains during the course of this project in order to maintain the pace of
transforming its agricultural industry.
90.   When this funding terminates, the major change in circumstances will be an end to this particular
source of funding through a loan from The World Bank and, perhaps, others. The rate of change will
be maintained only if all or at least a major part of the expiring funds can be replaced with domestic
support. There are a number of ways in which this might be done.
91.   First, MOST already is supporting a large number of faculty members and the facilities in which
they teach and do research and also funds some small amount of agricultural research. One possibility



ANNEX A                                                 - 64 -                            Project Implementation Plan
would be for MAF to allocate its own funds to continue the adaptive, applied and strategic research
started under this project.
92.    A second way would be for the government

equivalent by budgetary contribution, together they would adequately finance investment in agriculture
research.

97.    Ten candidates representing the major institutions would be provided with a study tour to visit
overseas research stations where these changes have been established. The candidates targeted for short
overseas training would be the younger mid-career scientists who would be leading the various sub-
projects and therefore require experience in a multidisciplinary approach to project implementation. Short
courses of two to three weeks, backed up with practical exposure in the field, would be provided for
twelve scientists.
98.    The project would also assist departments and individual scientists to develop interchange of ideas
through reciprocal visits to other countries where complementary research projects are being operated.
Initial priorities would be given to farming systems research, integrated plant protection, tillage and soil
management.
EQUIPMENT
99.    Equipment would be not be made available directly but only in the context of research contract
funded under the ARC. This would induce research proposals to minimize equipment cost and in
particular to try to find alternatives to the purchase of new equipment (e.g. sharing of equipment with
other national or international research facilities).
TECENCAL ASSISTANCE (TA)
100.   The Agriculture Research Council. The effective design and evaluation of the research program
relies heavily on the performance of the ARC. The concepts being introduced for project prioritization
and the radical changes to critically concentrate on national programs through a multidisciplinary
approach with strong economic evaluation inputs will need expert planning assistance, particularly in the
first year of operation. TA should be applied to assist in the introduction of the system, the review and
selection of the first-year projects and their supervision during the first years' growing season. A senior



ANNEX A                                          - 66 -                        Project Implementation Plan
scientist with experience in the functioning of research councils for a period of five months, over possibly
three visits would be provided under the project.
B. RESEARCH COMPONENT COST
101.  Total Cost (including contingencies) are estimated at US$8.0 detailed cost breakdown is given
in Annex B, Table 2.2.
C. ORGANIZATION, MANAGEMENT AND IMPLEMENTATION
DESIGN OF REQUESTS FOR PROPOSALS
102.  The following provides guidelines for the development of the operational procedures for the ARC
which wuld take place in late 1995 with Technical Assistance financed under a Japanese Grant in
collaboration between MAF and MOST.
103.  The universal experience is that scientists like to do research and take every opportunity to secure
funding for their research programs. Another observation is that scientists are willing to change their
research activities to accommodate the requirements of the funding sources or the sponsors. It also was
apparent that many faculty, research institutes and former "agro-kombinat" research specialists are
interested in doing applied research of immediate relevance to farmers. While no laboratory is ever
"fully equipped", and with the possible exception of the dislocated faculty at Osijek, agricultural scientists
in Croatia seem to feel their facilities are mostly adequate to undertake the private farmer focused
research needed. No doubt that greater international contacts in the future will reveal deficiencies that
the Agricultural Research Council should work to rectify.
104.  The large number of potential investigators who have had very little if no research funding from
the state in the recent past and the large range of topics this group of scientists could address, makes it
imperative that the topics for research be carefully identified and described. This will permit those
persons with skills to do the research required by the Agricultural Research Council to be able to identify
their interest with the need and submit proposals.  On the other hand it would not give false
encouragement to those excellent scientists who do not have the right training and interest to perform the
research needed. In short, a clearly stated request for research and extension proposals would avoid a
flood of proposals that are not relevant and would help assure that the submitted proposals are most likely
to meet the Council's requirements even though not all of them could be funded. Experience elsewhere
has shown that there will be requests for more funds than are available.
105.  Advisory Input: The structure of advisory councils at the Zupanija and zonal levels is focused
to provide the majority of members on the Agriculture Research Council, assure that they are identified
by the best available elective process and also assure they are private farmers. These advisory councils,
especially at the zonal levels, have a stated responsibility to propose the extension and research needs to
the Council. These facts and the desire to make the research relevant to farmer needs makes it of utmost
importance that the advisory system is used and listened to during the planning of research thrusts to be
supported each year.
106.  Once started, advisory input to the Council should be continuous and suggestions accepted and
kept on file until decisions are to be made on which will be pursued. Six weeks or two months before



Project Implementation Plan                     - 67 -                                       ANNEX A
decisions are to be made on research thrusts once or twice a year, a reminder should go to all Zupanija
and zonal offices that any additional suggestions should be submitted in order to be considered. Members
of the Council from the various zones should also talk about extension and research needs whenever they
meet with the Zonal Units and other farmers and they should bring suggestions they hear to Council
meetings.
107.  Administrative Determination: The Council will finally decide upon the fields in which it wishes
to request proposals for projects. Having done so, it should scrupulously follow its own requests relative
to proposals to consider. Even if a very good proposal that does not fit the criteria announced is received
it should be deferred. If a non-conforming proposal is a worthwhile idea, the Council may add it to the
next round of requests for proposals and perhaps receive even better proposals from among which to
choose.
108.  It is suggested that research awards be made twice a year because both fall sown and spring sown
crops are common and they represent entirely different species in most cases. The call for proposals
would make this clear in the announcement. In addition to permitting scientists better to utilize recently
collected data, the two awarding dates would spread the work load for the Council as well as its
secretariat. Making more frequent awards of funds might overwhelm a system that is just beginning to
function.
109.  Eligibility for Funding: Any bona fide Croatian research institute, public or private, would be
eligible to participate in this competitive grant research approach.
REsARCH AND/OR EXTENSION PROPOSALS
110.  In order to fulfill the close association of research with extension and to assure that research will
be relevant to private farms, all applied field work needs to be evaluated by a standardized procedure
even if different priorities are given to adaptive, applied and strategic research and demonstrations.
111.  Some funding agencies which expect large volumes of projects use a two-step process. The
first step is a preproposal and, as implied, is a short description of the project. Usually a length limit,
e.g., 2 pages or 3 pages of text is set plus, the budget sheet. These are quickly evaluated and those that
interest the sponsor would be developed into a full proposal in the form of the project outline discussed
above. Those of no further interest would be dropped from consideration.
112.  Evaluation: This procedure is done on both the preproposal and/or the full proposal. It would
be done for the Council by a panel of anonymous reviewers who do not have a direct relationship to the
project under the guidance of MOST.
113.  If these were preproposals, the Council would look carefully at the documents and evaluation and
identify those of greatest interest. It would then determine the cost of these based upon the budgets and
determine how many could be funded at the full levels, and if all proposals would still be as interesting
or if the order could be changed or budgets adjusted. Then a decision would be made on which scientists
would be asked to submit a full proposal.
114.  Funding: The final step is for the Council to decide which projects to fund, for how long and
at what level of funding, perhaps negotiating for lower expenses by reducing the project or other means.



ANNEX A                                          - 68 -                        Project Implementation Plan
115.  Fairness and good morale both demand that reasonable research expenditures be made in all
regions. There are several ways to do this, but one way of allocating funds regionally can be described
in the request for proposals is based on a certain part of the total funds available divided equally among
the zones and the rest on the basis of a formula. For example, 25% could be allocated for good
proposals equally among the zones, and the balance allocated on the basis of number of private farms,
the rural population, gross agricultural product, hectares of arable land, etc., or a combination of two
or, no more than, three. So, as an example again, the remaining 75% of the funds could be allocated
equally based on number of private farms and hectares of cultivated crops. There should always be a
provision that if enough acceptable good proposals did not come from some zone, the unused funds would
be allocated among the others by the formula. The cost of the secretariat and the cost of Agricultural
Research Council activities would be taken out before allocation. New equipment or machinery needs
would be a part of the project proposals. New permanent facilities would be handled outside the ARC
funding process.
REPORTING AND DEFENSE
116.  A formal written report of progress must be required at least once a year for all funded projects.
This written report would be due a month or six weeks before new projects are considered for funding
and previously funded projects scheduled for extension for the second or subsequent years of their
planned terms. Oral reports would also be given at the time just prior to when new awards are to be
made. These two reports would give the Agricultural Research Council an opportunity to decide whether
the project has made progress enough to justify its continuation or whether it should be terminated
because it is not ever likely to succeed.
117.  If the Council decides to proceed with projects starting twice a year, then the report reading and
hearing tasks would be spread and the job of overseeing the results made easier. In case projects are
funded only once a year, the reporting could be made a month earlier so tentative decisions on continuing
projects could be out of the way before new projects are considered. If an outstanding new project
surfaces, the Council could still give it preference over a previously funded project that may be making
less than normal progress.
118.  In addition to Council members at the oral report meetings, the science professionals from the
Zonal Units should be present for the reports along with all the principal investigators making reports as
well as members of the technical review panels. It is important for the Zonal Unit coordinators to be
present so all can hear what has been achieved in the other three zones. The technical review panel
members will benefit because these reports will help them better to understand research problems and
perhaps make better evaluations for future proposals. They could also be used to help evaluate the
progress reported, in which case the would meet privately with the Council after the oral presentations.
These procedures are regularly used in progress evaluation. Again, the Council will have to conclude
its own evaluation because it is the final authority responsible for the research done with the funds
expended.
119.  One purpose of oral presentations is to allow questions to be asked from the audience so that the
investigators can defend their procedures, data collection, analysis of data and conclusions drawn. This
exercise will also be informative to Council members who will have to make more decisions about these
and other projects. It will enable them and the technical review panel members also to give advice or
suggestions on how to make the studies more meaningful and may also help them write better requests
for proposals.



Project Implementation Plan                    - 69 -                                       ANNEX A
ADAPTING TO EXTENSION USE
120.  Research results of applied studies will have no practical value until they are taken to farmers,
explained and farmers begin to adopt them. The information will need to be expressed in lay language
for farmers to study and understand. There are a number of ways in which information can be
disseminated. Some are listed as follows:
(i) Reports by the Agricultural Research Council of projects that are funded and where they will
be conducted.
(ii) Opportunities for farmers to view demonstrations and experiments during their development.
(iii) Local reports on radio, television, and newspaper reporting what is happening.
(iv) Reports of the results at the end of the season at the time the written report is due.
(v) News of demonstrations planned on the basis of what more complex experiments have shown.
(vi) Writing of pamphlets or bulletins for farmer use with the Zonal Unit science professionals and
research investigators cooperating.
(vii) Hold instructional meetings for farmers in each Zupanija.
(viii) Hold informational meetings for extension professionals and farm advisors in each zone.
121.  As always, scientists should be encouraged also to publish their work in scientific and technical
journals and bulletins. These publications would be a legitimate cost of doing research. While these
scientific papers might be slower in becoming published, the publications for extension use need to be
distributed as soon as possible by oral, visual or written means. The urgency of changing the nature of
agriculture in Croatia demands that information be released promptly and put into use as soon as it is
prudently possible.
MONITORING
122.  Component monitoring would be carried out by MAF's Department of Agriculture and of
Extension on the basis of the Performance Indicators outlined in Annex D.



ANNEX A                                        - 70 -                        Project Implementation Plan
V. COMPONENT DESCRIPTION FOR ANIMAL
HEALTH SUPPORT SERVICES
A. DETAILED DESIGN FEATURES
123.  Current constraints with livestock disease control in Croatia, and to a lesser degree veterinary
epidemiology, are associated with outdated diagnostic equipment, inadequately trained staff, insufficient
resources for monitoring aspects of animal health and food quality particularly food residues, and
inefficient data management and information dissemination. Project investment would assist in providing
the basic resources to overcome these constraints and is focused on the government veterinary sector were
these services are expected to remain for the foreseeable future, due to the public goods nature of these
services.
124.  Summary. Animal Health would be improved through the strengthening of epidemiological
services leading to better disease identification and eradication programs. The National Veterinary
Institute (NVI) and its four regional branches would be provided with vehicles and a comprehensive range
of equipment to intensify the national disease surveillance program, a process which would be
complemented by strengthened border quarantine posts. NVI national and regional animal health data
bases would be linked to a strengthened MAF epidemiological service, and MAF's national disease
reporting service strengthened to support national disease eradication and international disease reporting
requirements. Private and public veterinarian technical and managerial skills would be improved through
local and overseas training. The National Veterinary Chamber would be strengthened. International
specialists would support the data management and veterinarian training programs.
125.  Detailed Features. Veterinary epidemiological services of the NVI would be reinforced by
provision of two national epidemiologist and two vehicles for field work, the development of a
epidemiological information service, establishment of a comprehensive Enzyme Linked Immuno-sorbent
Assay (ELISA)-based diagnostic service2 and through additional equipment to selected departments of
NVI, particularly the food residue detection laboratory (Table 1). Equipment would also be provided for
mastitis monitoring and blood, faecal and plant tissue analysis capability in support private veterinary
practices and farmers participating in a whole herd health program.
126.  The four regional diagnostic centers would be assisted with ELISA and other equipment to enable
them to support the national epidemiological service and regional whole herd health programs (Table 2).
Equipment would also be provided to the Poultry Institute in Zagreb to strengthen its support capacity
(Table 2). Overseas training programs, estimated at 18 person months, for staff at the NVI and regional
laboratories would address deficiencies in disease diagnostic capability and be linked to the supply of new
equipment and services.
2/ ELISA is an immunoassay test which is superseding more traditional disease identification tests,
particularly for infectious diseases. Its advantages include low cost, high speed, field applicability
and relatively high enzyme-labeled antibodies or anti-immunoglobulins, which attach themselves
respectively to the infective viral/bacterial antigens or antibodies (produced in response to disease
infection) in the sample. Color-coded "detectors" are subsequnetly added to provide visual evidence
of infection.



Project Implementation Plan                      - 71 -                                        ANNEX A
127.  Disease reporting and information management would be improved with the establishment of a
computer network linking MAF's Zupanije (i.e., district level) veterinary inspection services and MAF's
border control posts to NVI (Table 3). This would be first initiated in project Year 1, and expanded to
the full network in Year 2 after proving effectiveness. A standard word processing and database
management software package would also be provided with each unit. Technical assistance support of 5
person-months to design and test an integrated data base and familiarize staff with its use would also be
provided. Additional technical assistance would include two person-months for whole herd health
management and three person-months for veterinary epidemiology (2 visits).
128.  Six all terrain vehicles, one each to the NVI, the four regional institutes and the Poultry Institute,
would be provided for field investigation and sample transfer work. Each vehicle would be fitted with
a post-mortem kit, field microscope and transportable cold chain. Two liaison vehicles would also be
provided to NVI (Table 4).
129.  Each of the border control posts where veterinary inspection staff are based, would be supplied
with a computer system including a laser printer and modem for connection to a national network for the
transfer of disease reports and related epidemiological information. The project would provide 20 person
months (two weeks per staff member) of introductory training for Zupanije veterinary inspectors in
computer operation and the use of the custom-designed data management software package (see para 10).
130.  A project financed re-orientation training program (Table 5) would be designed to provide private
veterinary practices with the technical and management skills needed for efficient operation. In parallel
with the Zupanije program, one veterinary doctor per practice would be provided an introductory course
in computers and related office and data management (50 person-months). The project would contract the
Croatian Veterinary Faculty (working in collaboration with the Veterinary Chamber) to identify and
design a series of short courses for private veterinary practitioners on technical subjects including, though
not limited to, whole herd health and production management, fertility management, ruminant and non-
ruminant nutrition, artificial insemination, management-related disease, animal housing and welfare, small
animal work and ELISA technology. Course work would be structured to fit the work program of a
private veterinary practice by regional weekend or evening seminars and tutorials. Provision has been
made for the preparation on contract of 10 such courses, including all course teaching materials, at an
estimated cost of US$ 10,000 each. Participants would pay in advance a course fee to cover the recurrent
costs of the course including lecturers' salaries.
131.  The support provided to NVI and the regional institutes would not entail any additional staffing.
An estimate of additional recurrent costs to make full use of the equipment that would be supplied by the
project is US$ 250,000 per annum or 10 per cent of the investment cost. It is expected that these cost will
be largely recovered through inspection fees.
132.  This component would also provide short term support to Croatian animal health services by
assisting the fledgling Veterinary Chamber to provide technical, ethical and professional support to
veterinary doctors in Croatia, subject to the legal recognition of the Veterinary Chamber under the new
Veterinary Law. It is expected that a successful and efficient support organization for the veterinary
profession would attract the majority of qualified veterinary doctors who are presently not members and
would thus become even more effective in its support and regulatory functions.
133.  Support would include equipping the Chamber's office (furniture, photocopier, communications
equipment and computer hardware and software); the publication of a Croatian Veterinary Code; and the
cost of publishing 12 issues of a quarterly veterinary newsletter (Table 6).



ANNEX A                                         -72 -                         Project Implementation Plan
B. ANIMAL HEALTH SUPPORT SERVICES COSTS
134.  The total cost (including contingencies) of the Animal Health Support Services Component is
estimated at US$6.1 million. A detailed cost breakdown is given in Annex B, Table 2.4.
C. BENEFTs AND BENEFIcIARES
135.  The expected benefits from this program are:
(i) an increased capacity of the regulatory veterinary services to diagnose and control the major
diseases of animal and public health importance;
(ii) a national, readily accessible data bank on disease distribution and occurrence to assist
Government in policy and planning work and to fulfil its obligations toward disease
reporting to the international community;
(iii) capability by the statutory veterinary institutes to analyze products of animal origin intended
for human consumption and use thus ensuring protection of public safety and health;
(iv) the capacity to monitor quality of exported food products thereby protecting the integrity of
existing markets and opening opportunity for new markets, particularly for "healthy" foods;
(v) an improved diagnostic and clinical service for farmers through the regulatory and private
veterinary services;
(vi) an effective professional and regulatory body for animal health matters; and
(vii) a body of veterinary doctors aware of and competent to carry out the professional services
required of them by the farming community.
136.  Beneficiaries will be the private farmers who will have access to enhanced cost-effective
veterinary services and thus improving the opportunity to earn greater profits. Government would also
benefit in having access to additional and more readily available animal health data on which to make
planning and policy decisions.
C. MANAGEMENT
137.  The Director, Veterinary Directorate, MAF, would have overall responsibility for the
implementation of the animal health sub-program. The Director would also be directly responsible for
the planning and implementation of the epidemiology program but would delegate responsibility for the
strengthening of the veterinary diagnostic services to the Director, National Veterinary Institute, who
would directly supervise the project activities at the NVI, the poultry institute and the four regional
diagnostic laboratories. The training modules to be produced for private veterinary practitioners would
be contracted to the Veterinary Faculty of Zagreb University which, supported by the Veterinary
Chamber in the prioritization of subject matter, would be assisted in their development by selected,



Project Implementation Plan                    - 73 -                                       ANNEX A
internationally recruited technical advisors. The Croatian Veterinary Chamber would implement its own
development as financed by the project and would collaborate with the Veterinary Faculty in the
implementation of the private veterinary training program.
D. IMPLEMENTATION
138.  The program would be executed over the four years of the project life. Laboratory equipment,
for NVI would be purchased in stages over three years while computers specifically for its use would be
made available in year one: one liaison vehicle and the 4-WD vehicle would also be made available in
year one. Laboratory equipment, computers and vehicles for the four regional institutes and the Poultry
Institute would be phased to equip two centers in year one, and three in year two. Computer equipment
to the Zupanije and border stations would first be pilot tested, then expanded to a full network in Year
3 assuming initial successful utilization of services provided.
139.  The main training and data base computer would be made available at the beginning of year one
and the data base program for epidemiology. Training of technicians, computer operators and private
veterinary practitioners would begin in year one and continue through the life of the project. Modules
for training of private veterinary practitioners would be prepared in a sequence of four modules in Year
one and three modules each in Years two and three of the project.
140.  Assistance to the Croatian Veterinary Chamber would be provided in year one.

Project Implementation Plan                    - 83 -                                     ANNEX A
VI. COMPONENT DESCRIPTION FOR
PASTURE AND FODDER DEVELOPMENT
A. OBJECTIVE
141.  The objective of the pasture program would be to reduce the cost of livestock production to
private farmers, to reduce reliance on expensive concentrate feeds and hence increase the competitiveness
of the livestock subsector by developing grassland production both on natural and sown pastures. Upland
areas would be made more productive through increased yields of natural grasslands and better
management. There would also be closer integration of livestock and crops in the agricultural systems
of the lowlands, leading to increased farm productivity.
B. DESIGN CONSIDERATIONS
142.  Pasture is among the most under-utilized resources in Croatia. Improved pasture and fodder
productivity, in terms of quality, quantity and seasonality of supply, could help reduce ruminant livestock
production costs and enable diversification of production. The enhancement of the upland grazing
industry could see the emergence of a pasture based upland cattle and sheep industry supplying weaned,
immature stock to lowland fatteners. Grassland-based livestock farmiing also offers the opportunity for
the entry into the industry of a much needed younger generation of farmers. Research into both
sustainable technology for incremental pasture production and the farming systems and sociological
settings where the technology is to be applied should help support the maintenance of the relatively
undisturbed environment that still pertains over much of Croatia. This would auger well for future niche
marketing of eco-friendly agricultural products amongst others, strengthened by project-based government
incentives in support of private market development initiatives.
143.  Pasture research in Croatia in the past has been directed to the development of sustainable,
livestock grazing-based production systems. Most pasture development has, however, focused on
uneconomic changes to the environment as the means of increasing perennial pasture productivity.
Expensive drainage and uneconomic liming and fertilization practices have made pasture improvement
uneconomic for private farmers. These policies have led to considerable government subsidy but only
limited pasture development.
144.  Government policy in the past has been unfavorable to the emergence of a pasture based livestock
industry and subsidies favored grain-based livestock feeding systems. A core scientific group continued
to investigate pasture production but its activities were severely curtailed by policy and funding
restrictions. The result has been limited progress in the development and particularly demonstration of
appropriate technology. However, with this small core scientific resource in place, opportunities for
technology transfer from similar environments and production systems in neighboring countries and with
its participation in a regional pasture research and development network, Croatia is well placed to
organize the rapid development of its pasture resource. The project would address the most immediate
of these constraints through investment in an applied, and more particularly adaptive, research program
closely linked to the work of the extension service.



ANNEX A                                        - 84 -                        Project Implementation Plan
145.  A re-oriented pasture development strategy would include: emphasis on species having low
fertilizer requirements and better adaptation to local agro-climatic conditions; modification only of the
micro-environment around the young plant; increased attention to marginal returns to incremental
fertilization; and management of the improved sward.  Exhaustive testing of alternative species,
particularly legumes, lime coating of inoculated seed, establishment of yield response curves to macro-
and micro-element (particularly molybdenum and sulphur on acid soils) and animal manure application,
increased supplies of single element fertilizer (particularly superphosphate), and improving and
maintaining pasture quality are key actions required to support a more cost-effective pasture development
strategy. With regard to pasture resource management, the establishment of land lease legislation, the
setting and policing of stock carrying capacity, and investment in low cost (electric) fencing would be pre-
requisites to effective management.
146.  The process of forage development must be supported by research. Some possible strategies
require further research before being available for commercial application but many are inmediately
implementable and could become the focus of a restructured extension service. The introduction of
wrapped silage as a means of fodder conservation by contractor service is also a promising avenue.
Plastic wrapped round-bale silage optimizes ensiling conditions and minimizes wastage were only small
daily quantities are needed. Another significant advantage offered by silage production is the capacity
to maintain production almost irrespective of weather conditions, thus allowing timely harvest particularly
with respect to fodder quality (digestibility and protein content).
147.  On-farm Technology Testing (OFTT) and field demonstration and the monitoring of management
systems adopted by leading farmers would be required to support the research program. Minimum tillage
is likely to play a big role in forage development through cost reductions and opportunities for relay
cropping resulting from more timely field operations. Unravelling the complexities of the practical
application of reduced-tillage farming systems would require tenacious research effort, and, as with the
pasture research program, much of it must be conducted on private farm holdings under practical
management conditions.
C. DETAILED DESIGN FEATURES
148.  The development of a grass-fed livestock industry would be supported through the implementation
of an applied and adaptive pasture development and research program, On-Farm Technology Testing
(OFTT), and demonstrations. The existing upland pasture research station at Sljeme 35 km from Zagreb
on Zagreb Mountain would be strengthened and research capability for lowland pasture and fodder crops
would be enhanced. Scientific staff skills would be strengthened, primarily through post-graduate
training, regional study tours and improved linkage with the FAO European Research Cooperative
Network on Pastures and Fodder Crops. Extension staff experience would be developed through
participation in the implementation of the OFTT and demonstration program, backed by in-service
training.
149.  The pasture development program would commence with a detailed assessment of the national
pasture resource by agro-ecological zone. The study would detail ownership, distribution and use patterns
of pastures, species mix and productivity, and agro-climatic and soil conditions. It would include the
preparation of a bibliography of published and unpublished scientific literature on pasture and fodder
production in Croatia. This part of the program would be implemented under contract by the Department
of Forage and Grassland Production of the Zagreb Faculty of Agriculture. When completed, the report
would form the basis for the development of a national strategy for the integration of pastures and forages



Project Implementation Plan                     - 85 -                                     ANNEX A
into livestock farming systems. Two person-weeks of technical assistance would be provided to support
this work. In addition, the expertise of a livestock/pasture economist would be provided to give the future
pasture and fodder development program a stronger economic focus.
150.  The project would support an applied and adaptive pasture and fodder research program, initially
in conditions relative to the mountain/upland agro-ecological zones. The research program would include
an OFTT program (where much of the field equipment listed in Table 1 would be deployed), implemented
in collaboration with the extension service and other regional research stations. The research program
would commence in Year 1 in the upland and continental areas, and Year 2 in the mediterranean area at
the Agricultural Research Institute at Split. The Institute would implement the adaptive research program
under the supervision of the Department of Forage and Grassland Production.
151.  The Zagreb University upland pasture research farm at Sljeme would be upgraded for use as a
training center in pasture management and livestock production. With only six scientists currently
actively working on pasture and fodder production, there is an urgent need to develop a cadre of younger
scientists to lead future research and development of this grossly under-utilized resource. The project
would finance three postgraduate programs in subjects including but not limited to species adaptation,
fertilizer response, fodder crop production, pasture/soil management, livestock production from pasture,
and pasture pests and disease. Students would receive external postgraduate training but would do
associated research work in Croatia. Supervisors of students would visit Croatia to support field research
work on a technical assistance (per diem and travel only) basis for a total of eight person-weeks.
Zupanije extension staff would receive training in pasture and fodder management and subsequently
participate in regular in-service training programs in pasture management. Pasture management extension
programs would be developed and promoted through Zupanija Extension Service Centers. Funds for on-
farm pasture management demonstrations would also be provided under the project.
152.  Finance would also be provided to strengthen the fodder corn and pasture legume breeding and
species adaptation work being conducted by the Agricultural Faculty of Zagreb University. Specifically
the project would finance a limited scope of field equipment to support an expanded variety trials
program, plot-scale seed processing equipment, one field vehicle, a desktop computer and the cost of
incremental laboratory services.
153.  The program would be closely linked to the work of the FAO European Research Cooperative
Network on Pastures and Fodder Crops (ERCNPFC). The project would provide funds for the active
participation of Croatian pasture and fodder scientists in ERCNPFC collaborative research programs and
associated international meetings.
154.  The support provided by the project to the University of Zagreb would not entail any additional
staffing. Additional recurrent costs to make full use of the equipment that would be supplied by the
project is estimated at US$24,000 per year for the laboratory equipment and US$12,000 for field work
elements or a total of US$36,000 per year.
D. PASTURE AND FORAGE PROGRAM COSTS
155.  The total cost (including contingencies) of the Pasture and Forage Development Prograin amounts
to US$2.3 million. A detailed cost breakdown is given in Annex D, Table 2.4.



ANNEX A                                        - 86 -                       Project Implementation Plan
E. BENEFITS AND BENEFICIARIES
156.  The expected benefits from pasture and fodder development include:
(i) increased productivity and cost effectiveness of extensive sheep farming from low-cost
upland pasture improvement,
(ii) increased (possibly doubled) dry matter production of upland pastures to yield energy for
ruminants at about 20 per cent of the cost of concentrates and a potential to produce an
additional 0.4 million tonnes of milk or 0.1 million tonnes of beef;
(iii) high productivity at significantly reduced cost from conservation of high quality roughage
resulting in potential milk yields of 4,000-4,500 litres per lactation and live weight gains
of 1 kg per day on rations based on 60 per cent maize silage and 40 per cent leguminous
forage; and,
(iv) strengthened livestock-crop integration.
157.  The beneficiaries would be the farmers in both upland permanent grasslands and in lowland mixed
farms. It is expected that the low cost systems proposed would also favor young people entering into
agricultural production.
F. COMPONENT MANAGEMENT
158.  The pasture and fodder development program and the postgraduate training program would be
coordinated by the Department of Fodder and Grassland Production, Zagreb University, which would
take full responsibility for management of the applied research program in coordination with three
regional research institutions (Porice, Split and Osijek). The Department of Fodder and Grassland
Production would also be responsible for developing the technical specifications of the field and
laboratory equipment to be procured under the project (see Table 1).
159.  The OFTT and field demonstration program would be coordinated nationally by the Department
for Forage and Grassland Production. Zupanije Extension Service Centers would implement the
demonstration program in collaboration with interested farmers and use the trials and demonstrations as
a focal point for field days and farmer group visits. Technical advisors employed by livestock product
processors and input suppliers would be encouraged to participate actively in this program.
G. IMPLEMENTATION
160.  The program would be implemented over four years. The grasslands inventory would be
completed and the report written up and the bibliography produced by the end of the first year of the
project.
161.  The civil works related to the new infrastructure and repair of the existing facilities of the Sljeme
Pasture Research Station would be initiated at the beginning of the first year of the project and be
completed and operational by end-year two.



Project Implementation Plan                     - 87 -                                      ANNEX A
162.  Training of extension workers would be spread evenly over the life of the project. The three
postgraduate studies would be awarded in first two project years.

Project Implementation Plan                    - 89 -                                      ANNEX A
VII. COMPONENT DESCRIPTION FOR SEED INDUSTRY
DEVELOPMENT
A. OBJECTIVE
163.  The objective of this component is to improve the regulatory and administrative framework of
the Croatian seed and plant genetic subsector, with a view to improving domestic plant genetic resources
and increasing the industry's export market access and competitiveness.
B. STRATEGIES
164.  To overcome identified constraints (see Annex L, Supplemental Volume) and to improve the
efficiency and credibility of the regulatory system as a whole, the project would (i) establish a unified
seed industry quality control authority, supported by an adequate analytical capability, regulatory
framework and enforcement capability; (ii) update and expand plant variety testing and registration
procedures; (iii) establish conditions enabling Croatian membership of international seed organizations
and (iv) re-establish the national gene bank.
C. DETAILED PROPOSALS
NATIONAL SEED AUTHORITY
165.  The project would establish the National Seed Authority (NSA) as an agency separate from but
answerable to the Ministry of Agriculture and Forestry (see Figure 1). The purpose of the new Authority
would be to implement all regulatory and testing functions in plant agriculture as required under Croatian
law in an impartial manner and to report the results to the Minister of Agriculture. The Authority would
be supervised by a Board consisting of about 9 people, from academia, industry, and the MAF. The
Authority will combine, under a single management structure, the functions and staff and physical
resources of the MAF plant testing and border quarantine services, the Authority of Plant Protection, the
Viticulture Institute and parts of the MAF Forestry Department. The Authority is expected to employ
about 130 people, half of whom would be employed in border quarantine programs. While significant
staff savings are projected from the coalescence of these organizations, about 25 incremental staff will
be required to manage the VCU3 and DUS4 varietal testing program which will be moved from a
contracted to an Authority implemented program. The NSA would, in time, become entirely self
sustaining through service fees, as promulgated in the new draft Seed Law legislation. The NSA would
include, central laboratory, computing, statistical, research and financial and administrative services
supporting three Divisions, split on functional lines and responsible respectively for (1) Varietal Trials
(VCU and DUS Trials Services), (2) Plant Material Certification (Field Inspection, Quality Testing and
Certification Services), and (3) Plant Health (Border Quarantine, Integrated Pest Management and Agro-
chemical Registration Services). Detailed descriptions of the laboratory, office and field equipment
required to establish the NSA are contained part F (Field Trial Budget) of this chapter.
3/ Official varietal performance testing (Value for Cultivation and Use).
4/ Official varietal identification testing (Distinctness, Uniformity, Stability).



ANNEX A                                           - 90 -                         Project Implementation Plan
166.  NSA Central Services. The laboratory staff and equipment of the Plant Protection Institute would
be transferred to the NSA and supported with additional equipment and one bacteriologist. Equally, the
NSA Statistical Services, Financial Services and Administrative Services Units would comprise staff
transferred from the Plant Protection Institute. The Research Unit would employ a single Research
Coordinator responsible for channeling spare scientific capacity within the NSA toward competitive
research contracts with the new Agricultural Research Council. The National Variety Listing Committee
would comprise appointed experts under (i) the Chair-personship of MAF's Deputy Minister for
Agriculture Production; (ii) with the Director NSA a permanent Committee member; and (iii) the
Director, MAF Agriculture Department, the Committee secretary. The Committee would meet on an "as
needs" basis.
167.  The Plant Testing Division, would evaluate varieties for inclusion in a restructured National List.
This would ensure that the National List gives farmer's an impartial assessment of those varieties which
are reasonable to grow. This is different and separate from extension trials and demonstration; the
purpose of the former is to give an estimate of the interaction between varieties. A variety would have
to be distinct, uniform, stable and have adequate performance before it can be placed on the national list.
The Division would consist of two Services with testing commencing with the agricultural crops and
introducing horticultural crops in year three or four. The responsibilities of the units would be:
(i)    Performance Testing Service (VCU, value for cultivation and use);
Variety Performance Trials. The objective of this Service would be to establish
repeatable high quality trials which adequately discriminate between varieties and are run
by unbiased, well qualified NSA staff. These would be carried out from three centers,
a main center (Zagreb), a sub-center (central east) and a Mediterranean sub-center in
Istria, the latter to be established once the two continental climate centers are operational.
Each center would manage a series of satellite stations. Each center would be staffed by
a qualified trials manager, a pathologist, 2-3 technicians and a tractor driver. Total staff
would not exceed 6 persons. Casual labor would be employed as required. Land would
be rented from farmers on an annual basis and trials laid out on each location. The trials
locations would be serviced from each center on a day to day basis. Species would be
grouped wherever possible and the travel distance kept to a minimum. This may require
some relocation of the currently used sites. Yield and agronomic data would be collected
in an organized and timely manner, from replicated trials following internationally
accepted designs, under the control of one person (the center manager). Field trial
protocols (reflecting local growing practices) would be written by the trials working
group for each species and the center manager would be responsible to ensure they are
carried out.
(ii)    Variety Identification Service (distinctness uniformity and stability, DUS);
Plant Variety Discrimination. This Service would carry out trials under UPOV5 guidelines
in such a manner as to allow the discrimination between varieties for Plant Variety Rights
purposes. This function would be new to the trials Authority. One field center would be
required for this morphological work6, provided irrigation was available and that a
sample was kept to allow replanting as required. One team of four people would be
established at the location (Zagreb) to do this work. As well as expert technician staff
5/ International Union for the Protection of New Plant Varieties.
6/ It is suggested that the internationally accepted guidelines are used wherever possible for variety discrimination.



Project Implementation Plan                          - 91 -                                           ANNEX A
they will also need access to good quality reliable casual labor. This unit would be
supported by the centralized NSA statistical and laboratory service groups. Introducing
the full range of species into this overall testing regime would require attention to detail,
careful timing and organization because: (i) the appropriate regulations would have to be
in place; (ii) a period of change-over would be required where both VCU systems (old
and new) are running in parallel; and (iii) the DUS system would be introduced for
several species and there is little or no experience of such work in the country.
168.   The introduction of Plant Variety Rights (PVR) would mean that a sample of the proposed seed
crop would need to be verified as authentic. It is proposed that when a variety is registered for seed
production that a sample (drawn by a licensed sampler) be sent to the Seed Testing Center. They would
grow it in small plots for detailed maturity and off-type analysis (thus avoiding the detail of checking
large fields) and control along with the DUS group the distinctness of the variety, initially by
morphological means but eventually with electrophoresis. UPOV proposes to allow the introduction of
electrophoresis as a diagnostic tool in 1995. It is proposed to introduce maize to the new system first,
because it is the crop from which the greatest genetic/agricultural improvement could be expected. This
would be followed by winter wheat the next year and the balance of the cereals and other miscellaneous
crops the year after. However, the spring planting season for maize was missed. This phasing is
important because it would be unlikely that the proposed change-over would occur without constraints.
An element of security would be achieved by running the old and new systems in parallel for a year. The
old system would then be progressively phased out as the new one is phased in. The overlapping year
serves two purposes (i) it ensures data is recorded from both systems (ii) it ensures a lot of data is
recorded, thus allowing a good estimate of variety value to be achieved to allow a significant rejection
of poor varieties from the National List.
169.   The reference collection, i.e. those varieties already on the national list, would need to be
established before any additional varieties are added, because, it is against these that all new variety
distinctness characteristics must be quantified for DUS purposes. The reason for this is that varieties
must be distinct before they receive PVR. Maintaining a large reference collection is expensive,
particularly so when many mediocre varieties are listed and not in everyday trade. The reduction in the
reference collection would be achieved by: (i) introducing a fee to keep varieties on the national list; (ii)
re-evaluating variety VCU and only listing the top 25 percent7.
170.   The Seed Certification Division, would be fully responsible for the monitoring of production
quality and health in the field and processing centers and for issuing of all plant material related
certificates. Quality control for the bulk of the harvested seed is presently checked by seed trade
laboratories, usually at their processing plants. This is a very satisfactory system and should be
maintained. Again, the NSA seed quality control laboratory (NSA central services) would be the final
arbiter on all questions of seed quality and health. This laboratory would train and license seed analysts
and monitor licensed seed trade laboratories. The Division would be totally responsible for controlling
the issue of licenses and the maintenance of standards. Individuals would pay to attend the training
courses and for the license, thus the training element should be self-financing. The Center would include
three Services
(i)    Field Inspection Service
State Inspectors would inspect all seed at elite and super elite levels growing under
breeder control. Private inspectors, licensed by the State, would inspect all commercial
7/ It is unlikely that a list of more than six varieties within any species and/or maturity group is of any real value for use in
Croatia. Specific varieties may require PVR for export purposes.



ANNEX A                                          - 92 -                        Project Implementation Plan
seed, unless it was for export, in which case a State inspector would be responsible. This
would mean that the seed trade, using licensed inspectors, would control the quality of
commercial seed. Because the quality of the starting seed would be much improved over
that which is available now, there should be fewer problems. The Service would arrange
the rotation for the day to day inspections thus avoiding people controlling their own
crops.
(ii)    Certification Service
All field, seed, plant and agro-chemical inspection reports would be sent to the Service
via a computer network only accessible by authorized personnel at each testing/data entry
point. If satisfied with test reports, the Division would give an inspection certificate, thus
allowing harvesting or sale of the relevant product. In the case of seed inspection, control
would be maintained by the Certification Division by only issuing licenses to people who
have satisfactorily attended specific NSA implemented training sessions for the
appropriate crops and who manifestly do a good job in the field. The use of random
inspections by the Field Inspection Service inspectors should adequately regulate the
system. Incompetent inspectors would lose their license.
(iii)   Plant Product Quality Service
The NSA Certification Division would also include a Plant Product Quality Service
responsible for testing plant products against specific standards and approving the
issuance of quality certificates. It is expected that NSA will register its own quality
certification label, the use of which would generate royalty income. However, this
Service will only be established in project year 3 and then only following the successful
establishment of the Field Inspection and the Certification Services and associated
computer network.
171.  The Plant Health Division, would be responsible for the management of the national Plant
Quarantine Service, a Integrated Pest Management Service and the Agro-chemical Registration Service.
(i)    Quarantine Service
This Service, already established under MAF management would be transferred to NSA
management in the second year of the project. No significant investment is proposed for
the relocated Service. Laboratory requirements would be met through the NSA central
laboratory where a bacteriologist would be added to already employed staff to provide
a comprehensive plant health analytical service.
(ii)   Integrated Pest Management (1PM) Service
Presently located within the Plant Protection Institute, the IPM service has been
marginalized in recent years. The project would finance two incremental staff and field
monitoring equipment to bolster farmer access to reliable pest risk information. The IPM
Service would initially focus on a few critical crop pests.
(iii)   Agro-chemical Registration Service
The existing agro-chemical registration service would be transferred to the NSA where
it would be supported by a more comprehensive laboratory service. Agro-chemical
registration requirements would be brought into line with European Union norms and the



Project Implementation Plan                           - 93 -                                            ANNEX A
certification process channeled through the NSA's "single window" Certification
Division.
172.   The NSA would join the International Seed Testing Authority (ISTA) as soon as possible. A fee8
would be charged for inspections made by the Certification Center personnel for super elite, elite and all
export seed. Samples checked by the NSA laboratory would also command a fee, unless they were part
of a random control procedure. Efforts must be made to improve the quality of seed production and to
teach quality control as a management input.
173.   In addition to establishing the NSA, the project would finance the following technical assistance
and training inputs in support of the establishment of the NSA and the development of new testing and
regulatory procedures.

D. COMPENENT COSTS
175.   The total cost (including contingencies) of the Seed Industry Development Program is estimated
at US$3.8 million. Detailed cost breakdown is presented in Annex B, Table 2.5.
E. BENEFITS AND BENEFICIARIES
176.   The major benefits from the reorganization of the plant industry regulatory and quality control
services would include:
(i)  rapid introduction of a range of modem varieties (protected by PVR) into
Croatian agriculture;
(ii)  the improvement of seed quality to the farmer, this would ultimately allow
a reduction of 20-30 percent in the sowing rate;
(iii) an increase in farm outputs. The program should add 10 percent to the
national production in maize;
(iv)  a reduction in seed prices through increased competition;
(v)  an improvement in the organization, administration and cost effectiveness of
the regulatory and quality control network, with consequent improved access
to export markets.
177.   The main beneficiaries would be:
(i)  farmer would benefit the most through improved income from reduced seed use
and higher yielding, more efficient varieties;



Project Implementation Plan                         - 95 -                                           ANNEX A
(ii) MAF, as seed testing and quarantine would be removed from their direct
responsibility.
(iii) companies producing seeds and plant genetic materials which would have a higher
quality product to market domestically and more assured access to international
markets.
F. TRIAL BUDGET ESTIMATES
178.   The restructured variety trials program  would establish a system  which will allow  the
discriminative evaluation of varieties submitted for official testing, to establish and control their plant
variety rights, and register approved varieties on the national list as required. Species groups will require
specific machinery for either sowing or harvesting. It is anticipated that the sugar beet and potato trials
will be harvested by hand, with potato also hand planted.

satellites) with the FAO 400 and FAO 500 maize varieties are at six locations that each variety x location
has 5 replications. Assuming about 600 varieties on trial in the project period, this will require about
10,000 plots or about 3,500 plots per center. With an average plot size of 7 sq.m. (based on 6m centers),
the land required at each center for trials will be 2.5 ha. Allowance for borders, discards and roadways
