B. SPEcIc
Istria Mixed Farm Model
23.      This model shows a gross incremental annual production value of US$2,740 on a without project
base of US$7,600. The model differs from others in that the increment is mainly achieved through a
change in cropping pattern to focus on more profitable crops, lucerne and potatoes. The net incremental
family benefit of US$1,350 is added to a very low without project base of US$1,460 per year. This is
a realistic description of the without project situation in Istria where many families live on part-time,
casual work in the tourist industry. The net return in US$ per hour of family labor rises from 1 to 2 with
project. No incremental livestock returns are budgeted. Alternative Istrian livestock models could be
developed, provided milk and meat markets are established.
24.      Incremental cash production costs are US$1,360 per year and this would have to be financed in
the first year by off-farm income or phased in over several years. The incremental cash needs are
equivalent to about 3 months employment in the hotel industry for one family member.
25.      The Net Present Value (NPV) before financing at 10 per cent is US$8,400. Switching value
analysis shows that incremental revenues cold be halved or operating costs rise by 101 per cent before
NPV declined to zero. This suggests that the model is robust and capable of absorbing large increases
in costs or medium to large falls in revenue, before returns fall below 10 per cent.
26.      More detailed data on this model is available in Farmod Tables 1-3-1 and 1-5-1 in Annex N  and
on diskette.
Dalmatia Vegetable Farm Model
27.      The improved production practices of this model increase gross value of production by US$3,410
on the very high annual base of US$34,100 without project. Some US$2,910 of the increment is from
vegetable production, with the balance from field crops. After incremental production costs and hired
labor are paid, the net family benefits increase by US$2,100 per year with project, above a base of
US$27,800. This is a small proportional increase but in practice larger increases rely on investment of



Project Implementation Plan                      - 147 -                                       ANNEX E
the type envisaged in the proposed Rural Finance Pilot Project,especially improved irrigation and
additional land. There are good markets available for the range of vegetables in the model, at the times
they are harvested, and farmers are beginning to consolidate shipments. There is a project role in market
infornation, extension support for scheduling plantings to ensure marketable quantities coincide and for
technical advice on crop protection/IPM in particular.
28.   Returns to family labor are high at US$13 per hour both without and with project. This is three
times higher than in the next most remunerative model and this income is reflected in the living standard
of the Dalmatian farmers who have already achieved the with project situation.
29.   Annual incremental cash costs are US$1,280 which is easily within the capacity of a farmer in
the without project situation to fund from retained earnings.
30.   The NPV before financing at 10 per cent (10 years) is US$13,100. Switching values show that
incremental inflows could fall by 63 per cent or incremental operating costs rise by 167 per cent before
the NPV declines to zero. Changes of that size are considered most unlikely, provided proper attention
is paid to marketing.
Lika Mixed Farm Model
31.   Overall net farm family benefits increase from US$3,590 per year without project to US$6,650
with project. This substantial increase is mainly achieved through increased livestock, especially cheese,
production. Cheese making investments are negligible and the process adds about US$870 net per year
to the value of milk otherwise sold fresh. It is appropriate in a region where milk collection costs are
high. The increment in family benefit is proportionally large in this model, as is the required increase
in labor input. Average returns to family labor increase from US$2 to US$3.8 per hour with project.
32.   Additional cash operating costs are US$2,615 per year with project. This is a substantial sum for
a family with a without project cash flow of US$3,580 per year. However milk and cheese returns will
be received monthly (compared to annually for most crops) and this greatly reduces financing needs. The
additional with project cow will be obtained by retention of a calf, with a negative impact of US$395 on
cash flow in the year of retention.
33.   The NPV for net incremental family benefits is US$840. This is the largest of any model and
reflects strong demand for livestock products and the technical opportunities available. Switching values
suggest that incremental revenues can fall by 53 per cent or added operating costs rise by 114 per cent
before NPV is reduced to zero. Such changes are most unlikely in this case.
SlavonUa Crop Farm Model
34.   Net Incremental family benefits in this model are US$840 per year on a base of US$5,680
without project. About 40 to 50 per cent of crop production is used or consumed on-farm, mainly for
pig produc.ion. The with project model seeks to supply superior pig rations from within the farm and
sacrifices some cash return from lower sugar beet production as a result. Given the low economic value
of local sugar beet production, such a change is likely to be forced in the short term and the financial
sacrifice may be apparent rather than real. Most of the increment in family benefit is earned through
higher pig sales and lower unit feed costs. Family labor returns rise from US$3.5 to US$4.3 per hour
with project.



ANNEX E                                         - 148 -                       Project Implementation Plan
35.   The changes in cropping pattern and pig ration mean that there is no increase in cash outflows
to be financed with project.
36.   The NPV before financing is US5,200 and switching values show that incremental inflows would
have to fall by 107 per cent to reduce NPV to zero. Because incremental operating costs are slightly
negative (i.e. with project operating cost is slightly lower than without project), incremental operating
costs would have to increase from minus US$350 to US$4,850 before the NPV return drops to zero.
West Continental Dairy/Beef Farm Model
37.   Net Incremental farm family benefits are US$1,090 on a without project base of US$4,990 per
year. These benefits are derived from improved cow management, health and feeding and some
incremental forage crop sales. Milk yield per cow rises from 3,500 to 4,025 1 per year and this is readily
marketable in north west Croatia. Return to family labor rises from US3 to US3.5 per hour with project.
38.   Incremental cash production costs are US$790 per year. This may be related to without project
annual production costs of US$3,400. Given that milk payments are made monthly rather than
annually, as with crops, most families should be able to fund the incremental cash needs from their own
resources.
39.   The NPV is US$6,760 for incremental family benefits and switching values show that a 58 per
cent fall in incremental inflows or a 140 per cent increase in operating costs would be needed to reduce
NPV to zero. The dairy model is robust but small in scale and could profitably be more than doubled in
size if investment funds were available.
IV. FARM MODEL ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
A. GENERAL
40.   Annex M presents estimates of economic prices (in terms of nominal protection coefficient -
NPC) for key commodities and inputs: wheat, maize, soybeans, sugar beet, milk and fertilizer. The
reciprocals of the NPC's of Annex M have been used as conversion factors in FARMOD prices to
calculate economic prices for the above items. In all other cases (outputs and inputs) the conversion
factor 1.0 has been used, equating financial and economic prices. For these models the other main
incremental products(not listed above) are meat and vegetables. Croatia currently engages in only small
amounts of international trade in meat and vegetables and there are no major subsidies in place to support
their production. In this situation it seems reasonable to accept a NPC of 1.0. Incremental labor has also
been valued at market price in the economic analysis although it is likely that economic labor value may
be lower in some locations. The five models show a similar distribution of annual economic benefit to
that existing in financial terms: three models are similar in size of without project economic benefit
(-US$1,800 per year) , the Dalmatia Vegetable Model yields benefits more than ten times larger
(US$24,000) and the Istrian model yields a small negative return (-US$190)without project.
41.   All models show positive increments in net economic value of production with project and have
positive economic Net Production Values (NPVs), usually a little lower than the corresponding financial
NPV, at 1995 prices and NPC's. The analysis was for a 10 year period and the discount rate was set
at 10 per cent. As changes in policy are implemented it is likely that production shifts will occur to
further increase the economic value of incremental production. The Slavonija Crop Farm Model is an



Project Implementation Plan                       - 149 -                                        ANNEX E
existing example of a situation in which expected poor economic performance is improved by much of
the incremental production being in the form of pig meat produced by feeding low value grains and by
a reduction in the with project area of the most heavily subsidized crop (i.e., sugarbeet).
Table E.2: Summary of Indicative Economic Analysis
(US$ per Farm)
Net Value of Production (Annual)
Model                                                                            Implicit
Without        With       Incremental   NPV (at 10 %    Fin./Econ.
Project       Project       Value      over 10 years)    NPC (%)
1. Istria Mixed Farm      -190           1,100        1,290         8,100          104
2. Dalmatia Vegetable     24,420        26.350        1,930         12,070         109
3. Lika Mixed Farm         1510          3,870        2,360         14,700         124
4. Slavonija Crop Farm    2,100         3,390         1,290         8,050          65
5. Dairy/Beef Farm        1,990         2,960          970          6,100          111
42.    Simple comparisons between financial and economic increments (such as those in Table E.2) can
be misleading, if used as proxy NPC's. First incremental family labor costs have been deducted by
FARMOD in calculating economic increments but not financial increments. There are big differences
between models in incremental family labor.
B. SPECIFIC
Istria Mixed Farm Model
43.    The Istrian model has a negative economic and low positive financial annual return in the
without project situation. This assessment is supported by the existence of abandoned farms in Istria.
However both the model and farm visits clearly show that economic and financial profitability is feasible,
provided production is focused on higher return crops or livestock activities, especially those related to
the tourist market. This model takes a conservative approach (in that further crop changes towards high
value production could be managed and livestock improvement also included, but these opportunities have
been ignored with project) and shows incremental annual economic benefits of US$1,290 with project
and a NPV of US$8,100 for net economic benefits at 10 per cent over 10 years. The economic and
financial NPV's are similar as most of the incremental production comprises potatoes and forage which
have identical financial and economic prices in the model. Table 1-4-1 in Annex N presents more detailed
data.
Dalmatia Vegetable Farm Model
44.    The Dalmatia model also shows similar economic and financial returns as incremental production
is confined to vegetables with a price conversion factor of 1.0 in the model. The net economic increment
of US$1,930 per year results in a NPV of US$12,070, after incremental family labor of US$280 per



ANNEX E                                        - 150 -                        Project Implementation Plan
year is deducted. This increment is small relative to the without project net economic return of
US$24,420.
Lika Mixed Farm Model
45.   This model yields an economic increment of US$2,360 on a base of US$1,510 without project.
The NPV is US$14,700 the highest of any model. This performance relies in part on the sale of specialty
cheese at full market price with project, compared to the sale of milk (NPC 1.25) without project. The
economic increment is estimated after the deduction of incremental family labor (US$765); the financial
increment is presented as a return to labor. If the financial and economic increments are compared on
a consistent labor basis, they are similar in this model.
Slavonija Crop Model
46.Interpretation of the economic analysis of this model is complex. The economic increment of
US$1,290 exceeds the financial increment of US$840. The increment is earned on a base economic
return of US$2,100 per farm per year. Sugar beet has a large economic loss per ha and the effect of
reducing sugar beet area in the with project situation is to reduce farm returns by US$190 in financial
prices and increase them by US$180 in economic prices, a net difference of US$370 per farm in favor
of the economic increment. Additionally the main production increment is pig meat which has identical
financial and economic prices in this model. The NPV is US$8,050.
West Continental Dairy/Beef Farm Model
47.   The NPV for economic increments in this model is US$6,100. This is based on an annual
increment of US$970 above the without project level of US$1,990. This is slightly below the financial
increment as milk has an NPC of 1.26, although incremental forage sales have equal financial and
economic value in the model. The net economic effect of other changes in the with project case is small.
V. FARM ANALYSIS: CONCLUSION
48.   Financial analysis of the five models indicates that:
* project activities are likely to yield sufficient rmancial benefit to farmers, of the kinds
modelled, to encourage them to participate in or actively use better extension, animal health
services, seeds and market information. Annual financial increments range from a low of 8 per
cent of the high base earnings in Dalmatia to a high of 92 per cent of the low Istrian base.
* in most of the models farmers clearly have the ability to fund incremental short term inputs
from retained earnings, monthly milk payments or pig sales. In the Istrian model it may be
necessary for farmers to fund the added inputs from off-farm work in the tourist industry or by
phasing changes over several years.
* switching values indicate that the models are robust, requiring an increases of more than 100
per cent in operating costs or a fall of more than 50 per cent in incremental inflows to reduce
NPV to zero at 10 percent.



Project Implementation Plan                      - 151 -                                       ANNEX E
* financial benefits may be earned through changes in cropping pattern or livestock
processing/marketing as well as increased technical efficiency.
* there is a substantial surplus of farm labor available on the modelled farms.
* farmers have an ability to pay for useful services, especially where the scale of operation is
large enough to cover overheads and basic living costs or where high value vegetable, cheese
or similar products are involved.
49.    Economic analysis concludes that positive incremental returns are earned in each model, given
the NPC's estimated in Annex M. Even in Slavonija where several common crops have higher NPC's,
the usual practice on private farms is to feed these crops to livestock with strong positive economic and
financial results.
VI. PROJECT ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
A. INTRODUCTION
50.   Based on the results of the farm model analysis previously discussed, the following economic
analysis attempts to quantify the project net incremental economic returns. Quantifying the economic
returns of the vast range of project activities including agriculture extension, applied research, animal
health, pasture and fodder development, seed industry development, and agriculture policy support is
notoriously difficult, given the broad range of non-service factors influencing decision making at the farm
level, and uncertainties associated with the translation of improved farm services into economic farn
benefits. The purpose of estimating project returns, therefore, is to verify that the project is broadly
economically viable and robust to changes in project cost and benefits. The following analysis is based
on a number of conservative assumptions discussed below. Economic flows are calculated over a ten year
time horizon, albeit benefits are expected to accrue well beyond the tenth year.
B. PROJECT INCREMENTAL BENEFITS
51.   Project benefits are based only on economic farm returns as identified under the indicative farm
models described above. Many other potential benefits occurring outside the farmn have not been
quantified such as export losses avoided through improved animal disease control, improved export
earnings by the seed subsector in the context of an improved regulatory framework, the possible positive
fiscal impact of a more rational agriculture policy, or improved environmental sustainability through
better extension and research services. Project benefits also exclude the potential positive impact of
improved farm services on the transition of the agro-kombinat sector. Hence, economic returns to this
project may be somewhat underestimated.
52.   In order to obtain economic benefits on a ha basis, the net annual incremental value of production
(in economic terms) of the individual model farms is divided by the area under cultivation by the
respective farm (see Table E.3, columns 1 to 4). The economic benefits on a ha basis, representing the
major farming areas in Croatia are weighted by the share of the respective farming area in overall
agriculture area in the country, resulting in a weighted average net incremental value of production per
ha of arable land, i.e US$385 ha/year (Table E.3, columns 5 and 6). To obtain the potential annual
incremental value of production of private agriculture making full use of project services, this figure is



ANNEX E                                                 - 152 -                            Project Implementation Plan
multiplied by the area of arable land privately farmed (i.e. roughly 1.6 million ha). The actual project
benefits are, however, likely to be realized only by a much smaller share of the private farming
community; in the analysis it is conservatively assumed that only 5 percent of farmers (i.e. some 25,000
out of 500,000 farmers) would reap the full benefit of the project. Total annual project farm benefits are
estimated to be in the order of US$31 million (Table E.3, columns 7 and 8). As agriculture service-
related benefits require time to materialize, it is, again conservatively, estimated that the mentioned
annual benefit stream sets in only in Year 6 of the calculation, i.e. in the second year after project
implementation; no benefits are assumed to accrue during implementation and during the first year after
project implementation (see Table E.4).

E. SENsrnviTY ANALYSIS
55.    In order to test the sensitivity of project returns to variations in costs and benefits, major

robust to significant increases in costs and decreases benefits (20 percent respectively). A 20 percent
decrease in benefits could occur from a reduced number of farmers being reached by the various support
services (i.e. 20,000 farmers rather than 25,000 farmers in the base case). Only when cost increase and
benefits decrease by 20 percent and benefits lag by one year does the project become economically
unviable. The likelihood of the three events occurring jointly is considered low.

REPUBLIC OF CROATIA
FARMERS SUPPORT SERVICES PROJECT
ANNEX F
PROJECT ENVIRONMENTAL ANALYSIS
I. INTRODUCTION AND SUMMARY
1.        According to World Bank rules, an Environmental Analysis (EA) is required prior to the
implementation of the "Farmer Support Services Project" (FSSP). The Ministry of Agriculture and
Forestry (MAF) of the Republic of Croatia assigned this task to an expert team competent in EA
questions, consisting of Mr. Ferdo Basic Ph.D., Mr. Zoran Griic Ph.D. - Faculty of Agronomy,
University of Zagreb, and Ms. Visnja Jelic-Muck B.Sc., State Directorate for Environment Protection.
2.        The team reviewed the project documentation to identify potential positive or negative impacts
of FSSP on environment. The EA task can be summarized into a few objectives:
* To assess the actual environmental situation, before FSSP implementation, and to
identify potential direct and indirect impacts on the environment; and
*  To review the existing legislation as it relates to the use of agrochemicals, fertilizers,
animal feed stuffs and veterinary drugs.
3.       It is important to point out that FSSP is, according to the World Bank regulations, a category
B project which means that it has a limited impact on the environment.
4.        The activities to be implemented under the FSSP have a great importance for the promotion
and recognition of ecologically sustainable management of Croatian national resources.
5.       In implementing this project, no negative environmental impacts are expected. It is realistic
to expect that the effects of some project components will strengthen the notion of sustainable agriculture
which combines technologies, policies and activities aimed at integrating socioeconomic principles with
environmental concerns so as to simultaneously: maintain or enhance production, minimize risk of
diminished food production, protect natural resources and prevent degradation of soil and water quality,
all with a view to being economically viable and socially acceptable.
6.        This EA does not propose a specific mitigation plan (nor does it cost mitigation efforts)
because no direct negative environmental impacts are anticipated in the context of implementing this
project. However, as a crucial mitigation activity during the first year of project implementation, MAF
would develop special ('best practice') guidelines for its extension service and the farming community
in support of the concept and practices of sustainable agriculture and appropriate handling of agriculture
chemicals.



* -.A  F                                        - 156 -                       Project Implementation Plan
A major contribution to the development of these best practice guidelines can be expected
rern the findings of the on-going project on 'Proper Handling of Pesticides and their Residues in
.4gr^iculture Practice" managed by the Agency for Special and Dangerous Waste.
In addition, MAF will organize an  "International Workshop on Water Pollution and
Protection in Agriculture Practices" in Zagreb, in October, 1995. This workshop will be organized in the
framework of the new international project on "Pollution of Ground and Surface Water by Nitrates,
Heavy Metals and Pesticides through Percolation from Agriculture Areas" which has been suggested by
Croatia and accepted by the Third Session of the FAO/ECE Working Party on Relations between
Agriculture and Environmnent, held in Geneva in 1994.
9.        In presenting the Environmental Analysis, we want to emphasize the suggestions made by the
Deputy Ministers, Ministry of Agriculture and Forestry, Mr. Vlado Bicanic and Dr. Stjepan Tanic. We
take this opportunity to express our appreciation for their cooperation.
[l, EXISTING STATE OF ENVIRONMENT, REGIONAL STRUCTURE AND OWNERSHIP
0.       The heterogeneity of agro-ecological conditions in Croatia suggests regional differences in
structure of agriculture, level and direction of its development, and, accordingly, the state of the
environment. Project implementation will also affect the various agro-ecological environments in
different ways.
11.      The major impact of agriculture in general on the environment is on agricultural land and
water resources. That is why the review of the environment situation is based on agricultural regions.
Tlhere are pedological characteristics, geo-morphological (soil type) and geo-lithological (climate) features,
all contributing to a characterization essential for crop and livestock production.
I2        No reliable national database exists which would assist project designers, and implementing
agencies in assessing the extent and importance of issues such as risky soil management practices, narrow
crop rotation, soil erosion, persistent herbicide use, and the intensity of agriculture inputs, given the
complete lack of systematic research in these and related fields. Therefore, the statements made below,
though important, should be viewed more as a descriptive assessment without a systematic empirical
basis. These judgments are inferred from occasional and isolated small-scale research work undertaken
in the past which should be seen as descriptive indicators rather than quantification of a prevailing
situation in a given agricultural region.
A. ENVIRONMENTAL CONDITIONS IN AGRICULTURAL REGIONS OF CROATIA
:. iAgricultural area in Croatia is composed of three regions, and smaller agro-ecological
subregions (See Figure F. 1)
Pannonian Region
14.       This region comprises the largest share (54.2%) of Croatian territory, and it is the most
Xvaluable agricultural region in the state. Known as the Pannonian plane, the region can be distinguished
into four subregions, with corresponding particularities.



Project Implementation Plan                             - 157 -                                              AN NEX 
AGRICULTURAL REGIONS
OF THE REPUBLIC OF CROATIA

PI - Eastern Panonian Subregion
15.        This subregion is distinctly crop area in nature. Dominant crops are wheat, maize, sugar
beet, soybean and sunflower in the plains, and exceptionally valuable vineyard plantations on the hilly
areas. In this subregion, the largest concentration of production lies in the ex-social sector, where highly
intensive agricultural production has been applied, especially with respect to crop production (less so for
livestock production) and predominantly in the form of semi-liquid manure. For more than five decadec..
farmyard manure has not been used. There has been a process of anthropogenic soil degradation arisinP
from the intensive farming primarily on ex-social sector farms resulting in:
*  anthropogene soil compaction;
*  herbicide deposition (especially atrazin), due to narrowed crop rotation, especially v ir0-
respect to maize;
*  decreased humus content; and
*   soil erosion by wind and water
16.        There are also noted occurrence of subsurface water contamination by nitrates and
eutrophication of water flows. It is expected that the average private household acreage will increase as
the ownership structure changes in favor of private ownership.   Increased investments in irriga;io,n
systems in the future which are, however, outside the scope of the activities supported by FSSP ul ill
enable more intensive crop cultivation (especially vegetables) as well as secondary crop cultivation



ANNEX F                                           - 158 -                         Project Implementation Plan
17.       An essentially different and more favorable situation is that of fragmented private farms,
where traditional production is practiced, with high share of livestock production and common use of
farmyard manure. Processes of soil degradation have not been registered, except in cases of wind and
water erosion.
P2 - Central Pannonian Subregion
18.       The production structure in this subregion is dominated by intensive crop farming in the
plains, and on all slopes (hill-sides). Vineyard and livestock production is particularly prevalent in the
south. The ex-social sector is not extensive and fragmented private farms dominate. Crop production
is pervasive with some orchard and vineyard production. In relation to the previous subregion, there is
a growing trend in the use of livestock and farmyard manure. Favorable conditions and a prospective
development exists for vegetable growing, with irrigation.
19.       On soils among the ex-social sector, the processes of anthropogenetic soil degradation is
registered, whereas soil among the private sector is considerably more preserved, owing to a higher
importance of livestock production and integrated farming.   Relief is more complex here, as soil
structure is unstable and more vulnerable to erosion by water, compounded also with a high share of
maize in crop rotation. That is why particular attention should be given to soil conservation.
P3 - Western Pannonian Subregion
20.       In this subregion, private, fragmented households clearly predominate. Livestock production
is developed to the extent that the support from grazing stock is necessary. Consequently, a significant
and untapped potential exits for development of sheep- and goat-breeding. The basic direction for
investment has to be into irrigation and drainage for vegetable growing, and considering that the soil is
mainly acidic, it should be taken into account that liming needs to carried out regularly to correct the
acidic soil. Extraordinarily favorable conditions exist for fruit-and wine growing development as well.
21.       One of the ecologically questionable management practices on soils in this subregion is
narrow crop rotation in favor of maize, which stimulates erosion, and for which increasing herbicide use
is regularly used, contributing to a high persistence in soil, such as in the case of atrazin.
22.       Within this subregion there are also concentrated sources of emission of different ecologically
risky substances, such as industrial plants in Zagreb, ironworks and petroleum refinery in Sisak and food-
processing industries in Vrbovec and Koprivnica.
P4 - Northwestern Pannonian Subregion
23.       Clearly dominated by private and exceptionally fragmented households, this subregion's
arable crop production is oriented towards feed stuff production. An ecologically doubtful management
practice here is the high share of maize production within the crop rotation. This is compounded by high
rainfalls in Spring combined with a period of black fallow soil, and also because crop stands are too thin
to cover and protect the soil.
24.       Soils in this region require conservation measures such as in drainage in the Varazdin region
and irrigation in the Medjimurie region. In addition, the especially valuable use of liming is implemented
regularly and systematically. This area is very densely populated and households are highly fragmented.
The social sector is negligibly small.



Project Implementation Plan                          - 159 -                                           ANNEX F
Mountain Region
25.        This region comprises 14.2%  of the total area, and involves the thinly populated Pery
Mountain and Pery Mountain subregions. Mainly wooded areas with little agricultural land, traditional
and extensive production exists only on private and poor households. Soil is completely preserved from
any form of degradation or damage, except natural erosion. The virgin nature has crystal-clear and
beautiful rivers which are of an exceptional quality for irrigation.
GI - Pery Mountain Subregion
26.        Production capabilities of this area are potentially huge, but totally unused.  Especially
favorable conditions exist for the development of livestock production, indeed for all forms of ecological
agriculture. Almost without exception, soils require liming, and drainage in water-carrying valleys as
well. A possible vegetable production orientation would require irrigation, for which extraordinary
conditions exist here. Potential hydroelectric development among the pristine rivers would open the way
for irrigation.
G2 - Mountain Subregion
27.        This subregion is mountainous, built mostly of Mesozoic limestones and dolomite of high
cleanliness. Silicate sandstone and metamorphic rocks are also present. The subregion is characterized
by a mountain climate, with great amounts of precipitation causing erosion and acidification. A frost-free
period is short, so the selection of crops is narrowed to those resistant to a very unfavorable climate
conditions, like rye and potato. Favorable conditions exist for production of seed-potato which could be
produced in quantities exceeding Croatian needs and merit further attention. Beside investments in
irrigation, there is need to finance agro-technical soil melioration, particularly calcification.
28.        Private ownership is fragmented, with a prevailing orientation towards livestock production,
particularly sheep- and goat-breeding. Given the clear beautiful rivers, the national park of the Plitvice
Lakes located in this area, as well as botanical preserves and natural gardens, conditions are exceptionally
favorable for tourism.
Mediterranean Region
29.        This region comprises 31.6% of Croatia's coastal area and islands, and is quite diverse in soil
and climate characteristics.
MI - Northern Mediterranean Subregion
30.        This subregion involves the northern coastal area and north littoral islands. It differs from
other subregions in that it has a relatively large soil area on a flysh deposit subject to erosion to a serious
degree. Efficient soil protection is a precondition to any agricultural production in this area.
31.        Soils of this subregion are highly productive, given the favorable climatic conditions (warm
temperatures), and a valuable substratum for agricultural production, particularly with regard to
Mediterranean crops such as olives, grapes, almonds, peaches, nectarines, mandarins, and also high
quality vegetables, for which irrigation would, however, be necessary.



ANNEX F                                             - 160 -                         Project Implementation Plan
32.        Private farms are small and fragmented, and use of the high production potentials is minimal.
A basic limiting factor is unfavorable distribution of rainfall and the typical summer droughts. Any
investment here would have to include irrigation for which there is enough water.
M2 - Central Mediterranean Subregion
33.        This region includes the central part of the coast, with continental background and the
belonging islands. It is this area that exemplifies the traditionally developed agriculture such as wine-
growing, fruit-growing, vegetable-and flower-growing both on fields and in glass-houses.
34.        It is expected that investments to ameliorate irrigation systems will take place. The ex-social
sector is relatively highly represented in this area. The typical, geomorphological units, so-called Karst
field, with its deep and productive soil, is extremely drained. These are usually flooded during the
autumn and winter period and in summer period without rain, the water level falls into Karst subsurface.
Because Karst areas are especially sensitive, usage of agro-chemicals has to be extremely rational and of
the mobile variety. Persistent pesticides must be avoided, with regard to quantity and timing. Adversely,
use of persistent pesticides might come to contaminate soil and waters of the area, with serious
consequences for a even wider area. Agriculture production in the wider area of Vransko Lake deserves
particular attention, especially considering the different potential uses of such a valuable water resource.
M3 - Southlern Mediterranean Subregion
35.        Ex-social sector representation is negligible in this subregion. More common are small
privately-owned plots. Common Mediterranean crops are mandarins, olives, figs, but also peaches, and
cherries and all kinds of vegetables and flowers on fields and in sheltered areas (glass-houses). A
c.ommon characteristic of all products from this area is their high quality. Investments are necessary,
inter alia, in irrigation, and special importance should also be given to island revitalization.
36.        Specially valuable and ecologically sensitive units within this subregion is the river and valley
of Neretva, where fertile soil can be found, along with plenty of sunny days, the Mediterranean climate,
and plenty of high-quality water. Skilled and hardworking farmers there practice very intensive growing
of all Meditecranean crops such as mandarins, vine and vegetables. Because of regular usage of large
quantities of various agrochemicals, there are some signs of disturbances in the locally sensitive
ecosystems.
B. ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENT PRACTICES BY OWNERSHIP
37.        Applied cultivation operations in Croatian agriculture have influenced, inter alia, soils and
water. The direction and intensity of that influence is sharply different with regard to farm ownership;
there are negative influences on soil and water in the ex-social sector, while there is little or no negative
influence among the private sector.
38.        Again, due to the absence of reliable data it is not possible to provide a full quantified
assessment within the scope of this EA of the impact of different farm types as well as the impact of the
project on environmental conditions.



Project Implementation Plan                         - 161 -                                          ANNEX F
Ex-social Sector
39.        With regard to soils within the ex-social sector, all intensive agricultural operations have been
carried out, involving usage of large quantities of agrochemicals (mineral fertilizers, pesticides, esp.
herbicides including those of the atrazin group), and processes of anthropogene soil and subsurface water
degradation have been registered. Among these are:
Anthropogenic soil compaction and structural damage due to:
* overtillage, using heavy machinery;
* tillage in improper moisture conditions; and
* unfavorable changes of organic matter regime (lack of soil structure forming and
stabilizing agents).
Decrease of humus content and humus quality due to
*  improper recycling of organic residues;
*  lack of organic fertilizers; and
*  soil acidification (leaching, mineral and liquid fertilizers, acidic depositions, air
pollution), and biological degradation (undesirable changes in microbial populations:
total number, species, activity).
Soil and water pollution due to:
*  absorbed and insoluble P compounds transported by surface runoff directly to surface
water result in increasing eutrophication and unfavorable changes in the aquatic
ecosystems;
*  nitrate pollution of subsurface water caused by liquid manure application and
irrational or improper N fertilizer application not adequately selected for the crop
requirement (nutrient uptake), soil properties, and weather conditions, time of
application, etc.; and
*  pesticides and other organic chemicals pollution;
40.        Soil erosion (strong surface runoff, lack of permanent cover of crops, steep slopes, heavy
rainfall but limited infiltration), implies considerable loss of organic matter and plant nutrients, silting
of waterways, canals, and reservoirs, and increases the cost of more frequent cleaning and increasing
hazards of water logging and floods in the lower parts of the watershed.
Private Sector
41.        The practice of integrated, extensive production and greater coverage of livestock is the
reason why soil conditions are much better among private farms. Nevertheless, on some soils of private
households, the process of intensive erosion had been registered, particularly in the northwestern
Pannonian subregion, as well as in the complete Mediterranean area. Besides natural factors, one reason
for this situation is the unfavorable orientation of plots and plowing up and down the slope. In addition,
erosion is aggravated by the mix of main crops, especially when the rotation includes maize.
42.        Deposition of herbicides in these zones accumulates in the mountain base, leaching into the
water flows, which in turn causes water pollution. These pesticide residues lead to water eutrophication
among the water flows. Water pollution by nitrates also appears on sites where large quantities of poultry
manure are used on the acid, drained, light, gravely soils of northwestern Croatia.



ANNEX F                                          - 162 -                       Project Implementation Plan
III. POTENTIAL IMPACT OF THE PROJECT ON ENVIRONMENT
A. STARTING POINT
43.       The Farmer Support Services Project is conceived to involve agricultural research,
agricultural extension, animal health services, fodder and pasture development, seed industry development
(through institutional and regulatory development), and strengthen MAF's policy formulation capacities.
Since the Croatian private sector has historically been an avid supporter of ecologically sustainable
agricultural production, progress in these fields will strengthen the future development of sustainable
agriculture.
44.       No negative environmental impacts are expected in the course of FSSP implementation. It
is realistic to expect that the effects of some project components will strengthen the notion of sustainable
agriculture which combines technologies, policies and activities aimed at integrating socioeconomic
principles with environmental concerns so as to simultaneously: maintain or enhance production,
minimize risk of diminished food production, protect natural resources and prevent degradation of soil
and water quality, all with a view to being economically viable and socially acceptable.
45.       This EA does not propose a specific mitigation plan (nor does it cost mitigation efforts)
because no direct negative environmental impacts are anticipated in the context of implementing this
project. However, as a crucial mitigation activity during the first year of project implementation, MAF
would develop special ('best practice') guidelines for its extension service and the farming community
in support of the concept and practices of sustainable agriculture and appropriate handling of agriculture
chemicals.
46.       A major contribution to the development of these best practice guidelines can be expected
from the findings of the on-going project on "Proper Handling of Pesticides and their Residues in
Agriculture Practice" managed by the Agency for Special and Dangerous Waste.
47.       In addition, MAF will organize an  "International Workshop on Water Pollution and
Protection in Agriculture Practices" in Zagreb, in October, 1995. This workshop will be organized in the
framework of the new international project on "Pollution of Ground and Surface Water by Nitrates,
Heavy Metals and Pesticides through Percolation from Agriculture Areas" which has been suggested by
Croatia and accepted by the Third Session of the FAO/ECE Working Party on Relations between
Agriculture and Environment, held in Geneva in 1994.
B. EXPECTED TRENDS
48.       It cannot be forgotten that private farmers have historically been economically discriminated
against. Providing new economic power will lead to possible trends toward intensification of agricultural
production. Due to a certain "hunger in agrochemicals application" an unfavorable "polarization"
tendency in agrochemicals application may occur under two possible scenarios:
Scenario A:  Better soil - rich farmer - higner rate of agrochemical (in spite of the possible
lower requirements) - better nutrient status of soil - overdosage
Scenario B:  Poor soil - poor farmer - lower rate of agrochemicals (in spite of the possible
higher requirements) - lower nutrient supply of soils - underdosage



Project Implementation Plan                      - 163 -                                        ANNEX F
49.       Certain new dangers may present themselves as new agricultural land users are motivated to
realize as high a profit as possible in the short term. The short-term market-oriented production of land
users may lead to environmental damager because of the lack of necessary (and sometimes expensive)
preventive measures, especially in cases when harmful side-effects are detectable only one or two years
later or when they appear in the surrounding environment (nitrate pollution, acidification).
50.       Project implementation opens the way to a trend to overdosage under Scenario A (the
"maximum concept" principle), which, if the problem does not get sufficient attention, may cause various
environmental damages:
*  soil acidification;
*  contamination of drinking water resources by nitrate leaching;
*  accumulation of harmful toxic elements in the various stages of the "food chain".
51.       However, project implementation also opens a set of desirable processes and effects, which
will undoubtedly result positively on the environment. In the first place, FSSP finances research work
which seeks to define optimal management practices in agriculture. Secondly, Extension Service activities
are financed to provide accurate and professional information about all aspects of ecologically risky
management practices. In order to achieve this objective MAF would develop "Best Practices" Guidelines
for its extension service on the concept and practice of sustainable agriculture and on the handling of
agriculture chemicals.
52.       In addition, privatization will increase ownership feeling and more care for maintaining long-
run soil fertility and sustaining the environment. A private household, especially a family farm, allows
for much more flexibility in sustainable agriculture. Market forces and EU integration will orient
production towards quality standards and environmental aspects in general; such a concept will be a
guiding principle in the professional advice provided by the Extension Service.
53.       The FSSP is to a large extent focused on institutional strengthening and development.
Individual components are anticipated to have a beneficial impact on the environment as follows:
(i)  the  Agriculture  Extension  Component would  enhance  the  dissemination  of
environmentally relevant knowledge through the special guidelines developed by MAF
from international and national research;
(ii)  the Applied Agriculture Research Component would allow the investigation of pressing
environmental issues related to agriculture;
(iii)  the Animal Health Component would ensure a better control of contagious and zoonotic
diseases;
(iv)  the Pasture and Fodder Development Component would contribute to better erosion
control, landscape management (reduction of maize monoculture), and more animal-
friendly husbandry practices;
(v)  the Seed Industry Development Component would by improving the regulatory
framework ensure better and more rapid access to new, disease and pest resistant plant
genetic materials already available abroad which would enhance low input practices in
crop management; through the establishment of the National Seed Authority agrochemical



ANNEX F                                          - 164 -                       Project Implementation Plan
testing and registration as well as the development of integrated pest management (IPM)
practices would be integrated into one institution thus ensuring efficient development of
regulations and procedures.
(vi)  the Policy Analysis Support Component would increase MAF's capability to follow actual
developments related to environmental issues in the agricultural sector and concerns of
decision makers in view of including these issues into appropriate legislation.
C. LAND MANAGEMENT FOR SUSTAINABLE AGRICULTURE
54.       The development of Croatian agriculture to increase food and agricultural production while
meeting unsatisfied demand and recognizing the need to use environmentally friendly technologies will
carry forward into the 21st century. The main "pillars" and principles of Sustainable Land Development
(SLM)-Productivity, Security, Protection, Viability and Acceptability-(according to a Discussion
Paper of the Land and Water Development Division of FAO) are fully compatible with Croatian
conditions. It is clear that sustainable land management needs to be in the center of environmentally
sound sustainable agriculture.
55.       Implementation of FSSP will open the way to scientifically-based crop production
technologies, in particular with regard to: adequate crop rotation, integral plant nutrition systems
including plant residue management and integrated pest management system. In other words, FSSP by
our judgment opens the way to knowledge-based instead of high input-based agriculture.
56.       Experts of the extension service will need to keep in mind that quality of surface and
subsurface water resources and protection of the environment are required for the long-term sustainability
of agriculture. We consider that ideas expressed through the FSSP would support this concept. Criteria
of sustainable agricultural development and crop production would need to be defined and quantified in
the context of the aforementioned Extension Guidelines.
IV. SUMMARY STATUS OF ENVIRONMENTAL LEGISLATION
57.       Environmental legislation in the Republic of Croatia is not yet formed as a uniform and
systematic body of laws. However, since individual parts of this legislation are covered by laws on water
protection, agricultural land protection, forest protection etc., and these parts have a long tradition and
have been systematically developed, environmental legislation could be considered complete as a
combination of theses laws and on a regulatory level. Some parts of environment legislation are
developed on a sectoral basis (water-management, agriculture, forestry etc.).
58.       Generally, Croatian environmental legislation is characterized by the following features:
*  a variable degree of development of some parts of the legislation through a number
of bodies directly responsible for development, monitoring and implementation, and
inefficiency in enforcement and control;
*  the general character of legislation is mainly guiding, partly resulting in inefficient
implementation, and less of a restricting and obligatory nature; and



Project Implementation Plan                       - 165 -                                          ANNEX F
a strong legal activity related to changing the present and setting new regulations
based on constitutional prescriptions, (i.e., a need to harmonize with new social and
economic orientation of the Republic of Croatia), which means introducing modern
legislation solutions.
59.       The approach practiced in solving environmental questions is based on a broader, integrated
approach characterized by a long positive tradition of multidisciplinary work and intersectoral co-
operation, which is based not on regulations, but rather on experience. It has to be noted that, in former
Yugoslavia, issues of environment protection had been solved at the republic level, so that in the Republic
of Croatia there was continuity in improving the activities of environment protection, particularly since
the beginning of 1970s, and environment legislation had involved regulations set in the period from 1949
to present day.
60.       It can be expected that projects such as FSSP will positively influence the process of
completing and strengthening environmental legislation.
V. RELEVANT LEGISLATION FOR FSSP IMPLEMENTATION
61.       Central elements of environmental protection are covered by the Constitution of the Republic
of Croatia from 1990 which stipulates that: (i) the environment is under special protection by the State;
(ii) citizens right to a healthy life and a healthy environment are guaranteed; and (iii) citizens' right to
local self-government involves also the right to make decisions on environment protection and
improvement.
62.       The Declaration on Environment Protection in the Republic of Croatia (MN 34/92) is oriented
toward "an agriculture based on economically sustainable development...., and imposes:
"...rational soil management as an important measure, with the purpose of food
production; to be achieved by applying strong protection, by prohibiting the
reallocation of highly productive soils to non-agricultural use, especially building
purposes; by improving the lower categories of soil; by encouraging agricultural
production on fallow areas and by strictly controlling pesticide use as well as
other substances for plan protection, respectively..."
63.       Law on Environment Protection (MN982/94) prescribes a common starting point for
systematic actions in the field of environmental protection, and represent legislative starting point from
which legal solutions of particular environmental issues arise. This law consists of basic principles of
environment protection, determines creation of documents, the ways and means of implementing
environmental protection (standards, environmental impact assessment, monitoring, pollution cadastre,
information system, etc.), responsibility, control and penalties. As such, practical issues are solved
mainly by other laws and regulations.
64.       Law on Trade of Poisons (NN 53/91, 26/93, 37/94), determines a list of prohibited and
permitted poisons, classification of poisons in six classes (I to VI) and procedures for the trade of these
poisons. A new law is currently in the legislative process and is expected to be passed by Parliament
soon. The new law proposal has been developed in accordance with the WHO standards, details of which
will be further governed by new, additional regulations.



ANNEX F                                           - 166 -                         Project Implementation Plan
65.       Law on Production. Trade and Use of Poison (NN 20/83, 47/89, 26/93, 37/94) regulates the
production of poisons and provides detailed regulations for the trade and use of poisons which are
permitted by the Law on Trade of Poisons. According to this law, the production and use of poison may
be prohibited in spite of its proper application, if it is found that it endangers the health and life of people
or damages the environment. Each plant pesticide is required to pass a prescribed procedure to obtain
permission for trade and use which is based on a number of tests as prescribed in the regulations.
66.       Law on Water (MN 53/90, 9.91, 61.91, 43/93, 75/93. 75/94), amongst other, regulates issues
of water supply, preventive water protection from pollution, and measures for decreasing water
contamination, including designing water-management plans, issuing of water-management permits,
control and penalties.
67.       Law on Spatial Planning (MN 30.94). Spatial planning arrangements have a long tradition
in the Republic of Croatia. Total space of the state - its purpose, use and conditions for protection are
determined by the Spatial Plan of the Republic of Croatia (MN 12/89) and spatial plans of municipalities.
Spatial plans are created as coordinating documents involving all basic elements of sectoral development
- for example, water-management plans, forest-management plans, traffic plans etc. All operations in
space are carried out exclusively on the basis of such spatial plans.
68.       Law on Agricultural Land (MN 54/94,  65/94).  This law prescribes basic terms of
protection, usage and reallocation of agricultural land, use over state-owned agricultural land, and control
and penalties.
69.       Law on Plant Protection (MN 10/94. 19/94) regulates in detail the protection of plants and
plant products from pests, plant health control, transport of pesticides and prevention of negative
consequence of their use on humans, animals and environment, including control and penalties.
70.       Law on livestock production improvement measures (MN 11/79, 26/93) and Law on animal
health protection and veterinary activities (MN 52/91, 26/93) determine a general framework for the
improvement of livestock production and for systematic animal health protection, (i.e., veterinary
activities).
71.       Inspection control. The public inspection activities are prescribed by a broad range of laws.
Part of the control and monitoring of FSSP implementation is carried out by environmental protection
inspection, agricultural inspection, water management inspection, veterinary inspection, plant-protection
inspection, town-planning inspection, construction inspection, and sanitary inspection.
72.       The Republic of Croatia is a signatory member to a number of international conventions on
the protection of the environment such as (i) Protocol on the Mediterranean Protection from Continental
Pollution (Barcelona Convention Protocol) regarding the Adriatic part of Croatia; (ii) Convention on the
Cooperation on the Protection and Sustainable Use of the River Danube; and (iii) Convention on
Biological Diversity Protection, which have legal precedence over national law.
73.       Regulation on the study of Environmental Impact Assessment (MN 31/84). Regulations
require an EIA for the food-processing industry, livestock and poultry farrms capacities over 500 heads,
slaughter-houses capacities over 50 head and tanneries.
74.       Law on Nature Protection (MN 30/94, 72/94) prescribes procedures for obtaining licenses
for construction works and other works on non-construction locations.



Project Implementation Plan                         - 167 -                                          ANNEX F
75.        Regulations based on the Law on Water govern: (i) dangerous and harmful substances which
cannot be flowed or get into the water, or deposited on areas where the possibility of water pollution
exists, except under prescribed terms; water classification and categorization; terms to determine the
zones of sanitary source protection and protective measures - (Regulation on water categories [MN
15/81]); (ii) Regulations on maximal permitted concentration of danger substances in waters and coastal
see (2/84), Regulation on protective measures on terms for determining the sanitary protection variation
of fresh water sources (MN 22/86), Plan for protection of water from pollution (MN 22/86), Instruction
on record keeping on frequency of emissions of dangerous and harmful substances, their quantities and
composition, to the waters (MN 22/86).
76.        Regulations on agricultural land protection for contamination with harmful substances (MN
15/92) regulate the content of ecologically risky substances allowed, and determines how many and when
the manure can be used. The maximum tolerated contaminant content is determined by the Rule Book
on Agricultural Soil Conservation.
77.        Legislation regarding management of chemicals involves chemicals for plan protection (I I
regulations), mineral fertilizers (4 regulations) and veterinary drugs (4 regulations). These regulations
govern the protection from harmful impacts on human environment, classifications and registration,
information requirements and transport. Regulations of major importance are:
*  Law on quality, quality control an distribution of mineral fertilizers (MN 43/93)
* Regulations on Fertilizer Register and Fertilizer Declaring (MN 43/92)
*  Regulations on minimal and maximal substances and other characteristics of
particular sorts of mineral fertilizers (MN 43/92)
*  Set of sub-legal provisions regulates food protection issues - from animal control and
control of animal products, to the quality of certain kinds of food products.
78.        A modern environmental monitoring system is currently being developed encompassing all
the existing data bases, information and related services. It is a long-term project developed by the State
Directorate of Environment in cooperation with many other ministries and services envisaged to be
developed in accordance with international monitoring networks. The concept of this project is to obtain
an integrated and decentralized monitoring system. Presently, data on air, soil and water are made
available by numerous different administrative bodies, services and institutions.
79.        From  the above summary of the existing laws and regulations, the following could be
concluded with regard to the implementation of FSSP:
*  existing provisions have more of a guiding than a restrictive role;
*  the restrictions are mostly related to land use and development of a particular area,
and to some extent to the application of chemicals;
80.        Estimates of air, water and soil contamination for larger urban centers (Zagreb, Rijeka, Sisak,
Kutina)' and estimates of harmful substances emission from agriculture are carried out and generally
1/   Controlling and information cadastre system of polluters (NISO) by State Directorate of Environment.



ANNEX F                                           - 168 -                         Project Implementation Plan
analyzed based on produced quantities of fertilizers, consumption of fuel and lubricant for agricultural
machines, emission of harmful substances from fertilizer plants and share in liquid waste. Continuous
monitoring of harmful substances emission into the environment and of impacts caused by agricultural
production at the national level in Croatia still does not exist. According to the soil monitoring, created
within the working group for soil protection in Alps-Adria and Danube basin countries, the permanent
"monitoring" of soil, will be carried out in Croatia with the purpose of monitoring the essential soil
characteristics, particularly natural contents, anthropogene influence and ecological risky substances
balance. Soil monitoring and analysis will be carried out using standardized methods in all member-
states, which provides coordination in soil protection measures.
81.       The general environmental situation in Croatia is deemed to be satisfactory.  Some
environmental contamination, in some cases above European and World standard limits, are registered
in highly industrialized and urban areas (Zagreb, Rijeka, Sisak, Kutina). The general share of agriculture
in total emission of harmful substances is highly variable, but significantly below the level of industrial
emissions. For example, the share of exhausted nitrogen (NO) from agriculture is estimated at 15-25 %,
while share of industrial deposition is about 50%. The share of phosphorus deposition (P205) is about
10%, which is significantly less than industrial influence (60-70%). The major part of agricultural
pollution by nitrate and phosphorus related to water pollution. Estimates of Sok emission, based on total
consumption of fuel and contents of sulfur in some kinds of fuel, shows that agricultural machinery
utilization accounts for about 5 % of the total air pollution in Croatia.
82.       The relatively low levels of NO, SO,, and P04 emissions from agriculture so far are the result
of ownership structure and the economic positions of Croatian agriculture producers. Namely, the greater
part of agricultural and arable land, agricultural mechanization, as well as livestock assets are family
farm-owned, whose consumption of fertilizers and pesticides, and mechanization utilization is in most
cases considerably lower, due to its inferior economic position, limited agricultural acreage, traditional
production organization and (dis)parity of input and output prices. Even the intensive production family
farms have no particular crop or livestock capacities which could, due to their size and location on small
areas, significantly influence the increase of emission of harmful substances into environment.
83.       Currently no manual exists to guide farmers on the application, handling, and storage of
agricultural inputs, and it is recommended to include the development of such a manual within the
extension component of the FSSP.
84.       More attention should be given to the appropriate development of the role of the agriculture
extension services, as well as promotion of NGOs to introduce practical approaches to integrated and
sustainable agriculture. The role and coordination of inspection activities should be strengthened. The
regular control of soil fertility should be enforced to the extent possible.
